Tangible Qualities as the First of the Sensibles
The author explains why tangible qualities are called the first of the sensibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why tangible qualities are called 'the first of the sensibles' and the two reasons given by the author.

Core Idea
Tangible qualities are foundational because touch is universal and elemental bodies always have them.

In this passage, the author classifies sensible qualities and explains why tangible qualities are given the designation 'first of the sensibles.' This is not a temporal priority but a logical priority based on universality and necessity. Let's examine the two reasons he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview. Notice the two reasons given.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find where the author states the first reason (عموم القوة اللامسة) and the second reason (أن لأجسام العنصرية لا تخلو عن الكيفيات الملموسة).
3
Understand the secret behind the second reason
The author explains why the other senses require mediums free of their qualities, but touch does not. Consider how this supports the designation 'first'.
This is the 'السر' in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول : الملموسات ( المسماة بأوائل المحسوسات لوجهين . أحدهما عموم القوة اللامسة إذ لا يخلو عنها حيوان لأن بقاءه باعتدال مزاجه فلا بد له من الاحتراز عن الكيفيات المفسدة إياه فلذلك جعلت هذه القوة منتشرة في أعضائه، وأما سائر المشاعر فليس في هذه المرتبة من الضرورة، فقد يخلو الحيوان عنه كالخراطين الفاقد للمشاعر الأربعة وكالخلد الفاقد لحاسة البصر. والثاني أن لأجسام العنصرية لا تخلو عن الكيفيات الملموسة، وقد تخلو عن سائر المحسوسات والسر فيه؛ أنَّ الإبصار يتوقف على توسط جسم شفاف أي خال عن الألوان، لئلا تشتغل الحاسة به فلا تدرك كيفية المبصر على ما ينبغي، والذوق يتوقف على رطوبة لعابية خالية عن الطعوم، والشم يتوقف على جسم يتكيف بالرائحة أو يختلط بأجزاء من حاملها والسمع يتوقف على ما يحمل الصوت إليه، فلا بد أن يكون في نفسه خالياً عنه، بخلاف اللمس فإنه لا حاجة به إلى متوسط حتى يلزم خلوه عن الملموسات
The first type: the tangible qualities (called the first of the sensibles for two reasons. One: the universality of the sense of touch, for no animal is without it, because its survival depends on the balance of its temperament, so it must guard against corrupting qualities; therefore this power is made widespread in its limbs. As for the other senses, they are not at this level of necessity, for an animal may be without them, like the earthworm lacking the four senses, and like the mole lacking the sense of sight. The second: that elemental bodies are never devoid of tangible qualities, while they may be devoid of other sensibles. The secret behind this is that vision depends on the mediation of a transparent body, i.e., free of colors, lest the sense be occupied by it and thus not perceive the quality of the visible object as it should; taste depends on a salivary moisture free of tastes; smell depends on a body that is affected by the odor or mixes with parts of its carrier; hearing depends on something that carries sound to it, so it must itself be free of it. Unlike touch, for it has no need of a medium such that it would require being free of tangible qualities.)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. The author identifies the first type of sensible qualities as tangible qualities (al-malmūsāt), which are also called 'the first of the sensibles' (awā’il al-maḥsūsāt). He gives two reasons for this designation. The first reason is the universality of the sense of touch: every animal possesses it because survival depends on maintaining a balanced temperament and avoiding harmful qualities; thus the power of touch is distributed throughout the body. Other senses are not as necessary; some animals lack them entirely (e.g., earthworms lack four senses, moles lack sight). The second reason is that elemental bodies (al-ajsām al-‘unṣuriyyah) always possess tangible qualities, but they can be devoid of other sensibles. The author explains the underlying rationale: each of the other four senses requires a medium that is free of the quality it perceives. For sight, the transparent body (like air) must be colorless; for taste, the saliva must be tasteless; for smell, the air must be free of odors or mix with particles; for hearing, the medium must be free of sound. Touch, however, has no need of such a medium, so there is no requirement for the body to be free of tangible qualities. Therefore, tangibles are more fundamental and thus called 'first'.

حيوان
أجسام عنصرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'First of the sensibles' means they are temporally first in perception.
Correction: It means they are fundamental due to universality and necessity, not temporal priority.

Key Takeaway
Tangible qualities are called 'first of the sensibles' because the sense of touch is universal among animals and elemental bodies always possess them, unlike other sensibles that require special mediums.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What 'corrupting qualities' – like pride, envy, or heedlessness – are you failing to guard against because you have not treated your spiritual balance with the same urgency? Today, identify one such quality and commit to acting against it.
?
The other senses require a medium free of what they perceive: for clear vision, the medium must be free of color. If you want to perceive truth clearly, what 'medium' – distractions, desires, or biases – do you need to clear from your heart so that you can see reality as it is? What step will you take this week to create that clarity?
?
The author says elemental bodies always have tangible qualities; they cannot be free of them. Is there a 'tangible quality' in your character – a habit or trait – that you have considered optional but is actually always present? Is it helping or harming the balance of your soul? Confess honestly to yourself what change is needed.

Why Tangible Qualities Are Called Primary Sensibles
Explains why tangible qualities are called 'primary sensibles' with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why tangible qualities are called primary sensibles and how touch's universality in perception and existence establishes its primacy.

Core Idea
Tangible qualities are primary because touch is universal in perception and constant in existence.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains why tangible qualities (al-malmūsāt) are classified as the first type of sensibles. The author gives two compelling reasons rooted in the universality of the sense of touch and the constant presence of tangible qualities in physical bodies. Understanding these reasons clarifies the foundational role of touch in the hierarchy of senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the two reasons the author presents.
Pay attention to the examples of animals lacking other senses.
2
Identify the two reasons
The first reason is the universality of touch: every animal has touch. The second reason is that elemental bodies always have tangible qualities but may lack other qualities.
3
Understand the commentary's summary
The commentary explains that the first reason makes touch 'oldest in perception' and the second makes it 'oldest in existence.'
This highlights the complementary nature of the two reasons.
4
Reflect on the secret behind the second reason
Other senses require a medium free of the quality being perceived, but touch does not, so its medium need not be free of tangibles.
This explains why tangible qualities are always present in bodies.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحواس الخمس الظاهرة النوع الأول: الملموسات المسماة بأوائل المحسوسات لوجهين. أحدهما عموم القوة اللامسة إذ لا يخلو عنها حيوان لأن بقاءه باعتدال مزاجه فلا بد له من الاحتراز عن الكيفيات المفسدة إياه فلذلك جعلت هذه القوة منتشرة في أعضائه، وأما سائر المشاعر فليس في هذه المرتبة من الضرورة، فقد يخلو الحيوان عنه كالخراطين الفاقد للمشاعر الأربعة وكالخلد الفاقد لحاسة البصر. والثاني أن لأجسام العنصرية لا تخلو عن الكيفيات الملموسة، وقد تخلو عن سائر المحسوسات والسر فيه؛ أنَّ الإبصار يتوقف على توسط جسم شفاف أي خال عن الألوان، لئلا تشتغل الحاسة به فلا تدرك كيفية المبصر على ما ينبغي، والذوق يتوقف على رطوبة لعابية خالية عن الطعوم، والشم يتوقف على جسم يتكيف بالرائحة أو يختلط بأجزاء من حاملها والسمع يتوقف على ما يحمل الصوت إليه، فلا بد أن يكون في نفسه خالياً عنه، بخلاف اللمس فإنه لا حاجة به إلى متوسط حتى يلزم خلوه عن الملموسات.
قوله: (لوجهين الخ) حاصل الوجه الأول عمومها من حيث الإدراك فيكون أقدمها إدراكاً وحاصل الوجه الثاني عمومها من حيث الوجود أقدمها وجوداً. قوله: (باعتدال مزاجه) النوعي وأما بقاء الشخص فمنوط به الصحة. قوله: (في أعضائه) أي في ظاهر جميع الأعضاء غير مختصة بعضو معين كسائر الحواس لأنَّ اللمس واجب في كل منها. قوله: (كالخراطين) هو الدود الأحمر الذي يوجد في عمق الأرض ويقال له: معاء الأرض. قوله: (وكالخلد) بضم الخاء المعجمة وسكون اللام كورموش. قوله: (منتشرة في أعضائه إلا ما يكون عدم الحس أنفع له كالكبد والطحال والكلية على ما تقرر في موضعه.
The five external senses, the first type: tangible things (al-malmūsāt) called the primary sensibles (awā'il al-mahsūsāt) for two reasons. One of them is the universality of the sense of touch, for no animal is devoid of it because its survival depends on the balance of its temperament, so it must guard against corrupting qualities; therefore this power is made diffused in its limbs. As for the other senses, they are not at this level of necessity, for an animal may be devoid of them, like earthworms lacking four senses, and the mole lacking sight. The second reason is that elemental bodies are never devoid of tangible qualities, but may be devoid of other sensibles; the secret in that is that vision depends on a transparent body free of colors, lest the sense be occupied by it and not perceive the quality of the visible object properly; taste depends on salivary moisture free of tastes; smell depends on a body that becomes qualified by the odor or mixes with parts of its carrier; hearing depends on something that carries sound to it, so it must in itself be free of it. Unlike touch, it has no need for a medium such that its being free of tangibles would be necessary.
His saying: (for two reasons, etc.): The gist of the first reason is its universality in terms of perception, so it is the oldest in perception; the gist of the second reason is its universality in terms of existence, so it is the oldest in existence. His saying: (due to the balance of its temperament): generic temperament; as for the survival of the individual, it depends on health. His saying: (in its limbs): meaning on the surface of all limbs, not restricted to a specific organ like the other senses, because touch is required in each of them. His saying: (like earthworms): it is the red worm found deep in the ground, called 'intestine of the earth.' His saying: (and like the mole): with damma on the khā' and sukun on the lām, like kūrmūsh. His saying: (diffused in its limbs except where lack of sensation is more beneficial, like the liver, spleen, and kidney, as established in its place.)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies tangible qualities (al-malmūsāt) as the first type of sensible qualities, explaining why they are called 'primary sensibles' (awā'il al-mahsūsāt). It provides two reasons. The first reason is the universality of the sense of touch: every animal possesses it because survival requires maintaining a balanced temperament and avoiding harmful qualities. Unlike other senses, which may be absent in some animals (e.g., earthworms lack four senses, moles lack sight), touch is present in all. The commentary clarifies that 'universality in perception' makes touch the 'oldest in perception,' while 'universality in existence' makes it the 'oldest in existence.' The second reason is that elemental bodies are never devoid of tangible qualities, whereas they may lack other sensible qualities. The secret behind this is that other senses require a medium free of the quality being perceived: vision needs a transparent medium free of colors, taste needs saliva free of tastes, smell needs air free of other odors, hearing needs air free of other sounds. Touch, however, has no such need for a medium, so its medium need not be free of tangibles. The commentary also explains that the sense of touch is diffused over the entire body surface, except where lack of sensation is beneficial (like liver, spleen, kidney).

Key Terms
الملموسات
الحواس الخمس
القوة اللامسة
أوائل المحسوسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two reasons are sequential stages, with the first being more important than the second.
Correction: The two reasons are complementary: one relates to perception (universality of the sense) and the other to existence (constant presence of the quality). Both together justify the title 'primary.'

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you as vigilant in guarding your soul from spiritual corruption as your body from physical harm? What concrete step will you take today to increase that vigilance?
?
Touch needs no medium; it directly perceives. Consider your relationship with truth: Do you often rely on 'media'—opinions, distractions, biases—to know reality, or do you seek direct, unmediated understanding? What would it mean to 'touch' truth directly in your heart?
?
Animals devoid of other senses still have touch. Think about a time you felt spiritually numb or blind. Despite losing some awareness, did you still have an innate sense of right and wrong (like touch)? How can you strengthen that fundamental spiritual sense that never leaves you?

Objection and Answer on the Medium for Touch
Refutes an objection that touch's organ must also be free of tangible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the organ of touch does not need to be free of tangible qualities, unlike other senses, and how empirical observation resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Touch's organ can perceive opposites because direct contact overcomes the need for a pure medium.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the claim that touch requires no medium free of qualities. The objection analogizes touch to other senses, demanding consistency. The answer refutes by appealing to direct experience, showing that touch's organ, unlike external media, can still perceive when itself is qualified. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of the objection and its empirical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the analogy with smell and vision. The objector argues that if other senses require a pure medium, touch should require its organ to be free of tangible qualities.
The principle: the medium must be free of the perceived quality.
2
Identify the key principle
The objection relies on a general principle about perception: the medium or organ should not be occupied by the quality being perceived.
3
Read the answer and note the example
The answer gives an empirical counterexample: a warm hand can still perceive cold. This shows the principle does not hold for touch.
Contrast with sight: a colored medium hinders color perception.
4
Contrast with the other senses
Understand why touch is different: direct contact allows perception of opposites, whereas senses like sight depend on a medium that must be neutral.
Experience confirms this difference.
5
Reflect on the role of experience
The author uses experience as the decisive argument. Why is empirical evidence more convincing than logical analogy here?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل عليه كما أن تكيف المتوسط بالكيفيات المذكورة يمنع الإدراك على ما ينبغي فاقتضت الحكمة خلوه عنها كذلك تكيف المحل أيضاً مانع. كما أن تكيف محل الشم برائحة يمنع إدراك رائحة أخرى فالسر المذكور يقتضي أن يكون محل اللمس أيضاً خالياً الكيفيات الملموسات وإلا فالفرق تحكم. فالجواب أن العقل لا يحكم بوجوب خلو محل اللمس عن الكيفيات الملموسة بأسرها كيف وتكيف اليد بالحرارة لا يمنع إدراك البرودة في الملموس مثلا؟ بخلاف تكيف المتوسط بين الرائي والمرئي بشيء من الألوان مثلا والتجربة شاهدة بذلك.
It was said against it: Just as the qualification of the medium with the mentioned qualities prevents perception as it should, so wisdom required it to be free of them, likewise the qualification of the organ is also an impediment. Just as the qualification of the place of smell with an odor prevents perceiving another odor, so the mentioned secret requires that the place of touch also be free of tangible qualities; otherwise the difference is arbitrary. The answer: The intellect does not judge the necessity of the place of touch being completely free of tangible qualities; how so? The qualification of the hand by heat does not prevent perceiving cold in the tangible object, for example. Unlike the qualification of the medium between the viewer and the viewed with something of colors, for example, and experience bears witness to that.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that touch has no need for a medium free of qualities. The objection argues that just as a medium (like air for sight or hearing) must be free of the perceived quality to allow accurate perception, the organ of touch (the body) itself should also be free of tangible qualities. The example of smell is given: the olfactory organ must not be occupied by another odor. Thus, if touch's medium (the air? actually the organ) is not free, why should other senses require it? The answer refutes this by pointing to experience: the hand, even when hot, can still perceive cold in an object. This shows the organ does not need to be free of all tangible qualities. In contrast, if the medium for vision is colored, it hinders color perception. Experience confirms this difference. The argument uses empirical observation to justify the asymmetry between touch and other senses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the principle requiring the medium to be free of the perceived quality applies to all senses including touch, so the organ must be free of all tangible qualities.
Correction: Touch is an exception because its organ can perceive opposite qualities simultaneously, as shown by experience (a warm hand feeling cold).

Checkpoint

A) Because the intellect cannot judge necessity in such matters
B) Because experience shows that a hand can feel cold even when warm
C) Because touch does not involve a medium
D) Because the organ is not sensitive to its own state

Key Takeaway
The organ of touch does not need to be free of all tangible qualities because direct contact allows perception of opposites, as empirically demonstrated.

The Definition of Heat According to Ibn Sina
Ibn Sina's definition of heat and clarification of cold's effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's functional definition of heat and why cold's effect is not a simple opposite.

Core Idea
Heat separates unlike things and unites like things; cold's effect is not simply the reverse.

This lesson explores Ibn Sina's definition of heat from his work al-Shifa, presented in the Sharh al-Mawaqif. The passage defines heat by its observable effects and clarifies that cold operates differently than one might expect. The commentary addresses a common misunderstanding about cold's function.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall meaning. Focus on Ibn Sina's definition of heat.
Notice that the passage also discusses why cold is not in the title.
2
Identify the definition of heat
Find the exact phrase where Ibn Sina defines heat. What does heat do?
The definition is: 'Heat is that which separates different things and unites similar things.'
3
Understand cold's opposite meaning
The commentary explains that 'opposite' here does not mean cold does the reverse. Instead, cold unites both similar and dissimilar. Consider the examples given: butter and cracks in earth.
This is a key nuance: cold is not simply the inverse of heat.

The third observatory - the first investigation: On heat. (On heat) Just as tangibles were called primary sensibles as you know, similarly the four qualities—heat, its opposite, moisture and dryness—are called primary tangibles because they are established for elemental simples. Compounds result from them through the mediation of temperament derived from these four. The reason cold is not mentioned in the title, although it is mentioned in this investigation, is the disagreement over whether it is existentia. It (heat) has five inquiries. The first: on its reality. Ibn Sina said in al-Shifa: 'Heat is that which separates different things and unites similar things; cold is the opposite, i.e., it unites similar and dissimilar things.' Thus he mentioned in his book. And the explanation of that is that heat has an ascending power, i.e., moving upward, because it produces lightness in its place necessitating that. So when heat affects a body...

His saying: 'Thus he mentioned in his book': i.e., we interpret 'opposite' contrary to the apparent meaning because it is what is mentioned in his book, although some people say that cold unites different things (like in butter) and separates similar things (like cracks in the ground during severe cold).

Commentary
This passage begins the investigation of heat within the study of tangible qualities. It first states that the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) are called 'primary tangibles' because they are inherent in elemental simples, and compounds are produced from them via temperament. It notes that cold is not in the title due to disagreement over whether it is an existing quality. Then it lists five inquiries, beginning with the reality of heat. The definition is from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa: heat separates unlike things and unites like things; cold is the opposite, meaning it unites both like and unlike (as per Ibn Sina's wording). The commentary clarifies that 'opposite' here is not the expected meaning (that cold would separate like and unite unlike), but rather that cold unites both similar and dissimilar, which is the correct interpretation based on Ibn Sina's text. Some also claim cold can separate similar things (e.g., cracking earth) and unite dissimilar (e.g., butter). The definition focuses on heat's functional effect rather than its essence.

Hot in Actuality vs. Potency: The Four Signs
Defines hot in act vs. potency and enumerates four inferential signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between hot in actuality and hot in potency, and the four inferential signs used to identify hot/cold in potency.

Core Idea
What you cannot sense directly may still be real — latent heat is inferred through signs.

Before we examine physical qualities like heat and cold, we must distinguish between what is sensed directly and what is latent. The author first clarifies this distinction, then provides four methods — called 'analogical reasoning' — to detect hidden qualities. This passage is foundational for understanding how medieval Islamic science inferred unobservable properties.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the distinction
Understand that 'hot in actuality' is sensed immediately (like fire), while 'hot in potency' is latent, sensed only after contact (like drugs). The same applies to cold.
This classification is reminiscent of Aristotle's potentiality and actuality.
2
Learn the two methods
Direct experience (trial) is the first method; analogical reasoning (qiyas) via four signs is the second. The signs are color, taste, smell, and speed of reaction.
The first method is straightforward; the second requires careful inference.
3
Examine each sign's strength
Color is the weakest sign. Speed of reaction, especially when consistency is equal and strong, is a stronger indicator of latent quality.
Weak consistency can mislead: speed may be due to weakness, not an assisting quality.

(Second topic) i.e., the second investigation of heat (as it is said: the hot that is not sensed i.e., perceived) its heat actually (like fire for example. (It is said for what its heat is not actually sensed) but (it is sensed after contact with the animal body (and being affected by it) i.e., the body's being affected by the thing (like hot drugs) and foods (and it is called) such (hot in potency. Similarly, cold is applied to cold in actuality and cold in potency. All that is in the manner of the indication of the colors of bodies on the states of their temperaments as detailed in medical books. As for the weaker in consistency, its speed of reaction does not indicate an aiding quality because it is possible that its speed of reaction is due to its weak consistency.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the terms 'hot in actuality' and 'hot in potency'. It first gives examples: fire is hot in actuality because its heat is sensed; drugs with latent heat are hot in potency, sensed after contact with the body. The same distinction applies to cold. Then it presents two methods to identify hot and cold in potency: direct experience, and inferential reasoning (analogy) via four signs. The first sign is color: whiteness indicates cold, redness heat, dullness severe cold, yellowness excessive heat; but this is the weakest indicator. The second is taste (to be discussed later). The third is smell: sharp smells indicate heat, gentle smells cold. The fourth is speed of reaction when consistency is equal: a faster reaction to heat or cold suggests an internal quality assisting the external agent. If the substance is stronger in consistency, a faster reaction is even more indicative; but if it is weaker, speed may be due to weakness, not an assisting quality.

Innate Heat and Resolving an Apparent Contradiction
The author mentions innate heat and resolves a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction that heat imparts both softness and thickness depending on the recipients.

Core Idea
Heat's effect depends on the recipient, not a single property.

This passage addresses a philosophical puzzle: earlier it was said heat softens consistency, but here we consider innate heat which might cause thickness. The commentator shows there is no contradiction because heat produces different effects in different materials. This teaches us that theological and physical principles must account for context and receptivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Note the structure: a statement about innate heat, then a challenge, then a resolution.
The Arabic is truncated after 'الحرارة ما' but the marginal note completes the thought.
2
Identify the apparent contradiction
Find the two claims: earlier heat imparts softness, but here heat might be causing thickness. Why would that be contradictory?
The contradiction assumes heat has a single effect.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator says heat imparts both softness and thickness 'according to the recipients' (بحسب القوابل). This means the effect varies with the material.
This is a key principle in kalam: effects depend on receptivity.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this principle applies to other theological discussions: the same divine attribute can produce different effects in different beings.

Fire's Effect on Eggs as Example of Heat's Action
Al-Iji presents an example of fire's effect on eggs to illustrate heat's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of fire heating eggs illustrates heat's specific effect on consistency, distinguishing between thickening and joining.

Core Idea
Heat can thicken, not just thin—eggs show heat's coagulating power.

In discussing the nature of heat, Al-Iji provides a concrete example from everyday experience: the effect of fire on eggs. This example is not trivial; it demonstrates how heat can alter the internal consistency of a substance, rather than merely causing expansion or movement. Let's examine the passage to understand what this example teaches about heat's action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The passage says fire causes a change in consistency, specifically thickening (غلظ) of the yolk and white. It does not cause joining because they were already joined.
This distinguishes between two possible actions of heat: altering internal consistency vs. combining separate parts.
3

Some manuscripts add that fire will separate the egg by distillation. This shows heat can also separate substances, depending on the context.
This alternative highlights the nuanced understanding of heat's effects in kalam.

Hot and Cold: Actual, Potential, and How to Know Them
Al-Iji defines hot and cold in actuality and potentiality and lists two methods of knowing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between hot and cold in actuality and potentiality, and the two methods (experiment and analogy) used to identify potential hot/cold substances, including the four aspects of analogy.

Core Idea
Heat and cold can be actual or potential; we infer potential qualities through experiment and analogy.

This passage from al-Iji defines two senses of hot and cold and then explains how we can know them in potential substances. It is a practical classification that connects theoretical physics to empirical methods. We will explore the definitions and the evidentiary framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the passage to grasp the difference between actual and potential hot/cold. Actual is sensed directly (e.g., fire), potential requires contact and effect (e.g., drugs).
Note that 'potential' here means the quality is present but not manifest until interaction.
2
Learn the two methods
Identify experiment and analogy as the two ways to know potential hot/cold. Experiment is direct testing; analogy is inference from signs.
3
Study the four aspects of analogy
Examine each aspect: color, taste, smell, speed of reaction. Understand the indicators given (e.g., whiteness for cold, redness for heat).
Color is noted as the weakest.
4
Analyze the speed of reaction nuance
Focus on the condition of equal consistency and agent. Speed indicates an aiding quality only when bodies are equal in consistency; otherwise, speed may be due to weakness.
This is a critical refinement.

Source Text

Second [topic] of the heat discussions: just as it is said: 'hot' is [something] whose heat is not sensed actually, like fire; it is said for that whose heat is not sensed actually but is sensed after contact with the animal body and being affected by it, like hot drugs and foods. Such is called hot in potentiality. Similarly, cold is used for cold in actuality and cold in potentiality. They have two ways of knowing hot and cold in potentiality: first, experiment, which is obvious; second, analogy, which is inference from four aspects: by color (whiteness indicates cold, redness indicates heat, dullness indicates intense cold, yellowness indicates extreme heat, all according to the indication of body colors on their temperaments, as detailed in medical books; and this is the weakest of them), and by taste (will come later in tastes), and by smell (sharp smells indicate heat, soft ones indicate cold), and by speed of reaction with equal consistency and same agent: if two bodies are equal in consistency and one reacts faster to heat or cold, that indicates that the faster has a quality that aids the external agent in effect; or with its strength: if the stronger in consistency reacts faster, that is more indicative of an aiding quality. As for the weaker in consistency, its speed of reaction is not indicative of an aiding quality because it could be due to its weak consistency.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'hot' and 'cold' in two senses: actual and potential. It then discusses how to identify potentially hot or cold substances. Two methods: experiment (obvious) and analogy (inference). Analogy itself has four lines of evidence: color, taste, smell, and speed of reaction. Color is weakest. The author explains each briefly, emphasizing that speed of reaction must consider consistency and the agent. This is a classification of methods, not a full proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potential heat is always sensed immediately upon contact.
Correction: The text states that potential heat requires contact and effect (being affected by it), so it is not always sensed immediately; it depends on the interaction.

Checkpoint

A) Experiment
B) Analogy by color

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at others, how often do you rely on outward signs (like color, dress, or reputation) to judge their inner state? This lesson calls color the weakest analogy. What would it mean for you to replace that habit with actual 'experiment'—direct contact and genuine relationship—to truly know someone's character or faith?
?
The text distinguishes between potential and actual heat. Your iman (faith) may be 'by potential'—present but not yet actualized in action. How do you know whether your faith is merely potential or actual? Are you relying on mere analogy (past experiences, hearsay) or on direct experiment (tasting the sweetness of worship)? Which method gives you certainty?
?
Your speed of reaction to temptation might reveal an internal quality—or a weakness. Think of a recent moment when you fell quickly into sin. Was it because of a weak consistency (lack of spiritual training) or an actual quality aiding the temptation? How can you ensure that your swift reaction to good is from an inner aiding quality, not just impulse?

The Three Types of Heat Are Essentially Different
Al-Iji argues that three types of heat differ in essence due to distinct necessary effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical kalam philosophers argued that innate heat, celestial heat, and fire heat are different in essence based on their distinct necessary effects.

Core Idea
Different effects prove different essences.

In this passage, al-Iji presents a philosophical proof that three kinds of heat—innate, celestial, and fire—are not merely different in degree but in their very essence. He uses observable effects as evidence. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the central claim: the three heats differ in essence because their effects differ.
Notice the phrase 'لاختلاف آثارها اللازمة' (because of the difference of their necessary effects).
2
Identify the two examples
The text gives two examples: 1) Sun's heat harms the night-blind's eye in a way fire heat does not. 2) Innate heat resists fire heat and even hot poisons.
These examples illustrate how different effects must correspond to different essences.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The argument relies on the principle that if two things have different necessary effects, then their essences must be different. This is a key tool in kalam reasoning.
Necessary effects are inseparable from the essence.

مقاومة ومدافعة (للحرارة النارية) التي لا تلائم الحياة، فإنَّ الحرارة الغريبة إذا حاولت إبطال اعتدال المزاج الحيواني قاومها الحرارة الغريزية أشد مقاومة، حتى السموم الحارة لا يدفعها إلا الحرارة الغريزية (فيفعل إلخ) ما ذكره يدل على مغايرة الحرارة الكوكبية النارية ومغايرة الغريزية للنارية ولا يدل على مغايرة الكوكبية للغريزية ووجهه أن الكوكبية إذا قويت وأفرطت أوهنت القوى وأفسدت أفعال البدن بخلاف بخار حاصل بسبب ما يكدر نور الباصرة ليلاً وبالنهار يذوب بسبب حرارة الشمس فيبصر نهاراً وسبب الجهر ضد ذلك فالأعشى لا يضره حرارة الشمس بل تنفعه وتضر بالأجهر ويمكن أن يوجه بأنَّ حرارة الشمس مسخرة فتكون سبباً بعيداً للإضرار . قوله : ( لا يدفعها إلخ) فإن كانت القوة لا تنفعل عن السم الوارد أصلاً فلا يتأثر البدن عنه قوله : (لاختلاف (آثارها يحتمل أن تكون تلك الآثار آثاراً لوجود وناشئة من التشخصات المعينة. وإن كان لا يخلو عن بعد لتحققها في جميع أشخاص النوع، ولهذا قال الأشبه ولم يجزم باختلاف الماهية . قوله : ( فيفعل حر الشمس في عين الأعشى) فإن قلت : الأعشى هو الذي يبصر بالنهار ولا يبصر بالليل كذلك. قوله : ( فإن الحرارة الغريبة (الخ لا حاجة إلى تخصيصها بالحرارة النارية ليكون الدليل وارداً على الدعوى لأن دخول الحرارة النارية فيها كاف في الورود المذكور.
Third [topic]: The more likely correct [view] is that the innate heat present in animal bodies, and the celestial heat radiating from luminous celestial bodies, and the fire heat are types differing in essence because of the difference of their necessary effects indicating the difference of their essences in reality. For the heat of the sun causes harm to the eye of the night-blind (a'sha) that the heat of fire does not; so they must differ in essence. And the innate heat, harmonious with life, is the most resistant and defensive against the fire heat, which is not harmonious with life. For when external heat tries to corrupt the balance of the animal temperament, the innate heat resists it with utmost resistance, even hot poisons are only repelled by innate heat, for it is the tool of nature to repel incoming heat harm. Therefore it is attributed to it as the steward of the body. Aristotle said: this heat is acquired by the composite through emanation upon it just as the soul and powers emanate, according to what the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] narrated from him in al-Shifa'. [Commentary:] His statement (فيفعل etc.): What he mentioned indicates the difference of celestial and fire heat and the difference of innate from fire heat, but does not indicate the difference of celestial from innate. The reason is that when celestial heat becomes strong and excessive, it weakens the powers and corrupts the body's actions, unlike innate heat, which whenever it intensifies, as in youth, increases the quality of natural actions. His statement (في عين الأعشى etc.): The term 'a'sha is used in place of 'ajhar, because the a'sha is one who sees by day and not by night, while the 'ajhar is the opposite. The cause of night-blindness is vapor that clouds the light of vision at night, and by day it dissolves due to heat of the sun, so he sees by day; the cause of 'jahar is the opposite. So the a'sha is not harmed by the sun's heat but rather benefits, and it harms the 'ajhar. And it can be explained that the sun's heat is subservient and thus becomes a remote cause of harm. His statement (لا يدفعها etc.): If the power is not affected by the incoming poison at all, then the body is not affected by it. His statement (لاختلاف آثارها): It is possible that those effects are effects of existence arising from specific individuations, though this is not far-fetched because they are realized in all individuals of the species; therefore he said 'the more likely' and did not assert the difference in essence. His statement (فيفعل حر الشمس في عين الأعشى): If you say: The a'sha is one who sees by day and not by night, and it is reasonable that the sun's heat is beneficial to his eye, not harmful as the commentator mentioned; I say: Rather what he mentioned is reasonable because the sun's heat affects him gradually so that when evening comes he sees nothing, and when night enters the harm recedes little by little until morning when he sees. And there remains a discussion: It is possible that the affecting agent in the eye of the a'sha is light itself, not heat. So the better approach is to say: In explaining the difference of effects, the sun's heat blackens the face of the launderer and whitens cloth, while fire heat does not. His statement (فإن الحرارة الغريبة الخ): There is no need to specify it as fire heat for the proof to apply to the claim, because the inclusion of fire heat in it suffices for the mentioned application.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a proof and discussion. The main text argues that innate, celestial, and fire heat are essentially different because their necessary effects differ. It gives two examples: 1) The sun's heat harms the eye of the night-blind (a'sha) in a way fire heat does not. 2) Innate heat resists fire heat, even hot poisons. The commentator first notes that the proof directly shows difference between celestial/fire and innate/fire, but not between celestial/innate; he explains the difference: celestial heat weakens powers when excessive, while innate heat strengthens them. Then he clarifies the term 'a'sha versus 'ajhar, and addresses an objection about the sun's effect on the a'sha's eye (whether it harms or benefits). He provides an explanation that it harms gradually. He also raises a further point: the effect might be due to light, not heat, and suggests a more solid example: the sun's heat blackens the launderer's face and whitens cloth, unlike fire heat. Finally, he notes that the argument does not need to specify fire heat; including it is sufficient.

Correction: The passage argues that differences in necessary effects—like how sun heat harms the night-blind but fire heat does not—prove that the essences of these heats are fundamentally different, not just a matter of intensity.

Types of Heat and Their Essential Differences
Classifies three types of heat and proves their essential differences by effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three types of heat (celestial, fire, innate) differ in essence (māhiyya) as proven by their distinct necessary effects.

Core Idea
Heat is not all the same—its essence is revealed by its effects.

The author begins by classifying three kinds of heat and immediately presents a proof that they are essentially different. The key evidence lies in their effects: the sun’s heat harms the night-blind eye in a way fire heat does not, and innate heat resists foreign heat like fire. This lesson unpacks that logical argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three types of heat
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three named types: celestial (kawkabiyya), fire (nāriyya), and innate (ghariziyya).
Each type is defined by its source or relation to life.
2
Understand the proof from differing effects
The author argues that different effects indicate different essences. Focus on the example of the sun’s heat vs. fire heat on the eye of the night-blind person (aʿshā).
The effect is a necessary property (lāzima) that reveals the substrate.
3
Examine the example of the night-blind person
The aʿshā sees by day but not at night. The sun’s heat harms him gradually, while fire heat does not. This is the core case for distinguishing celestial and fire heat.
The commentary explains the terms aʿshā and ajhar and considers alternative explanations like light vs. heat.
4
Consider the innate heat’s resistance
Innate heat, being life-compatible, fiercely resists foreign heat (e.g., fire heat). This shows it is essentially different from fire heat.
The resistance includes repelling hot poisons and even cold, by moving the spirit.
5
Reflect on the reasoning for kalam
This passage demonstrates how physical differences can establish metaphysical distinctions, a method used in kalām to argue for the contingency of the world and the unity of the Creator.
Observable effects are signs (āyāt) pointing to unseen realities.

إلخ لفظ الأعشى وقع موقع الأجهر، لأن الأعشى هو الذي يبصر نهاراً؛ ولا يبصر ليلاً والأجهر بالعكس، وسبب العشي بخار حاصل بسبب ما يكدر نور الباصرة ليلاً وبالنهار يذوب بسبب حرارة الشمس فيبصر نهاراً وسبب الجهر ضد ذلك فالأعشى لا يضره حرارة الشمس بل تنفعه وتضر بالأجهر ويمكن أن يوجه بأنَّ حرارة الشمس مسخرة فتكون سبباً بعيداً للإضرار . قوله : ( لا يدفعها إلخ) فإن كانت القوة لا تنفعل عن السم الوارد أصلاً فلا يتأثر البدن عنه قوله : (لاختلاف (آثارها يحتمل أن تكون تلك الآثار آثاراً لوجود وناشئة من التشخصات المعينة. وإن كان لا يخلو عن بعد لتحققها في جميع أشخاص النوع، ولهذا قال الأشبه ولم يجزم باختلاف الماهية . قوله : ( فيفعل حر الشمس في عين الأعشى) فإن قلت : الأعشى هو الذي يبصر بالنهار ولا يبصر بالليل والمعقول كون حرارة الشمس نافعة لعينه لا مضرة كما ذكره الشارح قلت : بل المعقول ما ذكره لأن حرارة الشمس تؤثر فيه تأثيراً متدرجاً حتى إذا أمسى لا يبصر شيئاً، وإذا دخل في الليل يندفع الضرر شيئاً فشيئاً حتى إذا أصبح أبصر، وهكذا بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أنه يحتمل أن يكون المؤثر في عين الأعشى نفس الضوء لاحرارتها فالأقرب أن يقال: في بيان اختلاف اللوازم حرارة الشمس تسود وجه القصار وتبيض القماش وحرارة النار ليست كذلك. قوله : ( فإن الحرارة الغريبة (الخ لا حاجة إلى تخصيصها بالحرارة النارية ليكون الدليل وارداً على الدعوى لأن دخول الحرارة النارية فيها كاف في الورود المذكور. بتحريك الروح إلى دفعه وتدفع الحررارة أيضاً ضرر البارد الوارد بالمضادة، بخلاف البرودة فإنها لا تنازع البارد بل تقاوم الحار بالمضادة فقط، فالحرارة الغريزية تحمي الرطوبات الغريزية عن أن تستولي عليها الحرارة الغريزية كالحرارة النارية، فهي مخالفة لها في الماهية
The celestial (heat) overflowing from the luminous celestial bodies, and the fire (heat) are types differing in essence due to the difference of their necessary effects which indicate the difference of their substrates in reality. The heat of the sun inflicts on the eye of the night-blind person (aʿshā) of harm what the heat of fire does not, so they must differ in essence. And the innate heat which is compatible with life is the most resistant and defensive thing against the fire heat which is not compatible with life. For the foreign heat, when it attempts to abolish the equilibrium of the animal temperament, the innate heat resists it with the utmost resistance, even the hot poisons are repelled only by innate heat, for it is an instrument of nature by which it repels the harm of incoming heat. Then it was attributed to it as the stewardship of the body. Aristotle said: This heat is acquired by the composite through emanation, just as the soul and faculties emanate, according to what the Master (al-Shaykh) narrated from him in al-Shifā. His saying: ('fayafʿalu' etc.) What he mentioned indicates the difference between celestial heat and fire heat and between innate heat and fire heat, but does not indicate the difference between celestial and innate heat. His explanation: When celestial heat becomes strong and excessive, it weakens the faculties and corrupts the bodily functions, unlike innate heat which, whenever it intensifies as in youths, increases the quality of natural functions. His saying: ('fī ʿayn al-aʿshā' etc.) The term 'aʿshā' is placed in the position of 'ajhar', because the aʿshā is one who sees by day and does not see by night, while the ajhar is the opposite. The cause of night-blindness is a vapor that clouds the light of the eye at night, and by day it dissolves due to the sun's heat so he sees by day; the cause of day-blindness is the opposite. Thus the sun's heat does not harm the aʿshā but benefits him, and harms the ajhar. It can be directed that the sun's heat is subservient, so it becomes a remote cause of harm. His saying: ('lā yadfaʿuhā' etc.) If the power is not affected by the incoming poison at all, then the body is not affected by it. His saying: ('li-ikhtilāf āthārihā' etc.) It is possible that those effects are effects of existence (wujūd) and arise from specific individuations (tashakhkhuṣāt muʿayyana), although this is not far-fetched because they occur in all individuals of the species; therefore he said 'al-ashbah' and did not affirm difference in essence. His saying: ('fayafʿalu ḥarr al-shams fī ʿayn al-aʿshā') If you say: The aʿshā is one who sees by day and does not see by night, and it is reasonable that the sun's heat be beneficial to his eye, not harmful, as the commentator mentioned. I say: Rather the reasonable is what he mentioned, because the sun's heat affects him gradually until when night falls he sees nothing, and when he enters the night the harm is gradually repelled until morning he sees. Thus there remains here a discussion: It is possible that the effective agent in the eye of the aʿshā is the light itself, not its heat. So it is closer to say: In the exposition of the difference of necessary properties, the sun's heat blackens the face of the fuller and whitens cloth, while fire heat does not do so. His saying: ('fa-inna al-ḥarāra al-gharība' etc.) There is no need to specify it as fire heat for the proof to apply to the claim, because the inclusion of fire heat in it is sufficient for the aforementioned application. By moving the spirit to repel it, and it also repels the harm of incoming cold by opposition, unlike coldness which does not contend with the cold but resists heat only by opposition. So innate heat protects the innate moistures from being dominated by innate heat like fire heat, thus it differs from it in essence.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage classifies three types of heat: celestial (kawkabiyya), fire (nariyya), and innate (ghariziyya), and argues that they differ in essence (māhiyya) because their necessary effects differ. The proof is that the sun's heat harms the eye of the night-blind person (aʿshā) in a way that fire heat does not, so they must be essentially different. Similarly, innate heat resists fire heat vigorously, protecting the body's equilibrium. The commentator elaborates on each point: the sun's heat weakens faculties if excessive, while innate heat strengthens them; the example of the aʿshā is explained lexically and medically; and the possibility that light rather than heat causes the effect is considered. The passage concludes by emphasizing that innate heat's resistance to both hot and cold shows it is essentially distinct from fire heat.

حرارة نارية
حرارة غريزية
ماهية
أثر
أعشى
أجهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of heat are essentially the same; only their sources differ.
Correction: The passage shows that heat types differ in essence because they have different necessary effects (e.g., sun heat vs. fire heat on the night-blind eye).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main evidence that celestial heat and fire heat differ in essence?
A) They come from different sources.
B) The sun's heat harms the night-blind person in a way fire heat does not.
C) Innate heat is compatible with life while fire heat is not.
D) Aristotle said they are different.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference among types of heat is established by their distinct necessary effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the nature of a thing is revealed by its effects. Think of a habit, desire, or relationship in your life that 'feels' the same as another but produces harmful effects. Are you confusing something beneficial with something destructive because they seem similar on the surface? What concrete effects will you look for to distinguish them?
?
The innate heat in your body resists foreign heat to preserve life. What within your soul—your faith, your conscience, your sincere intentions—acts as that innate resistance against the 'foreign heat' of worldly distractions and sinful temptations? Are you strengthening that resistance or allowing it to weaken?
?
The passage shows that not all heat is the same; essential differences matter. In your spiritual life, have you assumed that all 'warm feelings' of religiosity are equal? How can you test whether the warmth of your devotion comes from genuine faith (like innate heat) or from something else? What effect would reveal the difference?

Al-Razi's View: Innate and Fire Heat Are One Genus
Al-Razi's argument for the unity of heat genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that innate heat and fire heat are essentially the same, differing only in inclusion in the composite.

Core Idea
The difference between innate and fire heat is not in essence but in context.

Imam al-Razi, in a view later adopted by Galen and physicians, argued that the heat we call 'innate' and the heat of fire are fundamentally the same. He explains that when fire acts on elements in a balanced way, its heat becomes the innate heat. This lesson presents his reasoning and clarifies his concept of 'inclusion in the composite.'

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'when fire mixes appropriately...'.
This passage is a direct quote from Imam al-Razi.
2
Identify the conditional reasoning
Al-Razi describes conditions under which fire heat becomes innate heat. What are the limits (not too much, not too little)? How does this condition define innate heat?
The key is balance—if fire overpowers or underperforms, its heat is not innate.
3
Understand the concept of inclusion
Al-Razi says the difference between innate and foreign fire heat is not in essence (māhiyya) but in inclusion (dākhil) in the composite. Think about what 'inclusion' means here: innate heat is part of the body's structure, foreign heat is not.
This is the core of al-Razi's argument—they are the same heat, just one is inside and the other outside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من جنس أي نوع واحد . فإن الإمام الرازي قال : والذي عندي أن النار إذا خالطت سائر العناصر وأفادتها طبخاً ونضجاً واعتدالاً وقواماً ولم تبلغ في الكثرة إلى حيث تبطل قوامها وتحرقها، ولم تكن في القلة بحيث تعجز عن الطبخ الموجب للاعتدال فحرارتها هي الحرارة الغريزية. وإنما كانت دافعة للحر الغريب لأنَّ ذلك الغريب يحاول التفريق وتلك الحرارة الغريزية أفادت المركب من الطبخ والنضج ما يعسر معه على الحرارة الغريبة تفريق أجزائه فالتفاوت بين الغريزية والغريبة النارية ليس في الماهية بل في كون الغريزية داخلة في ذلك المركب دون تلك الغريبة حتى لو توهمنا الغريبة داخلة فيه أو تدفعه بعد تأثر البدن به إما بنفسها بأن صارت قوية على دفعه بعد تفرقة أو بإمداد دواء يفيدها قوة، وإن كان الدواء وارداً بعد السم لإقبال الطبيعة على الدواء لموافقتها لها في حفظ التركيب . أجزائها . قوله : (الرطوبات الغريزية وهي الحاصلة في بدن الحي بعد تفاعل العناصر . قوله : ( ومنهم من جعلهما إلخ ) إِليه ذهب جالينوس وتبعه الأطباء. قوله : (بل) في كون الغريزية إلخ) أي قائمة بما هو داخل في المركب موجب لالتئام قوله : ( فإنها آلة للطبيعة) الطبيعة قد تطلق على النفس باعتبار تدبيرها للبدن على التسخير لا الاختيار وهو المراد هاهنا وقد تطلق على الصورة النوعية للبسائط كما سيأتي في مباحث القدرة . قوله: (ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من (جنس ورد بأن الحرارة الغريزية تفارق بالموت دون الأسطقسية كما يدرك في بشرته، ولذا يتعفن بدنه وينتفخ انتفاخاً عظيماً ولو كان وسط الجمد والثلج فهما متغايران قطعاً. وحكي عن أرسطو أنَّ الحرارة الغريزية من جنس الحرارة التي تفيض من الأجرام السماوية فإنه إذا امتزجت العناصر وانكسرت سورة كيفياتها حصل للمركب نوع وحدة وبساطة بها يناسب البسائط السماوية ففاضت عليه مزاج معتدل به حفظ التركيب وحرارة غريزية بها قوام الحياة وقبول علاقة النفس. قوله : ( بل في كون الغريزية داخلة في ذلك المركب أراد أنها كالجزء في عدم الانفكاك لا أنها جزء حقيقة إذ لا شك في أنها عارضة للمركب وها هنا بحث . وهو أن سياق كلامه يدل على أن الدافع للجزء الغريب إنما يدفعه لكونه جزءاً من المركب ألا يرى إلى قوله : حتى لو توهمنا الغريبة داخلة إلخ . فيشكل بالترياق يشرب على السموم حيث يدفع بحرارته حرارة السموم مع
And some made them—i.e., the innate and the fire (heat)—of one genus, i.e., one species. For Imam al-Razi said: My view is that fire, when it mixes with the other elements and imparts to them cooking, ripening, balance, and consistency, and does not reach such abundance as to ruin their consistency and burn them, nor such scarcity as to be incapable of the cooking that leads to balance, its heat is the innate heat. It is repelling of foreign heat only because that foreign heat tries to disperse, and that innate heat has given the composite from cooking and ripening such that it is difficult for the foreign heat to disperse its parts. So the difference between innate and foreign fire heat is not in essence but in that the innate heat is included in that composite while the foreign heat is not, even if we imagine the foreign heat included in it—or it repels it after the body is affected by it, either by itself having become strong enough to repel it after its dispersal, or by the supply of a medicine that gives it strength, even if the medicine is administered after the poison due to nature's inclination toward the medicine for its agreement with it in preserving the composition—its parts. His saying: ('al-ruṭūbāt al-ghariziyya') which are the moistures that occur in the living body after the interaction of the elements. His saying: ('wa-manhum man jaʿalahumā' etc.) Galen went to this and the physicians followed him. His saying: ('bal') in that the innate is inside etc.) i.e., subsisting in what is inside the composite, causing its cohesion. His saying: ('fa-innahā āla li-l-ṭabīʿa') The term 'ṭabīʿa' (nature) may be applied to the soul (nafs) in terms of its management of the body by subjugation not choice—and that is meant here—and may be applied to the specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) of simple bodies, as will come in the discussions of power. His saying: ('wa-manhum man jaʿalahumā' i.e., the innate and the fire from a genus) and it is refuted by that innate heat departs at death while elemental (asṭuqussiyya) heat does not, as perceived in his skin, and thus his body decays and swells greatly even if it were in the middle of ice and snow, so they are definitely different. And it was narrated from Aristotle that innate heat is of the same genus as the heat that overflows from celestial bodies, for when the elements mix and the dominance of their qualities is broken, the composite acquires a kind of unity and simplicity by which it becomes suitable to the celestial simples, so a balanced temperament overflows upon it by which the composition is preserved, and an innate heat by which life subsists and the soul's attachment is accepted. His saying: ('bal fī kawn al-ghariziyya dākhila fī dhālika al-murakkab') He meant that it is like a part in terms of inseparability, not that it is a part in reality, for there is no doubt that it is accidental to the composite. And here there is a discussion: That the context of his speech indicates that the repeller of the foreign part repels it only because it is a part of the composite—do you not see his saying: 'even if we imagine the foreign included in it' etc. So it is problematic with the theriac (tiryāq) that is drunk against poisons, as it repels with its heat the heat of the poisons...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the minority view that innate heat (ghariziyya) and fire heat (nariyya) are of one genus. It cites Imam al-Razi's argument that when fire acts on elements in a balanced way, its heat becomes the innate heat. The difference is not in essence but in inclusion: innate heat is inside the composite, while foreign fire heat is outside. The commentator then notes that Galen and physicians followed this view. A refutation is given: innate heat departs at death while elemental heat remains, as seen by decomposition even in cold environments—proving they are different. Aristotle's view that innate heat is of celestial origin is also mentioned. A concluding objection discusses the theriac (tiryāq) which repels poison's heat with its own heat, challenging the argument.

حرارة نارية
جنس
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All heat is essentially the same and indistinguishable.
Correction: The passage argues that heat from fire and innate heat are the same in essence, but they differ in their relation to the composite—one is included, the other is not.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the key difference between innate heat and foreign fire heat?
A) Their substance (jawhar)
B) Their essence (māhiyya)
C) Their inclusion (dukhūl) in the composite
D) Their temperature

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that innate heat and fire heat are essentially the same; they differ only in whether they are integrated into the living composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, what 'heat' (passion, drive, anger) have you allowed to enter and become part of your character, versus kept outside? Are you actively integrating beneficial influences until they become innate, or do you let destructive foreign 'heat' break your composure?
?
Al-Razi says innate heat strengthens the composite so that foreign heat cannot easily disperse it. What beliefs or practices have become so internalized that they protect your faith even when opposing ideas attack? Are they truly inside you, or just external knowledge?
?
Imagine you could 'mix' a difficult trial into your soul—like fire mixing with elements—until it brings about balance rather than destruction. What trial are you currently facing, and how can you let it cook and ripen you instead of burning you?

Refutation and Alternative Views on Innate Heat
Refutation of the genus unity and presentation of alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence that innate heat and fire heat are essentially different, including the post-mortem decomposition argument and Aristotle's celestial origin theory.

Core Idea
Innate heat leaves the body at death; elemental heat remains—proving they are distinct.

The view that innate heat and fire heat are one genus was refuted by a simple observation: after death, the body decomposes even in extreme cold, showing that innate heat has departed while elemental heat persists. This lesson presents this refutation, Aristotle's alternative that innate heat comes from celestial bodies, and a concluding puzzle about theriac that challenges al-Razi's inclusion argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation of the refutation. Look for the key evidence: post-mortem decomposition.
The refutation starts with 'wuridda bi-anna' (it is refuted by).
2
Understand the decomposition evidence
The argument says that innate heat departs at death, while elemental (asṭuqussiyya) heat remains. How does this prove they are different? Consider the phrase 'even if in the middle of ice and snow'.
If the body decays despite extreme cold, the heat that remains is not the same as the lost innate heat.
3
Learn Aristotle's alternative view
Aristotle is reported to say that innate heat is of the same genus as celestial heat. How does this fit with the refutation? It offers a different origin.
Aristotle's view provides a positive alternative: innate heat comes from the heavens, not from fire.
4
Consider the theriac problem
The passage ends with an objection: if al-Razi's inclusion argument were true, then theriac (a remedy) would have to be part of the composite to repel poison heat, but it is ingested after poisoning. Think about why this is problematic.
This objection is not fully resolved in the passage; it shows a tension in al-Razi's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من جنس أي نوع واحد . فإن الإمام الرازي قال : والذي عندي أن النار إذا خالطت سائر العناصر وأفادتها طبخاً ونضجاً واعتدالاً وقواماً ولم تبلغ في الكثرة إلى حيث تبطل قوامها وتحرقها، ولم تكن في القلة بحيث تعجز عن الطبخ الموجب للاعتدال فحرارتها هي الحرارة الغريزية. وإنما كانت دافعة للحر الغريب لأنَّ ذلك الغريب يحاول التفريق وتلك الحرارة الغريزية أفادت المركب من الطبخ والنضج ما يعسر معه على الحرارة الغريبة تفريق أجزائه فالتفاوت بين الغريزية والغريبة النارية ليس في الماهية بل في كون الغريزية داخلة في ذلك المركب دون تلك الغريبة حتى لو توهمنا الغريبة داخلة فيه أو تدفعه بعد تأثر البدن به إما بنفسها بأن صارت قوية على دفعه بعد تفرقة أو بإمداد دواء يفيدها قوة، وإن كان الدواء وارداً بعد السم لإقبال الطبيعة على الدواء لموافقتها لها في حفظ التركيب . أجزائها . قوله : (الرطوبات الغريزية وهي الحاصلة في بدن الحي بعد تفاعل العناصر . قوله : ( ومنهم من جعلهما إلخ ) إِليه ذهب جالينوس وتبعه الأطباء. قوله : (بل) في كون الغريزية إلخ) أي قائمة بما هو داخل في المركب موجب لالتئام قوله : ( فإنها آلة للطبيعة) الطبيعة قد تطلق على النفس باعتبار تدبيرها للبدن على التسخير لا الاختيار وهو المراد هاهنا وقد تطلق على الصورة النوعية للبسائط كما سيأتي في مباحث القدرة . قوله: (ومنهم من جعلهما أي الغريزية والنارية من (جنس ورد بأن الحرارة الغريزية تفارق بالموت دون الأسطقسية كما يدرك في بشرته، ولذا يتعفن بدنه وينتفخ انتفاخاً عظيماً ولو كان وسط الجمد والثلج فهما متغايران قطعاً. وحكي عن أرسطو أنَّ الحرارة الغريزية من جنس الحرارة التي تفيض من الأجرام السماوية فإنه إذا امتزجت العناصر وانكسرت سورة كيفياتها حصل للمركب نوع وحدة وبساطة بها يناسب البسائط السماوية ففاضت عليه مزاج معتدل به حفظ التركيب وحرارة غريزية بها قوام الحياة وقبول علاقة النفس. قوله : ( بل في كون الغريزية داخلة في ذلك المركب أراد أنها كالجزء في عدم الانفكاك لا أنها جزء حقيقة إذ لا شك في أنها عارضة للمركب وها هنا بحث . وهو أن سياق كلامه يدل على أن الدافع للجزء الغريب إنما يدفعه لكونه جزءاً من المركب ألا يرى إلى قوله : حتى لو توهمنا الغريبة داخلة إلخ . فيشكل بالترياق يشرب على السموم حيث يدفع بحرارته حرارة السموم مع

It cites Imam al-Razi's argument that when fire acts on elements in a balanced way, its heat becomes the innate heat. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since the body decomposes after death, the heat that causes decay is the same as the heat that was present during life.
Correction: The passage shows that decomposition occurs due to remaining elemental heat, while the innate heat has left, proving they are different.

Checkpoint
What evidence is given to refute the claim that innate heat and fire heat are of one genus?
A) Theriac works against poisons
B) Aristotle's opinion
C) Innate heat departs at death while elemental heat remains, causing decomposition
D) Al-Razi changed his mind

Key Takeaway
The decomposition evidence and the theriac problem strongly suggest that innate heat is essentially different from fire heat, with Aristotle offering a celestial source as an alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body's innate heat departs at death, leaving only decaying elemental heat. What does that stark difference tell you about the true source of your life? Are you investing more in what perishes or in what comes from beyond the elements?
?
The theriac problem shows that a later remedy can still work—your repentance and good deeds are never introduced too late. What 'remedy' have you been putting off, and what step will you take today to apply it before your innate heat leaves?
?
If innate heat is of celestial origin, your very vitality is a gift from the heavens. How does that change your sense of accountability to the One who sustains that heat every moment? What will you do today to honor that gift?

Innate Heat: Definition and Distinction
Al-Jurjani defines innate heat and distinguishes it from accidental heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of innate heat as fire heat tempered by mixture and how it differs from accidental heat.

Core Idea
Innate heat is part of the compound; accidental heat is external.

This passage unpacks a key concept in natural philosophy: innate heat. Al-Jurjani explains that innate heat is not raw fire but fire that has been moderated by the compound's mixture, giving it a balanced temperament. He then distinguishes it from accidental heat, which is external and seeks to disrupt the compound. Let's read and analyze.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the definition and the distinction.
Notice the parentheses: they indicate the author's original wording.
2
Identify the definition of innate heat
Find where innate heat is defined as fire heat that has departed from its purity and gained a balanced temperament.
What does 'departed from its purity' mean?
3
Understand the role of mixture
The mixture (mizaj) gives a balanced temperament, leading to perfect cohesion of the compound's parts.
This cohesion resists external forces.
4
Grasp the distinction between innate and accidental heat
The passage states one is part of the compound, the other is external, even though they share the same essence.
That is the key difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى ما نقلناه أشار المصنف بقوله ( الغريزية ( هي الحرارة ( النارية ) التي خرجت عن صرافتها ( واستفادت بالمزاج مزاجاً معتدلاً حصل به التئام تام بين أجزاء المركب (فإذا أرادت الحرارة) الغريبة ) والبرودة تفريقها أي تفريق أجزائه وتغييرها عن اعتدالها ( عسر عليها ) ذلك التفريق والتغيير ) والفرق ( بين الجارين الغريزي والغريب ( أنَّ أحدهما جزء المركب والآخر خارج عنه مع كونهما متوافقين في الماهية.
And to what we have transmitted, the author (al-Muṣannif) indicated by his saying: (The innate heat) is (the fiery heat) that has departed from its purity (and acquired through the mixture a balanced temperament, by which a perfect cohesion among the parts of the compound came about. (Then if the) accidental (heat) and coldness want to separate them, i.e., separate its parts and change them from their equilibrium, (it becomes difficult for them) that separation and change.) And the difference (between the two adjacent [heats], the innate and the accidental, is) that (one of them is part of the compound and the other is external, while they are compatible in essence).
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya) and distinguishes it from accidental heat (al-gharība). Al-Jurjani begins by saying that the author of the Mawaqif (al-Ijī) indicated the definition: innate heat is the heat of fire that has left its pure, unadulterated state and gained a balanced temperament through the mixture of the compound's components. This balance results in a perfect cohesion of the compound's parts. If an external heat or cold tries to disrupt this cohesion, it becomes difficult because the innate heat resists. The key distinction between the two types of heat is that innate heat is an integral part of the compound, while accidental heat is external to it, even though they are similar in essence. This definition sets the stage for understanding how heat functions within composite bodies in natural philosophy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Innate heat is identical to ordinary fire heat.
Correction: Innate heat is fire heat that has been moderated by mixture, losing its purity and gaining a balanced temperament.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between innate heat and accidental heat?
A) They have different essences.
B) Innate heat is part of the compound; accidental heat is external.
C) Innate heat is hotter than accidental heat.
D) Accidental heat comes only from the sun.

Key Takeaway
Innate heat is fire heat moderated by mixture, making it part of the compound, while accidental heat is external yet similar in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body's innate heat is what keeps you alive—yet you rarely think of it. What external 'heat'—worldly desires, anxiety, or pride—is trying to separate and disturb your inner equilibrium? Identify one such influence and commit to reducing its hold on you starting today.
?
This lesson teaches that innate heat becomes part of the compound only through a balanced temperament. Is your own spiritual temperament balanced? What elements of your character (anger, greed, envy) are off-balance, and how will you seek the proper moderation to achieve inner cohesion?
?
If accidental heat can disrupt the compound's harmony, what 'accidental' beliefs or habits have you allowed to enter your heart that are not truly part of your nature? Name one, and describe one concrete step you will take to remove its influence on your spiritual life.

Cold Defined: Privation or Sensible Quality?
Al-Jurjani defines cold and defends against the sensation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām definition of cold as absence of heat from a naturally hot body and the debate over whether cold is a non-existential or a sensible quality.

Core Idea
Cold is not mere absence; it is a sensed quality that changes while the body persists.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a key debate in kalām physics: Is cold simply the absence of heat, or is it a real, sensible quality? The definition includes a careful qualification to exclude the celestial spheres, and the objection from sensation challenges the very foundation of the privation theory. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the definition of cold as 'absence of heat from what is naturally hot.' Note the qualification—why it is not absolute absence.
The qualification avoids calling the celestial spheres 'cold'.
2
Identify the correlation
Notice that this makes heat and cold a case of privation and possession (تقابل العدم والملكة).
3
Encounter the objection
The objection states that cold is sensed like heat, but absence cannot be sensed. This is a direct attack on the definition.
4
Analyze the counter-argument
The response uses the example of water: cold intensifies, weakens, and disappears while the water remains. Therefore cold is not a non-existential attribute.
This supports the view that cold is a real quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها : البرودة قيل : هي عدم الحرارة لا مطلقا بل عما من شأنه أن يكون حاراً واعتبر هذا القيد احترازاً عن الفلك فإن عدم حرارته لا تسمى برودة إذ ليس شأنه أن يكون حاراً. وعلى هذا فالتقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة ويبطله من أي هذا القول أنها أعني البرودة محسوسة كالحرارة والعدم لا يحس بالضرورة. لا يقال : المحسوس حال عدم الحرارة ليس هو البرودة بل هو ذات الجسم لأن البرد يشتد ويضعف ويعدم وذات الجسم باقية بحالها. فإنا نحس من الماء برداً شديداً جداً ثم يضعف ذلك البرد شيئاً فشيئاً إلى أن ينعدم بالكلية مع أن الماء باق في هذه الأحوال على جوهره الذاتي فلا تكون البرودة أمراً عدمياً.
The fifth [point]: Coldness. It is said: It is the absence of heat, not absolutely, but [the absence] from what is naturally hot. And this qualification is considered as a precaution against the sphere, for the lack of heat in it is not called coldness because it is not naturally hot. And on this [basis], the correlation between them is that of privation and possession. And it is invalidated by whoever makes this statement: that it, meaning coldness, is sensed like heat, and absence is not sensed necessarily. It cannot be said: The sensed thing during the absence of heat is not coldness but rather the substance of the body, because cold intensifies and weakens and disappears while the substance of the body remains as it is. For we sense from water an extremely intense cold, then that cold weakens little by little until it disappears entirely while the water remains in these states on its essential substance; thus coldness is not a non-existential matter.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is the fifth discussion, concerning cold (al-burūda). It defines cold as the absence of heat, but not an absolute absence; rather it is the absence of heat from an entity that is naturally disposed to be hot. This qualification excludes the celestial spheres, whose lack of heat is not called cold because they are not naturally meant to be hot. Consequently, the relationship between heat and cold is that of privation and possession (تقابل العدم والملكة). Al-Jurjani then reports a refutation of this view: cold is sensed just like heat, while an absence cannot be sensed. One might object that what is sensed in the state of cold is not cold itself but the body's substance, because cold can intensify, weaken, and disappear while the body's substance persists. To illustrate, we sense very intense cold from water, then the cold gradually weakens until it disappears completely, yet the water remains unchanged in its essential substance. Therefore, cold is not a non-existential attribute; it is a real quality.

Key Terms
البرودة
عدم الحرارة
شأن
تقابل العدم والملكة
محسوس
ذات الجسم
يشتد ويضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cold is simply the absence of heat, and absence cannot be sensed.
Correction: Cold is a sensible quality that intensifies and weakens, as shown by the water example, so it is not mere absence.

Checkpoint
What is the qualification added to the definition of cold to exclude the celestial spheres?
A) It is the absence of heat from any body.
B) It is the absence of heat from something that is naturally hot.
C) It is the absence of heat from something that is naturally cold.
D) It is the absence of heat only in the sublunary realm.

Key Takeaway
Cold is defined as the absence of heat from a naturally hot body, but the sensation objection reveals it is a real, changing quality, not a mere privation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel cold, do you ever dismiss it as just 'the absence of heat'? How does this passage challenge you to take even the most ordinary sensations seriously as real qualities with their own reality?
?
The water example shows that cold can be intense, then fade, while the water remains the same. In your spiritual life, are there 'qualities' you have dismissed as mere absences (like lack of patience, lack of gratitude) that might actually be real, changing states requiring direct attention? What would it mean to treat them as real?
?
This argument refutes the idea that cold is nothing but a lack. Are there relationships in your life that you have reduced to 'just the absence of conflict'? How might they be positive, felt realities that need nurturing, not just the removal of something negative?
