Refuting the Objection from Elimination
The author refutes an objection that elimination of whiteness proves it is imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that the elimination of whiteness proves it is imaginary.

Core Idea
Elimination does not prove imaginary; the recipient only needs sequential absence.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: if whiteness can disappear, doesn't that show it is imaginary? He responds by clarifying that a substrate does not need to be permanently devoid of a quality to receive it; rather, it simply cannot hold opposites simultaneously. This subtle distinction preserves the reality of accidents like whiteness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premises
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the objector's two main points: non-elimination doesn't prove reality, and elimination doesn't prove imaginary.
Notice the examples: blackness of youth, alchemy.
2
Understand the author's response
The author responds by denying the major premise. The recipient of a thing does not have to be devoid of it absolutely; otherwise, it could never be described by it.
The key phrase: 'والقابل للشيء لا يجب أن يكون عنه'.
3
Grasp the sequential absence argument
The commentator clarifies that the original proof only requires sequential absence of colors, not permanent absence. This refutes the objection.
The recipient must be devoid 'على التعاقب'.
4
Reflect on the deeper implication
Consider why this matters: if the recipient had to be always devoid, nothing could ever be described by any quality. The sequential reading saves the reality of change.
This connects to the broader debate on the reality of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه بأنه عدم الانسلاخ لا يدل على كونه حقيقياً إذ يجوز أن يكون سبب تخيله لازماً لبعض الأجسام على أن سواد الشباب ينسلخ بالشيب وأهل الإكسير يبيضون النحاس برصاص مكلس وزرنيخ مصعد وبأن انسلاخ البياض لا يدل على أنه تخيل لجواز أن يكون حقيقياً مفارقاً والقابل للشيء لا يجب أن يكون عنه وإلا امتنع اتصافه به فلا يكون قابلاً له. قوله : ( والقابل للشيء إلخ) حاصل هذا البحث منع كون البياض تخييلياً بمنع كبرى دليله فاللائق لترتيب البحث تقديمه على قوله : وبأن انسلاخ ،إلخ، لأنه تسليم للانسلاخ، ثم إن هذا البحث مندفع بما قررناه لأن المعلل لم يقل بأن القابل لكل شيء يجب أن يكون عاريا عنه بل القابل للألوان يجب خلوه على التعاقب . قوله : ( وإلا امتنع إلخ) لأنَّ القابل بمعنى المعروض إذا وجب خلوه عن العارض حال القبول والاتصاف امتنع اتصافه به فلا يرد ما توهم أن المراد أن القابل ما دام قابلاً يجب خلوه فلا على أن يراد بالقابل المستعد ينافي اتصاف ذاته به
It was objected to him that the non-elimination does not indicate that it is real, because it is possible that the cause of its imagination is necessary for some bodies, as the blackness of youth is eliminated by gray hair, and the people of alchemy whiten copper with calcined lead and sublimated arsenic. And that the elimination of whiteness does not indicate that it is imaginary, because it is possible that it is a real [color] that separates. And the recipient of a thing does not have to be devoid of it; otherwise, it would be impossible for it to be described by it, so it would not be a recipient of it. His saying: 'And the recipient of a thing etc.': the gist of this discussion is to deny that whiteness is imaginary by denying the major premise of its proof. So it is fitting for the order of the discussion to present it before his saying: 'and that elimination, etc.' because it is an admission of elimination. Then this discussion is refuted by what we have established, because the person giving the reason did not say that the recipient of everything must be devoid of it, but rather the recipient of colors must be devoid of them sequentially. His saying: 'otherwise it would be impossible, etc.': because the recipient, in the sense of the substratum, if its being devoid of the accident at the time of reception and description is necessary, then its being described by it would be impossible. So it does not refute what was imagined, that what is meant is that the recipient, as long as it is a recipient, must be devoid of it. So it is not such that if the recipient is meant as the prepared [substrate], it would contradict its being described by it.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that whiteness is imaginary, and then provides a response. The objector argues that the mere disappearance (insilakh) of whiteness does not prove it is real, because it could be that the cause of its being imaginary is attached to some bodies. For example, the blackness of youth disappears with gray hair, and alchemists whiten copper. Moreover, the disappearance of whiteness does not prove it is imaginary, because it could be a real color that separates. The objector further argues that the recipient of a thing does not have to be devoid of it; otherwise, it would be impossible for it to take on the quality. The commentator clarifies that the objector is trying to deny the major premise of the proof for whiteness being imaginary. He then refutes this by saying that the one who argues for whiteness being imaginary did not claim that the recipient must be devoid of everything, but only that the recipient of colors must be devoid of them sequentially. The commentator explains that if the recipient (as substratum) must be devoid at the moment of reception, then it could not be described by the quality; but this is not the intended meaning. The intended meaning is that the recipient, as a potential substrate, must be clear of the quality to receive its opposite, but not that it must always be devoid.

Key Terms
الانسلاخ (elimination)
التخيل (imaginary)
القابل (recipient)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Elimination of a quality proves that quality is imaginary.
Correction: Elimination only shows sequential change; the quality can be real and the substrate simply does not hold opposites simultaneously.

Checkpoint
In the response to the objection, what does the commentator say about the recipient of colors?
A) It must be devoid of all colors at all times.
B) It must be devoid of colors sequentially.
C) It must never be devoid.
D) It cannot receive colors.

Key Takeaway
The elimination of a quality does not prove it is imaginary; substrates only need to be free of a quality sequentially to receive another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a quality you once had—like patience or generosity—that you feel you have lost. Does its disappearance mean it was never real in you? How does this lesson challenge you to see that loss differently?
?
You have received and shed many states: anger, joy, focus. What would it mean to treat each as a real but passing quality that your soul (the recipient) took on sequentially? How might this change how you judge your current state?
?
The objector assumed that if a quality leaves, it must be imaginary. Where in your life have you dismissed a past virtue or experience as 'not real' simply because it ended? Will you now choose to honor it as real but departed?

Ibn Sina's Two Positions on Whiteness
Ibn Sina's two statements on whiteness and their reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's two apparently contradictory positions on the reality of whiteness and how they are reconciled.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina first denied real whiteness, then affirmed it through another mode of occurrence.

Ibn Sina's statements on whiteness appear contradictory: initially he denies its real existence, attributing it to imagination, but elsewhere he affirms it occurs by a different path. This lesson examines these two positions and sets the stage for the empirical evidence that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the two quotations from Ibn Sina.
2
Identify the two statements
Find where Ibn Sina denies knowing whiteness as a real occurrence and where he says it may occur by another way.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The commentator notes that the first statement treats occurrence as imaginary, the second as real. This sets up the need for empirical evidence.
The third remark about transparency clarifies the context.

فإنه مبني قوله : (لا أعلم حدوث البياض فيكون حدوثه حدوثاً تخيلياً فلا الحدوث يقتضي وجوده في الخارج فيكون لوناً حقيقياً . قوله : (قد يحدث البياض بطريق آخر إلخ) فيكون حدوثه حدوثاً حقيقياً. قوله : ( كونه شفافاً) أي غير ملون . ( الأول : أنَّ بياض البيض مع كونه شفافاً يصير أبيض بعد سلقه وإغلائه بالنار ولم تحدث النار بالطبخ فيه هوائية وتخلخلا حتى يتخيل فيه البياض لأنه بعد الطبخ أثقل مما كان قبله وما ذلك إلا لخروج الهوائية منه، وأيضاً لو دخلت فيه هوائية وبيضته لكان ذلك خثورة لا انعقاداً. (الثاني: الدواء المسمى بلبن العذراء ويتخذه أهل الحيلة وهو خل طبخ فيه المردارسنج حتى انحل فيه ثم يصفى الخل حتى يبقى شفافاً في الغاية ثم يخلط هذا الخل المصفى بماء طبخ فيه القلى أولاً ، ثم طبخ فيه المردارسنج ثانياً، وصفي غاية التصفية حتى يصير الماء كأنه الدمعة فإنه ينعقد ذلك المخلوط فيبيض غاية الابيضاض كاللبن الرائب ثم يجف بعد الابيضاض فليس ابيضاضه لأنَّ شفافاً تفرق ودخل فيه الهواء وإلا لم يجف بعد الابيضاض لكنه لا يجف إلا بعده فدل ذلك على كثرة الأرضية حينئذ . وفي المباحث المشرقية أنه إذا خلط هذان الماءان ينعقد فيه المنحل الشفاف من المرتك ويبيض وليس ذلك لأنَّ شفافاً تفرق ودخل الهواء فيه، لأن ذلك كان منحلاً ومتفرقاً في الخل ولا لأن تلك الأجزاء تقاربت حتى انعكس ضوء بعضها إلى بعض فإن حدة ماء القلى أولى بالتفريق، بل ذلك على سبيل الاستحالة فليس كل بياض على الوجه الذي قالوه ولقائل أن يقول على هذين الوجهين : جاز أن يكون لتخيل البياض سبب آخر لا نعلمه إذ المفروض أنه لا اعتماد على الحس وإلا لوجب الحكم بكون الثلج أبيض حقيقة . ( الثالث : الاتجاه من البياض إلى السواد يكون بطريق شتى فمن الغبرة فالعودية أي يتوجه الجسم من البياض إلى الغبرة ثم منها إلى العودية ثم كذلك حتى يسود وهذا هو الطريق الساذج كأنه يأخذ
For it is based on his saying: 'I do not know the occurrence of whiteness, so its occurrence is an imaginary occurrence, so occurrence does not require its existence externally, so it is a real color.' His saying: 'Whiteness may occur by another way, etc.' so its occurrence is a real occurrence. His saying: 'its being transparent' means it is not colored. (First: The whiteness of egg, despite being transparent, becomes white after boiling and heating with fire, and fire did not introduce airiness and rarefaction into it so that whiteness is imagined in it, because after cooking it is heavier than before, and that is only due to the exit of airiness from it. Also, if airiness had entered it and made it white, that would be curdling (khuthura) not coagulation (in'iqad). (Second: The drug called 'milk of the virgin' (laban al-'adhra') which the people of artifice prepare – vinegar in which maradasanj (a mineral) is boiled until it dissolves, then the vinegar is filtered until it remains extremely transparent, then this filtered vinegar is mixed with water in which qali (alkali) was first boiled, then maradasanj was boiled in it a second time, and filtered to the utmost until the water becomes like a tear, then that mixture coagulates and becomes intensely white like clotted milk, then dries after whitening. Its whitening is not because a transparent thing separated and air entered into it; otherwise it would not dry after whitening, but it does not dry except after that, so that indicates the abundance of earthiness then. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: when these two waters are mixed, the dissolved transparent part from the maradasanj coagulates and whitens, and that is not because a transparent thing separated and air entered into it, because that was dissolved and separated in the vinegar, nor because those parts approached each other until light reflected from some to others – for the sharpness of the qali water is more likely to separate – rather that is by way of transformation. So not every whiteness is in the manner they said. And one may say concerning these two ways: it is possible that the imagination of whiteness has another cause that we do not know, since it is assumed that there is no reliance on the senses; otherwise it would be necessary to judge that snow is white in reality. (Third: The transition from whiteness to blackness occurs in various ways: from dust-color to wood-color, i.e., the body moves from whiteness to dust-color, then to wood-color, and so on until it becomes black. And this is the simple way, as if it takes...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's two positions on whiteness and three reasons supporting its reality. First, Ibn Sina is quoted as saying he does not know whiteness occurring except through imagination, implying it is not a real color. Then he is quoted from another place saying it can occur by another way, implying real occurrence. The commentator clarifies that the first statement treats occurrence as imaginary, while the second treats it as real. Then three reasons are given. First reason: The whiteness of an egg after boiling. The egg becomes heavier, indicating loss of air, so whiteness does not come from air entering. Second reason: A chemical process producing a white substance (milk of the virgin). The description shows that the whitening is not due to air entering but a real transformation. The commentator adds from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that the process is by transformation (istihala), not by separation and air. An objection is raised: these could still be imaginary due to unknown causes. Third reason: The gradual transition from white to black through intermediate colors like dust-color and wood-color, which is a simple progression. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the third reason is clear in showing a continuous change that suggests real color change.

Key Terms
البياض (whiteness)
التخيل (imagination)
الحدوث (occurrence)
الشفاف (transparent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina always considered whiteness imaginary.
Correction: He also affirmed real whiteness through a different mode of occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first of Ibn Sina's positions on the occurrence of whiteness?
A) It occurs as a real event.
B) It occurs only in imagination.
C) It occurs but not in colors.
D) It occurs only in transparent bodies.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's two positions show that whiteness can be imaginary or real depending on the mode of occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you treated a visible truth as merely imaginary because it was uncomfortable? What would it take for you to admit its reality and act on it today?
?
Ibn Sina's first statement hides behind 'I do not know'. Is there an area of your life where you hide behind ignorance to avoid responsibility? What is one concrete step you will take to seek the truth about it?
?
The lesson reconciles two statements by distinguishing modes of occurrence. Which contradiction in your own life—between what you say and what you do—needs such an honest examination? How will you begin that reconciliation now?

Three Empirical Reasons for Real Whiteness
Three empirical proofs for real whiteness: egg, chemical, and transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three empirical arguments Ibn Sina provides to demonstrate the real occurrence of whiteness.

Core Idea
Whiteness is a real transformation, not merely an imaginary effect of air or light.

Having acknowledged that whiteness can occur really, Ibn Sina presents three observations to support its reality: the whitening of an egg, a chemical preparation, and the gradual transition from white to black. These arguments aim to show that whiteness involves substantial change, not just the entry of air.

Guided Path
1
Read the descriptions of each reason
Read the entire passage once to get an overview of the three reasons.
The third reason is incomplete in the source.
2
Analyze the first reason (egg)
Note why the egg's weight increase disproves air entering. The text says the egg becomes heavier, indicating loss of air, not entry.
3
Analyze the second reason (chemical)
Follow the chemical steps and note why the whitening is due to transformation, not air or light. Also consider the objection and response.
The passage includes a quote from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya and an objection about unknown causes.
4
Consider the third reason (transition)
Observe the gradual progression from white through intermediate colors to black, suggesting real change.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the idea is clear.

The Ancient Explanation of Whiteness
An ancient recipe for whiteness and its causal explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient method for producing whiteness (virgin's milk) and the reasoning that led them to attribute whiteness to earthy particles rather than air.

Core Idea
Whiteness, to the ancients, came not from air, but from earthiness.

Can you manufacture whiteness? The ancient scholars described a recipe for creating a white substance called 'virgin's milk.' Their explanation for why it turns white reveals a reasoning based on physical properties like transparency and drying. This lesson walks through their method and the logical inference they made about the cause of whiteness.

Guided Path
1
Read the recipe carefully
Follow the steps: first prepare a transparent vinegar by dissolving mercury stone, then prepare a transparent water with potash and mercury stone. Mix them to get a white coagulate that dries.
Notice the emphasis on transparency before mixing.
2
Identify the causal argument
The text explicitly rejects the idea that air entering a transparent substance causes whiteness. Look for the phrase 'ليس لأن شفافاً تفرق ودخل فيه الهواء'.
The key evidence: the mixture dries after whitening, which wouldn't happen if air were the cause.
3
Understand the inference to earthiness
Because the mixture dries after becoming white, the ancients concluded that the cause must be an abundance of earthy particles. This is their positive claim.
The text says 'فدل ذلك على كثرة الأرضية حينئذ'.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how the ancients used empirical observation (drying after whitening) to rule out one cause and infer another. This illustrates early scientific reasoning in a theological context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسمى بلبن العذراء ويتخذه أهل الحيلة ( وهو خل طبخ فيه المردارسنج حتى انحل فيه ثم يصفى الخل) حتى يبقى شفافاً في الغاية (ثم يخلط) هذا الخل المصفى بماء طبخ فيه القلى أولاً ، ثم طبخ فيه المردارسنج ثانياً، وصفي غاية التصفية حتى يصير الماء كأنه الدمعة فإنه ينعقد ذلك المخلوط (فيبيض) غاية الابيضاض كاللبن الرائب ( ثم يجف ) بعد الابيضاض ( فليس) ابيضاضه ( لأنَّ شفافاً تفرق ودخل فيه الهواء وإلا لم يجف بعد الابيضاض لكنه لا يجف إلا بعده فدل ذلك على كثرة الأرضية حينئذ .
Known as virgin's milk and used by the people of artifice. (It is vinegar in which mercury stone is cooked until it dissolves in it, then the vinegar is filtered) until it becomes extremely transparent. (Then this filtered vinegar is mixed) with water in which potash was cooked first, then mercury stone was cooked in it secondly, and filtered to the utmost until the water becomes like a tear. Then that mixture coagulates (and becomes white) extremely white like clotted milk. (Then it dries) after the whiteness. Its whiteness is not (because a transparent substance dispersed and air entered it; otherwise it would not dry after the whiteness, but it does not dry except after it, so that indicates an abundance of earthiness then.)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage describes the ancient view on the production of whiteness. It presents a recipe for creating a white substance called 'virgin's milk.' The recipe involves mixing filtered vinegar with a specially prepared water; then the mixture coagulates, becomes white, and then dries. The author then explains the ancient reasoning: the whiteness is not due to the dispersal of a transparent substance and the entry of air (because if that were the case, it would not dry after becoming white, but it does dry), so the cause must be an abundance of earthy particles. This is the conclusion of the ancients' claim.

Key Terms
البياض
المردارسنج
القلى
الشفاف
الخثورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whiteness is caused by air entering a transparent substance.
Correction: The ancients observed that the mixture dries after becoming white, which would not happen if air were the cause. They concluded that whiteness results from an abundance of earthy particles.

Checkpoint
According to the ancients, what causes the whiteness of the mixture?
A) The entry of air into a transparent substance
B) The abundance of earthy particles
C) The reflection of light between particles
D) The transformation (istihāla) of the mixture

Key Takeaway
The ancients argued that the whiteness of virgin's milk arises from an abundance of earthy particles, inferred from the observation that the mixture dries after whitening.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you settle for surface-level explanations (like 'whiteness comes from air') or do you dig deeper for the 'earthiness'—the underlying cause? What is one area of your life where you have accepted an easy answer without careful observation, and how will you begin to examine it with the rigor of the ancient alchemist?
?
The ancient author used a simple observation—drying after whitening—to overturn a seemingly plausible assumption. Is there a belief about yourself, your faith, or your purpose that you have never tested against observable reality? Will you commit to testing that belief today, no matter how uncomfortable the result might be?
?
The recipe required meticulous preparation (filtering twice, cooking twice) to produce a result. Are you willing to put that same care into seeking truth about your own soul? What step can you take this week to filter out the impurities in your understanding and seek clarity like 'the tear'?

The Objection-Answer on Whiteness: Unknown Causes and the Limits of Doubt
Objection and response on the cause of whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the objection about whiteness's unknown cause defines the limits of sensory doubt and refutes unlimited skepticism.

Core Idea
Doubt without known cause is sophistry.

This passage engages a hypothetical objection: if we cannot trust the senses, could whiteness have an unknown cause? The response clarifies that such doubt is only valid when a specific cause of imagination is known; otherwise, it leads to universal skepticism and loss of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full translation. Notice the structure: alternative explanation, then objection, then response.
The passage is from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya and discusses the cause of whiteness.
2
Identify the alternative explanation
Find the alternative explanation for whiteness: it occurs by istihāla (transformation), not by air dispersion or light reflection. Understand why the author rejects the other two explanations.
3
Understand the objection
The objection says: since we assume the senses are not trusted, maybe whiteness has an unknown cause of imagination. Grasp the reasoning: if sensory appearances can be deceptive, any explanation is possible.
4
Analyze the response
The response limits the doubt: lack of reliance on senses applies only when a specific cause of imagination is known. If we allow doubt for any unknown cause, we lose all knowledge and fall into sophistry.
The key phrase: 'عدم الاعتماد على الحس ليس إلا فيما يعرف له سبب التخيل'
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why unlimited doubt is destructive. Without the limit, we cannot trust any sensory knowledge. This shows the importance of grounding doubt in known imaginative causes.
This connects to broader themes in epistemology and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المباحث المشرقية أنه إذا خلط هذان الماءان ينعقد فيه المنحل الشفاف من المرتك ويبيض وليس ذلك لأنَّ شفافاً تفرق ودخل الهواء فيه، لأن ذلك كان منحلاً ومتفرقاً في الخل ولا لأن تلك الأجزاء تقاربت حتى انعكس ضوء بعضها إلى بعض فإن حدة ماء القلى أولى بالتفريق، بل ذلك على سبيل الاستحالة فليس كل بياض على الوجه الذي قالوه. ولقائل أن يقول على هذين الوجهين : جاز أن يكون لتخيل البياض سبب آخر لا نعلمه إذ المفروض أنه لا اعتماد على الحس وإلا لوجب الحكم بكون الثلج أبيض حقيقة. وقد يجاب عنه بأن عدم الاعتماد على الحس ليس إلا فيما يعرف له سبب التخيل أما لمجرد تجوز السبب فلا، وإلا فلا علم لأنَّ من فقد حساً فقد علماً، وهو منه سفسطة.
And in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: When these two waters are mixed, the dissolved transparent part from the mercury stone coagulates and becomes white. That is not because a transparent substance dispersed and air entered it, because that was dissolved and dispersed in the vinegar, nor because those particles came close so that the light of some reflected onto others, for the sharpness of the potash water is more likely to cause separation. Rather, it is by way of transformation (istihāla). So not every whiteness is in the way they said. And a speaker may say concerning these two ways: It is possible that the imagination of whiteness has another cause unknown to us, since it is assumed that there is no reliance on the senses, otherwise it would be necessary to judge that snow is truly white. And it may be answered that lack of reliance on the senses is only concerning that for which the cause of imagination is known. As for merely the possibility of a cause, no. Otherwise, there would be no knowledge, because one who loses a sense loses knowledge, and this is sophistry.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative explanation from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: the whiteness occurs by chemical transformation (istihāla), not by air dispersion or light reflection. Then an objection is raised: since we do not rely on the senses (the assumption that whiteness may be illusory), there could be an unknown cause for the imagination of whiteness. The answer refutes this by stating that lack of reliance on the senses applies only when a specific cause of imagination is known; mere possibility of an unknown cause does not overturn a known explanation. Otherwise, all knowledge would be lost, which is sophistry.

Key Terms
التخيل
الاستحالة
الحس
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the senses are not trustworthy, then any explanation for a phenomenon is equally possible, and we cannot have certain knowledge.
Correction: The response shows that we only suspend trust in the senses when a specific cause of imagination is known. Without such a cause, we rely on the known explanation; otherwise, universal doubt would destroy all knowledge and lead to sophistry.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute the objection that whiteness might have an unknown cause?
A) By stating that we must rely on the senses in all cases
B) By limiting doubt to cases where the cause of imagination is known
C) By asserting that snow is truly white
D) By claiming that the ancients' view is always correct

Key Takeaway
Unlimited doubt about causes leads to sophistry; rational doubt must be grounded in a known cause of imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth claim that contradicts your experience, do you ever dismiss it by thinking 'there might be some unknown cause' without actually investigating? How does this lesson call you to examine that habit?
?
Think of a time you used uncertainty as an excuse to avoid accepting a difficult truth. What would it mean for you to apply the principle of this lesson: that we must have a specific reason to doubt, not just a vague possibility?
?
This lesson warns that losing trust in the senses altogether leads to sophistry. In your own life, where have you been too quick to distrust what is clear, and how can you commit to grounded reasoning even when it challenges you?

The Paths from White to Black and Ibn Sina's Proof
Enumerates color transition paths and proves light is a third color element.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three paths from white to black and Ibn Sina's argument that the existence of multiple paths proves light is a distinct color element.

Core Idea
Multiple paths from white to black reveal that light, not just mixing, creates colors.

Ibn Sina presents three distinct transitions from whiteness to blackness, each passing through different intermediate colors. He then argues that if only black and white existed, only one path would be possible. The existence of multiple paths proves that a third visible element—light—must be involved. This passage introduces a key proof for light as a color element in classical optics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three paths
Read the passage and note the three paths: dust-color, wood-color; redness, ash-color; greenness, indigo.
These paths begin from whiteness and end in blackness.
2
Follow Ibn Sina's argument
Notice his reasoning: if only black and white, then only one path; but multiple paths exist, so there must be a third element. What could it be?
The key is that the paths differ in kind, not just intensity.
3
Confirm the third element
Ibn Sina identifies light as the only visible thing that is neither black nor white nor a compound of them. Examples of mixing light with black and white produce various colors.
Examples include cloud-lit sun and black smoke with fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( ومن الخضرة فالنيلية ) أي يأخذ من البياض إلى الخضرة، ثم إلى النيلية، ثم إلى السواد قال ابن سينا وهذه الطرق لا يجوز اختلاف ما يتركب عنه الألوان المتوسطة فإن لم يكن إلا بياض وسواد وكان أصل البياض وهو الضوء الذي قد استحال ببعض الوجوه لم يمكن في الأخذ من البياض إلى السواد إلا طريق واحد لا يقع فيه الاختلاف إلا بالشدة والضعف على حسب اختلاط السواد بالبياض. ولا هناك طرق مختلفة فإنَّ ثبوتها يتوقف على شوب من غيرهما ولا بد أن يكون يتصور ذلك الشوب من مرئي وليس في الأشياء ما يظن أنه مرئي وليس سواداً ولا بياضاً ولا مركباً منهما إلا الضوء فإذا جعل الضوء شيئاً غيرهما أمكن أن تتركب الألوان وتتعدد الطرق فإنه إذا اختلط السواد والبياض وحدهما كانت الطريقة طريقة الاغبرار لا غير وإن خالط السواد ضوء فكان مثل الغمامة التي تشرق عليها الشمس ومثل الدخان الأسود الذي تخالطه النار كان حمرة إن كان السواد غالباً على الضوء أو صفرة إن كان السواد مغلوباً، وكان هناك غلبة بياض مشرق ثم إن خالطت الصفرة سواداً ليس في أجزائه إشراق حدثت الخضرة إلى آخر ما سيأتي تفصيله فقوله : ( ولولا اختلاف ما ...)
(Third: The transition from whiteness to blackness occurs in various ways: from dust-color to wood-color) i.e., the body proceeds from whiteness to dust-color then from it to wood-color, and so on until it becomes black. This is the simple path, as if it begins from the start with a weak blackness, then the blackness intensifies little by little until it becomes pure. (And from redness then ash-color) i.e., it goes from whiteness to redness, then to ash-color, then to blackness. (And from greenness then indigo) i.e., it goes from whiteness to greenness, then to indigo, then to blackness. Ibn Sina said: These ways cannot differ in what composes the intermediate colors. If there were only whiteness and blackness, and the origin of whiteness is light which has transformed in some manner, then in going from whiteness to blackness there would only be one way, which differs only in intensity and weakness according to the mixing of black with white. There would be no different ways. For their existence depends on a mixture from something else, and that something else must be conceived as visible. And among things, there is nothing that is thought to be visible and is neither black nor white nor composed of them except light. So if light is made something other than them, it becomes possible for colors to be composed and for the ways to be multiplied. For if black and white alone are mixed, the way is the way of dustiness (ighbirār) only. And if black is mixed with light, like a cloud on which the sun shines, or like black smoke mixed with fire, it becomes redness if black dominates the light, or yellowness if black is dominated, and there is a dominance of bright whiteness. Then if yellowness is mixed with black that has no brightness in its parts, greenness occurs, to the detail that will come. So his saying: ('And if not for the difference...')
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage enumerates three distinct paths from white to black: through dust-color (ghubra), through redness then ash-color, and through greenness then indigo. Then Ibn Sina argues that if only black and white existed, there would be only one path (simple mixing). The existence of multiple paths proves that a third element must be involved—light. Light is the only visible thing that is neither black nor white nor a compound of them. When light mixes with black and white, it produces other colors. For example, black and white alone give dust-color; black with light gives red or yellow depending on dominance; yellow with non-luminous black gives green. Thus, the multiplicity of paths confirms light as a distinct color element. The passage ends with a reference to further detail.

Key Terms
الغبرة
العودية
القتمة
الخضرة
النيلية
الضوء
الاغبرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple color transitions result solely from mixing black and white in different proportions.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that if only black and white existed, only one path (the simple dust-color path) would be possible; the existence of different paths proves a third element (light) must be involved.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why must there be a third element besides black and white to explain the multiple paths from white to black?
A) Because the number of paths is more than one
B) Because black and white cannot mix
C) Because light is the only visible thing that is neither black nor white nor a compound of them
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's proof that light is a third color element rests on the observation that multiple transition paths from white to black cannot be explained by black and white alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see colors every day, but this lesson reveals a hidden logic: light itself is a color element. Does this new understanding increase your awe of the Creator's intricate design? If not, what is preventing you from seeing His signs in the visible world around you? Commit today to pause and reflect on one color transition in nature (like a sunrise or sunset) and contemplate the divine wisdom behind it.
?
Ibn Sina's proof relies on careful observation and reason. How often do you use your intellect to examine the natural world as a pathway to strengthen your faith? Identify one specific aspect of creation you have been taking for granted, and decide how you will study it more deeply this week to draw closer to Allah.
?
The multiplicity of paths from white to black shows that simple assumptions (only black and white) are inadequate. In your own spiritual life, are you assuming there is only one way to grow or repent? This lesson challenges you to recognize that Allah's creation is rich with variety—how might your own journey of faith benefit from embracing multiple paths of learning and worship?

Opinions on Primary Colors: Two vs Five and the Verdict of Uncertainty
Al-Jurjani presents theories on color origins and concludes with epistemic caution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient debate on primary colors (two vs five) and the epistemological caution of suspending judgment on whether all colors are composite.

Core Idea
Not all colors are obviously composite; some may be simple, so we must suspend judgment.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani examines two competing theories about the origin of colors: one that posits only black and white as primary, and another that adds red, yellow, and green. After detailing the mixing processes for each, he concludes that while we can observe compositional effects, we cannot definitively assert that every color beyond the five is composite — hence the need for epistemic caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the matn and the sharh (commentary).
2
Identify the two theories on primary colors
Find where Al-Jurjani presents the view that black and white are the only primary colors, and then where he presents the view that there are five primaries. Note how each theory explains the origin of other colors.
The two-primary theory uses mixing with light and smoke; the five-primary theory uses grinding and mixing colored bodies.
3
Understand the mixing examples
For the two-primary theory, trace the examples: dust color, redness, darkness, yellowness, greenness, etc. For the five-primary theory, note that the evidence is based on sensory observation of mixtures.
The terminology includes terms like 'kifi' al-ghamam' and 'sawad mushrif'.
4
Grasp the final verdict of tawaqquf
Al-Jurjani states that one cannot be certain whether colors beyond the five are composite or simple; therefore, one must suspend judgment. This is the key epistemological conclusion.
The phrase 'فالواجب أن يتوقف فيه' is central.
5
Reflect on why this caution matters
Consider how this approach reflects the broader methodology of kalam: when definitive proof is lacking, one must acknowledge uncertainty rather than claiming certainty.
This connects to the principle of tawaqquf in theological and philosophical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وقال قوم: من المعترفين بالألوان (الأصل) فيها (خمسة: السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة (والخضرة فهذه الخمسة ألوان بسيطة ( وتحصل البواقي بالتركيب) من هذه الخمسة ( بالمشاهدة ) فإن الأجسام الملونة بالألوان الخمسة إذا سحقت سحقاً ناعماً ثم خلط بعضها ببعض فإنه يظهر منها ألوان مختلفة بحسب مقادير المخلطات كما يشهد به الحس فدل ذلك على أن سائر الألوان مركبة منها ( والحق أن ذلك ) أعني تركيب هذه الخمسة على أنحاء شتى ( يحدث كيفيات في الحس) هي ألوان مختلفة . كما ذكرتم ( وأما أن كل كيفية ) لونية سوى هذه الخمسة هذا القبيل أي مما نركب منها ( فشيء لا سبيل إلى الجزم به ولا بعدمه إذ يجوز أن يكون هناك كيفية مفردة هي لون بسيط ويجوز أيضاً أن يكون جميع ما عدا الخمسة مركبة منها فالواجب أن يتوقف فيه . فهو من قوله : ( والبواقي تحصل بالتركيب قياساً للألوان الطبيعية على الصناعية. قوله : ( كفيء الغمام أي كاختلاطهما مع الضوء في الغمام . قوله : ( وإن خالطها أي الصفرة سواد (مشرق هكذا في المباحث المشرقية وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من قوله : ثم إن خالطت الصفرة سواداً ليس في أجزائه إشراق حدثت الخضرة مذكور في الشفاء ولعل ذلك الاختلاف لأجل إرادة الخضرة المشرقة وغير المشرقة .
The Third Observatory - Part One - First Goal: The Saying of the Ancients about Color. (And that) which we said (is not more improbable than what the philosophers say about light being a condition for the occurrence of all colors. For it follows from that the non-existence of colors in darkness and their occurrence when light falls upon their loci. So if one removes a lamp, for example, from a dark room, the colors of the objects in it disappear, and if it is returned, they become colored like them, due to the impossibility of the re-creation of the non-existent according to them. And there is no doubt that this is more improbable than the occurrence of whiteness in transparent parts by the mixing of air without temperament. (And whoever acknowledges their existence) i.e., the existence of blackness and whiteness (said:) (i.e., some of them) (they are the origin and the rest) of the colors are obtained (by composition from them in various ways). For if they are mixed alone, dust color occurs, and (if they are mixed not alone but) (with the light of the cloud's shadow) upon which the sun has shone (and smoke) that fire has mixed with, (redness) occurs if blackness predominates over light in general, and if its predominance intensifies (then darkness), and with the predominance of light over blackness yellowness occurs, and if yellowness (i.e., the yellow) is mixed with (bright) blackness (then greenness), and (greenness) if mixed (with whiteness) (then verdigris) which is dust-gray, and if greenness is mixed with blackness, intense leek green occurs, and (leek green) if mixed with blackness (along with a little redness) (then indigo). Then indigo if mixed with redness yields purple, and by analogy treat the rest of the colors. (And a group said) among those who acknowledge colors: (the origin) among them is (five: blackness, whiteness, redness, yellowness, and greenness. So these five are simple colors), and the rest are obtained (by composition) from these five (by observation). For if bodies colored with the five colors are finely ground and then some are mixed with others, different colors appear according to the proportions of the mixtures, as sensed. So that indicates that the rest of the colors are composed from them. (And the truth is that) i.e., the composition of these five in various ways (causes qualities in sensation) that are different colors, as you mentioned. (But as for every) color quality besides these five being of this type (i.e., composed from them), (it is something that cannot be definitively asserted or denied, because it is possible that there is a simple quality that is a simple color, and it is also possible that all besides the five are composed from them. So one must suspend judgment on it.) That is from his saying: 'and the rest are obtained by composition' analogizing natural colors to artificial ones. His saying: 'the shadow of the cloud' means mixing them with light in the cloud. His saying: 'and if yellowness is mixed with bright blackness' is thus in the Eastern Investigations, and what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned earlier in his saying: 'then if yellowness is mixed with blackness that has no brightness in its parts, greenness occurs' is mentioned in the Healing, and perhaps that difference is for the purpose of intending bright green and non-bright.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on the nature of colors, part of the larger section on physical qualities. The author begins by comparing the claim about light being a condition for color to the claim about whiteness occurring in transparent parts by mixing air, stating that the former is no less improbable. He then presents the view of some philosophers that blackness and whiteness are the two primary colors, and all others are mixtures of these two under various conditions (with light, smoke, etc.), providing specific examples of mixing and the resulting colors. He then presents an alternative view that there are five primary colors (black, white, red, yellow, green) and that other colors are mixtures of these five, as evidenced by sensory observation of mixing ground colored bodies. Finally, he states the correct position: mixing can indeed produce new qualities in sensation, but it is not certain whether every color beyond the five is composite; it could be a simple color. Therefore, one must suspend judgment (tawaqquf). The subsequent commentary explains specific phrases, such as the analogy to artificial colors and the difference in descriptions of green.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that all colors are composite and that the composition is certain based on sensory observation.
Correction: The author argues that while observation shows mixing produces new colors, it does not prove that every color is composite. There might be simple colors beyond the five, so certainty is not possible.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct stance on whether all colors beyond the five primary colors are composite?

C) Neither can be definitively asserted or denied.

Key Takeaway
The composition of colors beyond the five primaries is not certain, so one must suspend judgment (tawaqquf) rather than claiming definitive knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a belief you hold with great conviction about your own life or faith—perhaps something you have never questioned. What if that belief is like one of the 'colors beyond the five'—not as certain as you assume? Can you sit in that possibility without panic?
?
The author warns against assuming that every observed composite quality is necessarily composite in essence. Where in your relationship with Allah or others have you mistaken your interpretation for absolute truth? What would it mean to hold that interpretation lightly, with tawaqquf, while still acting on it?

Ibn Sina's Core Argument: Light as a Condition for Color's Existence
Al-Jurjani presents Ibn Sina's argument for light as a condition for color's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that light is necessary for the existence of color, not merely for its visibility.

Core Idea
Color does not exist in darkness; light is a condition for its very being.

Ibn Sina and many philosophers held that light is a condition for the existence of color itself. This lesson presents his core argument, which uses the example of a person in a dark cave to show that the absence of vision in darkness is due to color's non-existence, not an obstacle. By the end, you'll see why he concludes that light is necessary for color's actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and its explanation
Start by reading the claim that light is a condition for the existence of color. Notice how the author explains that color occurs actually when light is present.
2
Follow the logical disjunction
The argument says that if we don't see a body in darkness, it's either because color is absent or because there is an obstacle. Understand why these are the only two possibilities.
3
Examine the cave example
The cave example shows that dark air is not an obstacle to vision. This eliminates the second possibility, leaving only the first: color does not exist in darkness.
This is the key empirical evidence.
4
Consider the conclusion
The conclusion is that light is a condition for color's existence. Darkness is a privation, not an obstacle. Reflect on how this differs from saying light only helps us see an already existing color.

Source Text

المرصد الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : الضوء شرط وجود اللون ( قال ابن سينا وكثير من الحكماء الضوء شرط وجود اللون) في نفسه ) فاللون إنما يحدث في الجسم بالفعل عند حصول الضوء فيه ( وأنه ) أي اللون ) غير موجود في الظلمة لفقدان شرط وجوده حينئذ ( بل الجسم) في الظلمة ( مستعد لأن يحصل فيه عند الضوء اللون المعين فإنا لا نراه ) في الظلمة (فذلك) أي عدم رؤيتنا إياه إما لعدمه في نفسه ( أو لوجود العائق ) عن رؤيته ( وهو الهواء المظلم ) إذ لا عائق هناك سواه ) والثاني باطل لأنَّ الهواء) لمظلم (غير مانع من الإبصار فإن الجالس في غار مظلم يرى من في الخارج ) إذا أوقد ناراً وقع عليه ضوؤها ( والهواء الذي بينهما مع كونه مظلماً ( لا يعوق عن رؤيته) وكيف تكون الظلمة من الرؤية مع كونها أمراً عدمياً . ( والمشهور) فيما بين الجمهور ( وهو مختار عائقة قوله : (إذ لا عائق إلخ) فيه بحث أما أولاً فلان عدم العلم بعائق سواء لا يدل على عدمه في نفسه إلا أن يبنى الكلام على عدم التفاوت بين حال الرؤية وعدمها إلا بحصول الظلمة وأما ثانياً فلأنه يجوز أن يكون العائق الظلمة المحيطة بالمرئي كما قوله : ( وكيف تكون إلخ فيه أن الدليل على عدمية الظلمة كما سيجيء هو الذي أقيم أَنَّ سيجيء. قوله : ( الضوء شرط وجود اللون ومن هاهنا قالوا : إنَّ اللون لا يوجد في عمق الجسم بل هو قائم بالسطح لأن عمق الجسم ليس بمضيء وكل لون مضيء. قال الإمام في الملخص: لما قدحنا في الكبرى توقفنا في هذه المسألة وقد يقال : الحق في المسألة السابقة أن الظهور للبصر بالفعل إن أخذ داخلاً في مفهوم اللون مقوماً له فلا وجود لشيء من الألوان في الظلمة كما ذكره الشيخ، وإن لم يؤخذ داخلا فالضوء شرط في صحة كونه مرئيا لا في تحققه في نفسه كما ذهب إليه الإمام. وأنت خبير بأن جعل الظهور بالفعل للبصر مقوماً للون أمر مستبعد جداً وإلا لتأني مثله في الضوء فيلزم أن يكون ضوء الشيء بعد الغيبوبة عن الإبصار معدوماً، وكذا في سائر المحسوسات لسائر الحواس، فتأمل. :قوله : فذلك إما لعدمه إلخ ) انحصار سبب عدم الرؤية في الأمرين بعد تحقق القابلية الذاتية على ما هو كذلك فيما نحن فيه فلا يرد أن الهواء ليس بمرئي مع انتفاء الأمرين فيه . واعلم أن هذا الدليل يدل على بطلان ما أول به كلام القائلين بأن الضوء شرط وجود اللون من أن اللون يحصل بحصول آثار علوية من الأنوار والأضواء الكوكبية فإنَّ الأمزجة تابعة لحصول استعدادات فائضة من أجرام سماوية. وقلما يحدث في المركب من الأركان مزاج بدون تأثير الحرارة الشمسية. قوله : (مع كونها أمراً عدمياً ) يشير إلى أنَّ الاستدلال مبني على عدمية الظلمة، فلا يرد
The Third Observatory - Part One - Second Goal: Light is a Condition for the Existence of Color. (Ibn Sina and many of the philosophers said: Light is a condition for the existence of color) in itself. So color only occurs in the body actually when light obtains in it, (and that) i.e., color (does not exist in darkness due to the absence of the condition of its existence at that time. Rather, the body) in darkness (is disposed for the specific color to occur in it upon light. For we do not see it) in darkness. (So that) i.e., our not seeing it (is either due to its non-existence in itself or due to the presence of an obstacle) to its vision, (which is the dark air) (since there is no obstacle other than it. The second is false because the air) (dark) (does not prevent vision. For the one sitting in a dark cave sees someone outside) if he kindles a fire and its light falls upon him, (and the air between them despite being dark) (does not hinder his vision). And how can darkness be a hindrance to vision while it is a non-existent thing? (The well-known) among the common people (and he chose) ... his saying: ('There is no obstacle etc.') contains a discussion. Firstly, the lack of knowledge of an obstacle does not indicate its non-existence in itself, unless the discourse is built on the absence of difference between the state of vision and its absence except by the occurrence of darkness. Secondly, it is possible that the obstacle is the darkness surrounding the visible object. His saying: ('And how can it be etc.') contains that the proof for the non-existence of darkness, as will come, is that which established ... his saying: ('Light is a condition for the existence of color') and from here they said: Color does not exist in the depth of the body but rather it resides on the surface because the depth of the body is not illuminated, and every color is illuminated. Al-Imam said in al-Mulakhkhas: Since we criticized the major premise, we suspended judgment on this issue. It has been said: The truth in the previous issue is that actual visibility to the sight, if taken as intrinsic to the concept of color and constitutive of it, then no color exists in darkness as the shaykh mentioned. But if not taken as intrinsic, then light is a condition for the validity of its being visible, not for its actual existence, as al-Imam held. And you are aware that making actual visibility to the sight a constituent of color is very far-fetched; otherwise a similar thing would apply to light, so the light of a thing would be non-existent after it disappears from sight, and likewise for other sensed objects for other senses, so reflect. His saying: 'That is either due to its non-existence etc.' The confinement of the cause of non-vision to the two things after the fulfillment of essential capacity is as it is in our case. So it is not objected that air is not visible despite the absence of both things in it. And know that this proof indicates the invalidity of what those who say that light is a condition for the existence of color interpret it as meaning that color occurs by the occurrence of celestial effects from lights and stellar illuminations, because temperaments are subordinate to the occurrence of predispositions overflowing from celestial bodies. And rarely does a temperament occur in a composite of the elements without the influence of solar heat. His saying: ('While it is a non-existent thing') indicates that the argument is based on the non-existence of darkness, so it is not refuted ...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and defense of Ibn Sina's position that light is a condition for the existence of color itself, not merely for its visibility. The author states the core thesis and then gives the argument: if we see a body in the presence of light but not in darkness, the absence of vision is either because the color is not there (non-existence) or because there is an obstacle (dark air). The second option is ruled out by an example: a person in a dark cave can see another person outside when light is present; the dark air between them does not prevent vision. Therefore, the color itself must be absent in darkness. The author then discusses objections: first, that lack of knowledge of an obstacle does not prove its absence; second, that the darkness surrounding the object could be the obstacle. The author also includes a back-and-forth regarding the interpretation of 'light as condition' and the view of al-Imam (al-Razi) that light might only be a condition for visibility. The author finds making visibility intrinsic to color far-fetched. Finally, the author notes that the proof relies on the non-existence of darkness (i.e., darkness is a privation), and that this premise cannot be refuted.

Key Terms

شرط وجود
اللون
الظلمة
العائق
الهواء المظلم
عدم الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is only needed to see an already existing color, not for the color's own existence.
Correction: The argument shows that color itself does not exist in darkness; light is required for its actuality, not just for visibility.

Checkpoint
What does the cave example demonstrate?
A) That darkness is an obstacle to vision.
B) That dark air does not block vision when light is present.
C) That color can be seen without light.
D) That the body is invisible in darkness.

Key Takeaway
Light is a condition for the existence of color itself, not merely for its visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that color does not exist in darkness because the condition for its existence—light—is absent. In your spiritual life, what 'colors' (virtues, insights, actions) are missing because you are relying on your own darkness rather than seeking the true Light? What condition are you failing to fulfill?
?
The person in the dark cave could see only when a fire was kindled. What 'fire' have you been waiting for to see clearly in your life? What small step can you take today to bring that light into your own darkness, rather than passively waiting?
?
Ibn Sina proves that darkness is not an obstacle; it is simply the absence of something. Is there a darkness in your life—a sin, a doubt, a neglect—that you have treated as a fixed barrier? What would change if you saw it for what it really is: a lack of the condition that you can actively seek?

Responding to the Obstacle Objection
Al-Jurjani discusses two objections to Ibn Sina's argument and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections raised against Ibn Sina's argument and how they are addressed.

Core Idea
Lack of knowledge of an obstacle does not prove its absence, and darkness could be an obstacle surrounding the object.

After presenting Ibn Sina's core argument, Al-Jurjani anticipates objections. The first is that not knowing an obstacle exists does not mean it doesn't; the second is that the darkness surrounding the visible object might itself be the obstacle. In this lesson, we see how these points are critiqued and the response that the argument is based on the privative nature of darkness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two parts
First, note the two criticisms: (1) lack of knowledge of an obstacle doesn't mean there is no obstacle, and (2) the darkness around the object might be the obstacle. Understand the logic behind each.
2
Examine the assumption in the original argument
The author points out that Ibn Sina's argument assumes that there is no difference between the state of vision and non-vision except the presence of light. This assumption is what the objection challenges.
3
Consider the response about darkness being privative
The author responds that darkness is a non-existent thing (privation). This is crucial because a non-existent cannot be an obstacle. But the objection anticipates that the proof for darkness being privative is yet to come.
This shows the argument is not yet fully conclusive.

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and defense of Ibn Sina's position that light is a condition for the existence of color itself, not merely for its visibility. The author states the core thesis and then gives the argument: if we see a body in the presence of light but not in darkness, the absence of vision is either because the color is not there (non-existence) or because there is an obstacle (dark air). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Sina's argument is airtight without further premises.
Correction: The objections show that the argument depends on assuming that darkness is not an obstacle and that the only difference between seeing and not seeing is light. These need to be established.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection against Ibn Sina's argument?
A) The dark air is not an obstacle.
B) The obstacle might be the darkness surrounding the visible object.
C) The cave example is not valid.
D) Light is not a condition for existence.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument relies on the premise that darkness is a privation, which needs separate proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain of something in your faith, have you ever considered that your certainty might rely on an assumption you never examined? What would it cost you to honestly test that assumption today?
?
The objection says your lack of knowledge does not prove something does not exist. Where in your life have you dismissed a spiritual reality simply because you have not experienced it? Will you commit to seeking it with an open heart?
?
Ibn Sina's argument needed a separate proof for darkness as a privation. What unproven belief about yourself or God are you holding onto that might be a 'darkness' blocking you from deeper truth? Are you willing to let that belief be examined?

The Nature of the Condition: Existence vs. Visibility
Al-Jurjani analyzes whether light is a condition for existence or visibility, and examines al-Razi's position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether light is a condition for color's existence or only for its visibility, and the implications of making visibility intrinsic to color.

Core Idea
Making actual visibility a constituent of color is far-fetched and leads to absurd consequences.

The discussion deepens as Al-Jurjani considers al-Razi's view that light might only be a condition for visibility, not existence. He then argues that if visibility were intrinsic to color, then color would cease to exist when unseen, which is absurd. This lesson explores this critical distinction and its implications for the nature of color.

Guided Path
1
Read the two interpretations
First, understand the two possibilities: light is a condition for existence (Ibn Sina) or only for visibility (al-Razi). The author distinguishes between 'intrinsic to the concept' and 'not intrinsic'.
2
Examine the far-fetched consequence
If visibility is part of the definition of color, then color would cease to exist when not seen. The author shows this leads to the same problem for light and other sensible qualities.
3
Reflect on the final note
The passage ends by reaffirming that the proof relies on the privation of darkness. Understand how this ties back to the earlier objections.

المرصد الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : الضوء شرط وجود اللون ( قال ابن سينا وكثير من الحكماء الضوء شرط وجود اللون) في نفسه ) فاللون إنما يحدث في الجسم بالفعل عند حصول الضوء فيه ( وأنه ) أي اللون ) غير موجود في الظلمة لفقدان شرط وجوده حينئذ ( بل الجسم) في الظلمة ( مستعد لأن يحصل فيه عند الضوء اللون المعين فإنا لا نراه ) في الظلمة (فذلك) أي عدم رؤيتنا إياه إما لعدمه في نفسه ( أو لوجود العائق ) عن رؤيته ( وهو الهواء المظلم ) إذ لا عائق هناك سواه ) والثاني باطل لأنَّ الهواء) لمظلم (غير مانع من الإبصار فإن الجالس في غار مظلم يرى من في الخارج ) إذا أوقد ناراً وقع عليه ضوؤها ( والهواء الذي بينهما مع كونه مظلماً ( لا يعوق عن رؤيته) وكيف تكون الظلمة من الرؤية مع كونها أمراً عدمياً . ( والمشهور) فيما بين الجمهور ( وهو مختار عائقة قوله : (إذ لا عائق إلخ) فيه بحث أما أولاً فلان عدم العلم بعائق سواء لا يدل على عدمه في نفسه إلا أن يبنى الكلام على عدم التفاوت بين حال الرؤية وعدمها إلا بحصول الظلمة وأما ثانياً فلأنه يجوز أن يكون العائق الظلمة المحيطة بالمرئي كما قوله : ( وكيف تكون إلخ فيه أن الدليل على عدمية الظلمة كما سيجيء هو الذي أقيم أَنَّ سيجيء. قوله : ( الضوء شرط وجود اللون ومن هاهنا قالوا : إنَّ اللون لا يوجد في عمق الجسم بل هو قائم بالسطح لأن عمق الجسم ليس بمضيء وكل لون مضيء. قال الإمام في الملخص: لما قدحنا في الكبرى توقفنا في هذه المسألة وقد يقال : الحق في المسألة السابقة أن الظهور للبصر بالفعل إن أخذ داخلاً في مفهوم اللون مقوماً له فلا وجود لشيء من الألوان في الظلمة كما ذكره الشيخ، وإن لم يؤخذ داخلا فالضوء شرط في صحة كونه مرئيا لا في تحققه في نفسه كما ذهب إليه الإمام. وأنت خبير بأن جعل الظهور بالفعل للبصر مقوماً للون أمر مستبعد جداً وإلا لتأني مثله في الضوء فيلزم أن يكون ضوء الشيء بعد الغيبوبة عن الإبصار معدوماً، وكذا في سائر المحسوسات لسائر الحواس، فتأمل. :قوله : فذلك إما لعدمه إلخ ) انحصار سبب عدم الرؤية في الأمرين بعد تحقق القابلية الذاتية على ما هو كذلك فيما نحن فيه فلا يرد أن الهواء ليس بمرئي مع انتفاء الأمرين فيه . واعلم أن هذا الدليل يدل على بطلان ما أول به كلام القائلين بأن الضوء شرط وجود اللون من أن اللون يحصل بحصول آثار علوية من الأنوار والأضواء الكوكبية فإنَّ الأمزجة تابعة لحصول استعدادات فائضة من أجرام سماوية. وقلما يحدث في المركب من الأركان مزاج بدون تأثير الحرارة الشمسية. قوله : (مع كونها أمراً عدمياً ) يشير إلى أنَّ الاستدلال مبني على عدمية الظلمة، فلا يرد
The Third Observatory - Part One - Second Goal: Light is a Condition for the Existence of Color. (Ibn Sina and many of the philosophers said: Light is a condition for the existence of color) in itself. So color only occurs in the body actually when light obtains in it, (and that) i.e., color (does not exist in darkness due to the absence of the condition of its existence at that time. Rather, the body) in darkness (is disposed for the specific color to occur in it upon light. For we do not see it) in darkness. (So that) i.e., our not seeing it (is either due to its non-existence in itself or due to the presence of an obstacle) to its vision, (which is the dark air) (since there is no obstacle other than it. The second is false because the air) (dark) (does not prevent vision. For the one sitting in a dark cave sees someone outside) if he kindles a fire and its light falls upon him, (and the air between them despite being dark) (does not hinder his vision). And how can darkness be a hindrance to vision while it is a non-existent thing? (The well-known) among the common people (and he chose) ... his saying: ('There is no obstacle etc.') contains a discussion. Firstly, the lack of knowledge of an obstacle does not indicate its non-existence in itself, unless the discourse is built on the absence of difference between the state of vision and its absence except by the occurrence of darkness. Secondly, it is possible that the obstacle is the darkness surrounding the visible object. His saying: ('And how can it be etc.') contains that the proof for the non-existence of darkness, as will come, is that which established ... his saying: ('Light is a condition for the existence of color') and from here they said: Color does not exist in the depth of the body but rather it resides on the surface because the depth of the body is not illuminated, and every color is illuminated. Al-Imam said in al-Mulakhkhas: Since we criticized the major premise, we suspended judgment on this issue. It has been said: The truth in the previous issue is that actual visibility to the sight, if taken as intrinsic to the concept of color and constitutive of it, then no color exists in darkness as the shaykh mentioned. But if not taken as intrinsic, then light is a condition for the validity of its being visible, not for its actual existence, as al-Imam held. And you are aware that making actual visibility to the sight a constituent of color is very far-fetched; otherwise a similar thing would apply to light, so the light of a thing would be non-existent after it disappears from sight, and likewise for other sensed objects for other senses, so reflect. His saying: 'That is either due to its non-existence etc.' The confinement of the cause of non-vision to the two things after the fulfillment of essential capacity is as it is in our case. So it is not objected that air is not visible despite the absence of both things in it. And know that this proof indicates the invalidity of what those who say that light is a condition for the existence of color interpret it as meaning that color occurs by the occurrence of celestial effects from lights and stellar illuminations, because temperaments are subordinate to the occurrence of predispositions overflowing from celestial bodies. And rarely does a temperament occur in a composite of the elements without the influence of solar heat. His saying: ('While it is a non-existent thing') indicates that the argument is based on the non-existence of darkness, so it is not refuted ...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and defense of Ibn Sina's position that light is a condition for the existence of color itself, not merely for its visibility. The author states the core thesis and then gives the argument: if we see a body in the presence of light but not in darkness, the absence of vision is either because the color is not there (non-existence) or because there is an obstacle (dark air). The second option is ruled out by an example: a person in a dark cave can see another person outside when light is present; the dark air between them does not prevent vision. Therefore, the color itself must be absent in darkness. The author then discusses objections: first, that lack of knowledge of an obstacle does not prove its absence; second, that the darkness surrounding the object could be the obstacle. The author also includes a back-and-forth regarding the interpretation of 'light as condition' and the view of al-Imam (al-Razi) that light might only be a condition for visibility. The author finds making visibility intrinsic to color far-fetched. Finally, the author notes that the proof relies on the non-existence of darkness (i.e., darkness is a privation), and that this premise cannot be refuted.

Key Terms
الظهور للبصر
مقوم
شرط صحة الرؤية
القابلية
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi's view (light only for visibility) is the correct interpretation of Ibn Sina's argument.
Correction: The author shows that making visibility intrinsic to color is problematic, and the argument's reliance on darkness as privation supports Ibn Sina's position.

Checkpoint
What is the far-fetched consequence of making actual visibility intrinsic to the concept of color?
A) Colors would become brighter.
B) Light would cease to exist when not seen.
C) Darkness would become visible.
D) Colors would be permanent.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether light is condition for existence or visibility hinges on whether visibility is intrinsic to color; the latter option leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive colors, do you assume they are intrinsic properties of objects or dependent on conditions like light? What does this lesson reveal about your own assumptions regarding reality and appearance? Will you commit to questioning one thing you usually take for granted today?
?
The author argues that making visibility constitutive of color leads to absurd consequences. Are there beliefs you hold that, if logically examined, would lead to similar contradictions? What is one belief you are willing to scrutinize rigorously this week?
?
Consider the distinction between existence and visibility. In your spiritual life, are there 'realities' (like taqwa or sincerity) that exist but are not always visible? How can you cultivate such inner qualities without seeking external validation?

The Ancient Corpuscular Theory of Light and Its First Refutation
Sanusi presents the claim and first refutation with two branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient claim that light is composed of small particles and the first refutation that shows it contradicts sensory experience.

Core Idea
Light cannot be particles because either we wouldn't see it or it would hide what it touches.

Sanusi begins by presenting the ancient philosophers' view that light consists of tiny bodies emitted from luminous sources. He then offers a powerful refutation based on two logical possibilities: either these particles are perceptible or not. Each leads to a contradiction with observed reality. But a subtle objection arises from the behavior of transparent objects, which is also considered.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and refutation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the claim that light is small bodies and the two branches of the first refutation.
The refutation is presented as 'yubṭiluhu wajhān'.
2
Analyze the two branches
Note the two possibilities: the particles are insensible or sensible. Understand why each leads to a contradiction: if insensible, light wouldn't be seen; if sensible, they would conceal what is below, but the opposite is observed.
The argument relies on immediate sensory experience.
3
Consider the transparent object objection
The author notes a potential objection: concealment is a property of colored bodies, not transparent ones like glass, which actually reveal more.
This objection weakens the second branch but does not salvage the corporeal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : المبصرات في الأضواء من قسمي المبصرات ) في الأضواء وفيه مقاصد ) أربعة :
[ المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في الضوء ]
( زعم بعض الحكماء الأقدمين ( أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء
هي
وتتصل بالمستضيء ويبطله وجهان: الأول (أنها أي تلك الأجسام الصغار التي الضوء (إما غير محسوسة ) بالبصر فلا يكون الضوء حينئذ محسوساً له ( والضرورة تكذبه أو محسوسة فتستر ما تحتها فيكون الأكثر ضوءاً أكثر استتاراً والمشاهد عكسه) . فإن ما هو أكثر ضوءاً يكون أكثر ظهوراً ) وفيه نظر فإن ذلك ) أعني ستر الجسم المرئي ما تحته ( شأن الأجسام الملونة) فإنها تستر ما وراءها لعدم نفوذ شعاع البصر فيها ( دون ( الأجسام ( الشفافة ) التي ينفذ نور البصر فيها ويتصل بما وراءها ( فإن صفحة البلور) والزجاج الشفاف تزيد ما خلفها ظهوراً ولذلك يستعين بها الطاعنون في السن على قراءة الخطوط الدقيقة ) . وقد يجاب عنه : بأنه لو كان جسماً
قوله : ( تنفصل عن المضيء لا بد لهم من القول بتحددها في المضيء لئلا يلزم الانقطاع أو وجود الأجسام الصغار الغير المتناهية بالفعل في مثل الشمس وهو سفسطة لا سيما في الكيفيات لعدم قولهم بالكون والفساد فيها . قوله: (وتتصل بالمستضيء) من غير أن تداخله ولذا لا يستضيء عنه فيكون الجسم المستضيء مع الضوء أكبر مقدارا منه إذا لم يتصل به فما قيل لو كان الضوء جسماً يلزم التداخل أو ازدياد حجم الجسم القابل للضوء واللازم باطل ليس بشيء .
.
قوله : (إِنَّ الضوء (أجسام قد يقال : لو كان الضوء جسماً يلزم التداخل أو ازدياد حجم الجسم القابل للضوء واللازم بين الفساد كما لا يخفى فكذا الملزوم . قوله : ( ولذلك يستعين بها الطاعنون في السن) نقل عنه رحمه الله أن وجه الاستعانة إما أن تلك الخطوط النورية تصفو وتزول كدورتها عند نفوذها في الشفاف أو لأن الزاوية الحادثة عند الرطوبة الجليدية تكون حينئذ أعظم فيرى المرئي أعظم وفي شرح المقاصد ربما يستعان
بالحائل على إبصار الخطوط الدقيقة عند ضعف في الباصرة بحيث يحتاج إلى ما يجمع القوة . قوله : ( وقد يجاب عنه بأنه لو كان جسماً) قيل : لقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون لجسم الضوء خاصة الإظهار فيزداد الجسم المقابل ظهوراً عندما ازداد لتلك الخاصة أولا يرى أن
The second section: visible things in lights. From the two divisions of visible things (in lights) and it has four objectives:
[The first objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning light]
(Some ancient philosophers claimed) that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous body (and connect to the illuminated body). It is refuted by two aspects: The first (that those small bodies which are light (are either insensible) by sight, in which case light would then not be sensible to it (but necessity contradicts it), or they are sensible and then they would conceal what is beneath them, so the more light there is, the more concealment, but observation is the opposite. Indeed, what is more light is more apparent. And there is a consideration in that: that (i.e., the concealment of the visible body of what is beneath it) is the property of colored bodies, for they conceal what is behind them because the visual ray does not penetrate them, (unlike (transparent bodies) which the light of vision penetrates and connects to what is beyond them (indeed, a sheet of crystal) and transparent glass increases the appearance of what is behind them, and for that reason those advanced in age use them to read fine lines). And it may be answered: that if it were a body...
His saying: (separate from the luminous) they must necessarily hold that they are delimited in the luminous to avoid discontinuity or the existence of infinitely many small bodies actually in something like the sun, which is sophistry, especially regarding qualities, since they do not hold generation and corruption in them. His saying: (and connect to the illuminated) without entering into it, and therefore it does not become illuminated by it, so the illuminated body with the light would be greater in quantity than it if it does not connect to it. So what is said: if light were a body, interpenetration or increase in volume of the body receptive to light would follow, and the consequent is false, is nothing.
.
His saying: (that light is bodies) it may be said: if light were a body, interpenetration or increase in volume of the body receptive to light would follow, and the consequent is clearly false, so likewise the antecedent. His saying: (and for that reason those advanced in age use them) it is transmitted from him, may God have mercy on him, that the reason for using them is either that those linear lights become pure and their turbidity disappears when they penetrate the transparent, or because the angle formed at the crystalline humor then becomes greater, so the visible is seen larger. And in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, perhaps one uses a screen to see fine lines when there is weakness in the visual faculty such that it needs to collect the power. His saying: (and it may be answered that if it were a body) it is said: one may say: it is possible that the light body has the property of manifestation, so the opposite body increases in appearance when it increases due to that property. Do you not see that...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ancient philosophical claim that light is composed of small bodies (corpuscles). The commentator presents the claim and then gives the first refutation, which has two branches: either these particles are insensible (in which case light would not be perceived, which contradicts experience) or they are sensible (in which case they would conceal what is beneath them, yet we observe that more light makes things more visible, not less). The commentator then considers a potential objection ('nazar'): that the concealment of objects is a property of colored bodies, not transparent ones; transparent objects like crystal actually increase the visibility of what is behind them. This objection weakens the second branch of the refutation. Then the commentator mentions a possible answer: that if light were a body, certain consequences would follow (like interpenetration or increase in volume), which are false. Then follow several marginal notes (qāla) that elaborate on specific phrases: one discusses the necessity of delimiting the particles in the luminous source to avoid infinite regress; another addresses the connection to the illuminated body without interpenetration; a third repeats the objection about interpenetration; a fourth explains why old people use transparent objects to read fine lines; and a fifth returns to the answer, suggesting that light bodies might have a special property of manifestation. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Key Terms
أجسام صغار
مضيء
مستضيء
شفاف
شعاع البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first refutation, if light particles are sensible, what follows?
A) They would not be seen.
B) They would conceal what is beneath them, so more light means more concealment.
C) They would be transparent.
D) They would increase visibility.

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that the corpuscular theory fails because it contradicts immediate sensory experience.
