Further Arguments Against the Corpuscular Theory
Sanusi presents additional refutations and discusses a practical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional refutations against the corpuscular theory of light, including the problems of infinite regress and interpenetration.

Core Idea
If light were particles, it would require impossible interpenetration or an infinite number of particles.

After presenting the first refutation, Sanusi considers further objections that expose deeper logical flaws in the corpuscular theory. These include the necessity of delimiting particles in the luminous source to avoid infinite regress, and the impossibility of interpenetration without increasing volume. He also discusses a practical example involving transparent media used by the elderly to read, and a final suggestion about a possible special property of light bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional arguments
Read the remaining passage from 'wa qad yujāb' to the end. Identify the arguments about delimitation, infinite regress, and interpenetration.
This part contains several sub-arguments and responses.
2
Understand the delimitation argument
The particles must be delimited in the luminous source, otherwise there would be an infinite regress of particles, which is absurd.
This is a reduction to absurdity (safsata).
3
Analyze the interpenetration argument
If light particles connect to the illuminated body without interpenetration, the body's volume would increase, which does not happen. If they interpenetrate, that is also impossible.
The commentator refutes a potential counter-argument about this.
4
Note the explanation about transparent media
The use of transparent objects to read fine lines is explained by the purification of light rays or the enlargement of the visual angle.
This is a side note but relates to the nature of light.

The second section: visible things in lights. From the two divisions of visible things (in lights) and it has four objectives:
[The first objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning light]
(Some ancient philosophers claimed) that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous body (and connect to the illuminated body). It is refuted by two aspects: The first (that those small bodies which are light (are either insensible) by sight, in which case light would then not be sensible to it (but necessity contradicts it), or they are sensible and then they would conceal what is beneath them, so the more light there is, the more concealment, but observation is the opposite. And it may be answered: that if it were a body...

His saying: (and connect to the illuminated) without entering into it, and therefore it does not become illuminated by it, so the illuminated body with the light would be greater in quantity than it if it does not connect to it. So what is said: if light were a body, interpenetration or increase in volume of the body receptive to light would follow, and the consequent is false, is nothing.
.
His saying: (that light is bodies) it may be said: if light were a body, interpenetration or increase in volume of the body receptive to light would follow, and the consequent is clearly false, so likewise the antecedent. His saying: (and for that reason those advanced in age use them) it is transmitted from him, may God have mercy on him, that the reason for using them is either that those linear lights become pure and their turbidity disappears when they penetrate the transparent, or because the angle formed at the crystalline humor then becomes greater, so the visible is seen larger. And in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, perhaps one uses a screen to see fine lines when there is weakness in the visual faculty such that it needs to collect the power. His saying: (and it may be answered that if it were a body) it is said: one may say: it is possible that the light body has the property of manifestation, so the opposite body increases in appearance when it increases due to that property. Do you not see that...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ancient philosophical claim that light is composed of small bodies (corpuscles). The commentator presents the claim and then gives the first refutation, which has two branches: either these particles are insensible (in which case light would not be perceived, which contradicts experience) or they are sensible (in which case they would conceal what is beneath them, yet we observe that more light makes things more visible, not less). The commentator then considers a potential objection ('nazar'): that the concealment of objects is a property of colored bodies, not transparent ones; transparent objects like crystal actually increase the visibility of what is behind them. This objection weakens the second branch of the refutation. Then the commentator mentions a possible answer: that if light were a body, certain consequences would follow (like interpenetration or increase in volume), which are false. Then follow several marginal notes (qāla) that elaborate on specific phrases: one discusses the necessity of delimiting the particles in the luminous source to avoid infinite regress; another addresses the connection to the illuminated body without interpenetration; a third repeats the objection about interpenetration; a fourth explains why old people use transparent objects to read fine lines; and a fifth returns to the answer, suggesting that light bodies might have a special property of manifestation. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Key Terms
تداخل
سفسطة
المضيء
المستضيء
الشفاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the interpenetration argument is easily dismissed by assuming light can interpenetrate matter.
Correction: The passage argues that interpenetration of bodies is impossible in the natural order, so this escape is not valid.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator argue about the volume of an illuminated body if light is a body that connects without interpenetration?
A) It remains the same.
B) It increases.
C) It decreases.
D) It becomes transparent.

Key Takeaway
The corpuscular theory leads to impossible consequences like infinite regress or interpenetration, confirming its falsity.

The First Refutation of Light as Small Bodies
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the ancient philosophers' view of light as small bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Jurjani's first refutation against the ancient philosophers' view of light as small bodies, including the dilemma, a counter-objection, and its resolution.

Core Idea
Light cannot be small bodies because more light would then mean more covering, not more visibility.

Al-Jurjani begins his critique of the ancient atomist conception of light by presenting a clear dilemma. He challenges the claim that light consists of tiny particles by testing its logical consequences. This short passage introduces the first of two refutations, but it also includes an objection and a reply, showing the dialectical sophistication of kalam reasoning. We will walk through each step to see how the argument holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the first sentence to understand what the ancient philosophers claimed about light. They said light is composed of small bodies that separate from the luminous object and attach to the illuminated one.
2
Follow the dilemma
The refutation presents two possibilities: either these particles are imperceptible or perceptible. Notice the consequence of each. The first leads to contradiction with experience, the second leads to an opposite observation.
The key is the observation that more light makes objects more visible, not covered.
3
Examine the objection
After the initial argument, al-Jurjani raises a potential objection (fīhi nazar). The objection says that covering is a property of opaque bodies, not transparent ones like glass, which actually enhance visibility.
This objection seems to undermine the refutation by showing a counterexample.
4
Understand the answer
Al-Jurjani provides a reply: even if light were a body, it could have a special property (khāṣṣiyyat al-iẓhār) that makes objects appear more. This preserves the original refutation because light's behavior is not like ordinary bodies.
The answer shows that the objection does not defeat the dilemma; rather, it forces a refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعم بعض الحكماء الأقدمين أن الضوء أجسام صغار تنفصل من المضيء وتتصل بالمستضيء ويبطله وجهان: الأول: أنها إما غير محسوسة بالبصر فلا يكون الضوء حينئذ محسوساً له والضرورة تكذبه، أو محسوسة فتستر ما تحتها فيكون الأكثر ضوءاً أكثر استتاراً والمشاهد عكسه. وفيه نظر: فإن ذلك أعني ستر الجسم المرئي ما تحته شأن الأجسام الملونة فإنها تستر ما وراءها لعدم نفوذ شعاع البصر فيها دون الأجسام الشفافة التي ينفذ نور البصر فيها ويتصل بما وراءها فإن صفحة البلور والزجاج الشفاف تزيد ما خلفها ظهوراً ولذلك يستعين بها الطاعنون في السن على قراءة الخطوط الدقيقة. وقد يجاب عنه: بأنه لو كان جسماً لجاز أن يكون له خاصة الإظهار فيزداد الجسم المقابل ظهوراً عندما ازداد لتلك الخاصة.
Some of the ancient philosophers claimed that light is small bodies that separate from the luminous object and connect to the illuminated object. It is refuted by two aspects: The first: that they (i.e., those small bodies that are light) are either imperceptible to the eye, in which case light would not then be perceptible to it, but necessity belies that; or they are perceptible and thus cover what is beneath them, so that the more light there is, the more covering there is, but the observed is the opposite, for what has more light is more apparent. There is a consideration in this: for that, namely the covering of the visible body of what is beneath it, is the property of colored bodies, for they cover what is behind them due to the non-penetration of the visual ray into them, unlike transparent bodies, into which the light of vision penetrates and connects to what is behind them, for a sheet of crystal and transparent glass increase the appearance of what is behind them, and therefore the elderly use them to read fine lines. And it may be answered: that if it were a body, it could have the property of making appear, so that the opposite body would increase in appearance when it increases due to that property.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the first refutation (wajh) against the ancient philosophers' claim that light is composed of small particles that separate from the luminous source and attach to the illuminated object. The argument is structured as a dilemma: either these particles are imperceptible to the eye, which contradicts our direct experience of light as visible, or they are perceptible and thus would cover what lies beneath them. If the latter, then more light would mean more covering, but we observe the opposite: brighter light makes objects more visible. The author then raises an objection (fīhi nazar): covering is a property of opaque, colored bodies, not of transparent ones like crystal or glass, which actually enhance visibility. The elderly use transparent sheets to read fine print, showing that transparent bodies do not cover but rather reveal. The answer to this objection is then given: light, if it were a body, could have a special property (khāṣṣiyyat al-iẓhār) that makes objects appear more, just as transparent bodies have the property of making what is behind them visible. This preserves the refutation because it allows light to be a body with an inherent quality of illumination rather than covering.

Key Terms
ضوء (dawʾ) – light
أجسام صغار (ajsām ṣighār) – small bodies
محسوس (maḥsūs) – perceptible
شفاف (shaffāf) – transparent
خاصية الإظهار (khāṣṣiyyat al-iẓhār) – property of making appear

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about transparent bodies completely refutes the original argument.
Correction: The answer shows that light could have a special property that allows it to be a body without causing covering, so the dilemma still stands.

Checkpoint
According to the first refutation, what is the consequence if light particles are perceptible?
A) They would be imperceptible to the eye
B) They would need to be transparent like glass
C) More light would cause more covering, contrary to observation
D) They would have no effect on visibility

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani's first refutation uses a dilemma to show that light cannot be particles, and even after an objection, it remains valid by allowing light to have a special property of illumination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something clearly in bright light, do you ever stop to doubt your own senses? The ancient philosophers proposed a theory that sounded logical but contradicted daily experience. What beliefs do you hold that seem logical but might crumble when tested against clear observation?
?
The objection in the passage shows that we must consider the specific properties of things, not just general categories. Have you ever assumed something about a person or situation based on a general label, only to miss the unique qualities? How can you apply the lesson's careful reasoning to your own judgments?
?
Al-Jurjani's refutation relies on a dilemma: either particles are imperceptible (absurd) or perceptible (contradicts observation). This forces a conclusion. Are there areas in your life where you avoid facing such dilemmas that would demand a change? What would it cost you to honestly confront a belief you hold?

The Second Refutation: Light Cannot Have Natural Motion
Al-Jurjani presents the second refutation based on the direction of light's motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that light cannot be a body because its motion would be natural and unidirectional, contradicting observed multidirectional light.

Core Idea
Natural motion implies one direction; light comes from all directions — hence light is not a body.

This lesson presents al-Jurjani's second refutation of the corporeality of light. It focuses on the nature of motion: if light were a body, its motion would be natural (since it has no will or external force), and natural motion always has a single direction. But light is observed to come from multiple directions, contradicting that. The passage also addresses an objection regarding light's transparency.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: premise, consequence, observed falsity.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument assumes that if light were a body, it would have no will or external compeller, so motion must be natural. This is a key premise.
Natural motion means movement toward the body's natural place.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says light might be transparent like celestial spheres. The answer clarifies that transparency does not override the rule of natural direction.
This shows the depth of the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that light cannot be a body because observed incidence from multiple directions contradicts the necessary unidirectionality of natural motion.
Think about why this matters for the nature of light.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان الضوء جسماً لكان حركته بالطبع إذ لا إرادة له قطعاً ولا قاسر يقسره أيضاً فكانت حركته الطبيعية إلى جهة واحدة فلم يقع الضوء من كل جهة بل من جهة واحدة فقط. والتالي باطل لأن الضوء يقع على الأجسام من جهات متعددة مختلفة. واعترض عليه: بأنه لو كان جسماً محسوساً لكان الأمر كما ذكر، لكنه جسماً شفافاً كالأفلاك لا يشتغل الحس به. وأجيب: بأن الشفافية لا تمنع اختصاص كل جسم بحيز طبيعي واحد، فالحركة الطبيعية تقتضي جهة واحدة، والضوء يقع من جهات متعددة، فلا يكون جسماً.
The second: if light were a body, its motion would be by nature, for it certainly has no will nor any compeller compelling it, so its natural motion would be to one direction, so light would not occur from every direction but from only one direction. The consequent is false, because light falls on bodies from multiple different directions. And it was objected: that if it were a perceptible body, the matter would be as mentioned, but it is a transparent body like the celestial spheres, and the sense does not occupy itself with it. And it was answered: transparency does not prevent each body from having a single natural place, so natural motion requires one direction, but light comes from multiple directions, so it cannot be a body.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation of the view that light is a body. The argument is as follows: if light were a body, it would have natural motion (since it has no will and no external compeller to give it voluntary or forced motion). Natural motion for any body is towards a single natural place (ḥīz ṭabīʿī), so light would move only in that direction. Consequently, light would only come from one direction, but we observe that light reaches bodies from many different directions (e.g., sunlight fills a room from various angles). Therefore, light cannot be a body. An objection is raised: light might be a transparent body like the celestial spheres (al-aflāk), which are not perceived by the senses and do not behave as ordinary bodies. The answer to this objection is that transparency does not negate the fact that every body has a single natural place; natural motion is still directed to that one place. Even if light were transparent, it would still have a natural direction, contradicting the observed multidirectional incidence of light. Thus, the refutation stands.

Key Terms
حركة طبيعية (ḥaraka ṭabīʿiyya)
جهة (jiha)
حيز طبيعي (ḥīz ṭabīʿī)
شفاف (shaffāf)
أفلاك (aflāk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that light's transparency exempts it from the laws of natural motion.
Correction: Transparency does not abolish natural direction; all bodies have a natural place, and their motion is toward that place.

Checkpoint
Why does the second refutation conclude that light cannot be a body?
A) Because light is not perceptible
B) Because light's motion would be natural and to one direction, but it comes from many directions
C) Because light has will
D) Because light is transparent

Key Takeaway
Because natural motion is unidirectional and light is observed multidirectional, light cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe light every day, coming from the sun, lamps, and screens. This lesson argues that light cannot be a body because natural motion would restrict it to one direction. How does this insight into the non-corporeal nature of light change the way you see the physical world around you? Are you too quick to assume that everything you perceive is material? Challenge yourself: the next time you see light, pause and remember that it is a sign of the Creator who brought forth something from nothing—something that defies the ordinary laws of bodies. Will you let this awareness deepen your awe of Allah?
?
Al-Jurjani answers an objection by stating that transparency does not negate having a single natural place. Reflect on an area of your life where you have made exceptions for yourself, thinking that your situation is 'transparent'—somehow exempt from the principles that apply to others. In what way do you rationalize your own disobedience or negligence? This lesson demands that you examine those excuses: is there a principle of truth that you are ignoring? Commit to identifying one such excuse today and replace it with honesty.
?
If light, which illuminates everything for you, itself has no will, no compeller, and its movement is simply part of the natural order decreed by Allah, then what about your own will? Your voluntary motions are a gift that light doesn't have. How are you using this gift of will? Light passively follows the laws placed upon it, but you can choose to orient yourself toward your Creator. Are you actively directing your life's motion toward Allah, or are you drifting? Resolve to make one intentional act of worship today that comes purely from your will.

Light Does Not Move: Refuting the Three Proofs
Refutes three proofs that light is a body and the shadow objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent motion of light is illusory and that light is created instantaneously, not through locomotion.

Core Idea
Light appears to move, but it is actually re-created at each moment.

The philosophers argued that light must be a body because it seems to move—reflecting, descending, and shifting. In this passage, al-Ījī refutes each of these grounds, showing that what we perceive as motion is merely the instantaneous creation of light in a new location when alignment changes. He also strengthens his point with the example of a shadow, which moves yet is agreed not to be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: it lists three proofs, then refutes them, then adds the shadow objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find where the philosophers argue that light is a body because it moves: reflection, descent from a high source, and transfer when alignment changes. Write them down.
These are listed at the beginning.
3
Understand the refutation
The author denies that light has any motion. He explains the illusion: light is created instantaneously in the opposite body when facing a luminous source. When alignment shifts, light vanishes from the first and is created anew in the second.
The key is that there is no physical movement through space.
4
Examine the shadow objection
The author brings up the shadow as a counterexample: it moves but is not a body. If they claim shadow doesn't really move but is re-created, then the same applies to light.
This strengthens the argument by analogy.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: if light were a body, it would have to be composed of particles and move through space. The refutation shows that light is a quality (accident) that appears and disappears based on alignment.
This aligns with the Islamic theological view that only God creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : قول الحكماء في الضوء والمصباح ( وينعكس ) الضوء ( عما يلقاه ) إذا كان صقيلاً إلى جسم آخر والانعكاس حركة فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة أن الضوء متحرك ( وكل متحرك جسم قلنا : ( ليس للضوء حركة أصلاً بل ( حركته وهم (محض) وتخيل باطل ( و ) سبب (ذلك ) التوهم حدوثه في المقابل) أي حدوث الضوء في القابل المقابل للمضيء فيتوهم أنه تحرك منه ووصل إلى المقابل ( ولما كان حدوثه فيه (من) مقابلة مضيء ( عال) كالشمس مثلاً ( تخيل أنه ينحدر ) من العالي إلى السافل وهو باطل إذ لو كان منحدراً لرأيناه في وسط المسافة. فالصواب إذن أنه يحدث في القابل المقابل دفعة ) ولما كان حدوثه) في الجسم القابل ( تابعاً للوضع من المضيء ) أي لوضعه منه ومحاذاته إياه فإذا زالت تلك المحاذاة إلى قابل آخر زال الضوء عن الأول وحدث في ذلك الآخر (ظن أنه يتبعه في الحركة) وينتقل من الجسم الأول إلى الجسم الاخر ( ولما كان ( الضوء يحدث في مقابلة المستضيء الذي وقع عليه الضوء من غيره كما يحدث في مقابلة المضيء بذاته ( والمتوسط) الذي هو هذا المستضيء بالغير (شرط في حدوثه أي في حدوث الضوء فيما يقابل هذا المستضيء أعني الجسم الذي انعكس إليه الضوء ( ظن أنَّ ثمة انتقالاً ) وحركة للضوء من المستضيء إلى المنعكس إليه فظهر بطلان الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكروها في حركة الضوء ( ويرد) أيضا ( عليهم الظل ) نقضاً على أصل دليلهم فإنه متحرك ومنتقل بانتقال صاحبه ( مع الاتفاق على أنه ليس جسماً. فإن أجابوا بأنه لا حركة له بل يزول عن موضع ويحدث في آخر على حسب تجدد المحاذيات قلنا : كذلك الحال في الضوء أيضا. قوله : ( إذ لو كان منحدراً إلخ) يعني لا دليل على انحداره إلا الحس ولو كان كذلك لرأيناه في وسط المسافة . قوله : ( لرأيناه في وسط المسافة فيه أنَّ عدم الرؤية يجوز أن يكون للطافة لحظة الحركة في الغاية . قوله : ( فإذا زالت إلخ جملة معترضة فاعلم؛ فعلم المرء ينفعه . قوله : (زال الضوء عن الأول وحدث في ذلك الآخر قيل : هذا الضوء يشاهد استمراره فلو جوز أنه ينتفي ويوجد بدله أنا فأنا لجاز مثل ذلك في الجسم المتحرك بعينه إذ لا فرق بينهما في ذلك عند بديهة العقل .
The Third Observatory - Section Two - First Aim: The statement of the philosophers about light and the lamp. (And it reflects) light (from what it encounters) if it is polished, to another body, and reflection is motion. So it is established by these three aspects that light is moving, (and every moving thing is a body). We said: (Light has no motion at all, but (its motion is pure imagination and false illusion). (And) the cause (of that) imagination is its occurrence in the opposite [body] – that is, the occurrence of light in the recipient opposite the luminous body, so one imagines that it moved from it and reached the opposite. (And since its occurrence in it [is due to] facing a luminous [body] high like the sun, for example, (one imagined that it descends) from high to low, and it is false, for if it were descending we would see it in the middle of the distance. So the correct view is that it occurs in the opposite recipient instantaneously.) (And since its occurrence) in the recipient body (is dependent on the position relative to the luminous body) – i.e., its position relative to it and its alignment with it – when that alignment shifts to another recipient, the light leaves the first and occurs in that other, (one supposes that it follows it in motion) and transfers from the first body to the other body. (And since (light occurs in facing the illuminated [body] which received light from another, as it occurs in facing the luminous in itself, and the intermediate) which is this illuminated-by-another (is a condition for its occurrence – i.e., for the occurrence of light in what faces this illuminated [body], namely the body to which light is reflected – (one supposes that there is a transfer) and motion of light from the illuminated to the reflected-to. So the falsity of the three aspects they mentioned concerning the motion of light became clear. (And also (the shadow) is brought against them) as a refutation of the basis of their proof, for it moves and transfers with the transfer of its owner, (with agreement that it is not a body). If they answer that it has no motion but rather vanishes from a place and occurs in another according to the renewal of alignments, we say: The same is the case with light as well. His saying: (If it were descending etc.) – meaning there is no proof of its descent except sense perception, and if it were so we would see it in the middle of the distance. His saying: (We would see it in the middle of the distance) – in it [is the response] that the absence of sight could be due to the subtlety of the moment of motion to the utmost. His saying: (When that alignment shifts etc.) is a parenthetical sentence – so know; for a person's knowledge benefits him. His saying: (The light leaves the first and occurs in that other) – it is said: This light is observed to be continuous, so if one allows that it ceases and its substitute comes into existence, then the same would be permissible for the moving body itself, for there is no difference between them in that according to the necessity of the intellect.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' claim that light is a body because it appears to move. It begins by listing three grounds for their claim: reflection as motion, apparent descent from a high source, and apparent transfer when the alignment changes. The refutation argues that light does not actually move; rather, it is created instantaneously in the opposite body at the moment of alignment. The illusion of motion arises because when the alignment shifts, light vanishes from one body and is created anew in another, but there is no locomotion. The passage also addresses the objection regarding shadow: shadow moves yet is not a body. The response is that shadow does not really move; it too vanishes and is re-created with changing alignments. The included glosses clarify specific points: the descent argument is based on sense but could be too fast to observe; the continuity of light is an illusion; and the shadow analogy is consistent. The lesson emphasizes that motion is not a necessary sign of corporeality.

Key Terms
الضوء
الجسم
الحركة
الانعكاس
الظل
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light moves from one place to another like a body.
Correction: Light does not move; it is created instantaneously in a new location when alignment changes, and vanishes from the previous location.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does light appear to move when alignment changes?
A) Light moves gradually through the air.
B) Light is created instantaneously in the new location and vanishes from the old one.
C) Light travels at an extremely high speed.
D) Light is a body that translates through space.

Key Takeaway
Light is not a body; its apparent motion is an illusion caused by instantaneous creation and destruction with changing alignments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that light, which you see every day, does not actually move – it appears to because it is created and destroyed each instant. If your most basic perception of a simple physical phenomenon can be so deceptive, how much more are your perceptions about your own self, your good deeds, and your faith subject to illusion? Will you trust your own judgment about your spiritual state without returning to the One who creates all things at every moment?
?
The philosophers assumed motion proves corporeality, and the author showed that assumption is false. Where in your life have you assumed that visible changes (e.g., in your character, your relationships) prove something permanent about you or others? How might that assumption be mistaken, and what would it mean to accept that everything is re-created by Allah each moment?
?
If light is not a moving body but is constantly re-created, then your own existence is at every instant a direct act of Allah's creation. Are you living as though you are self-sustaining, or do you feel the weight of your absolute dependence on Him every second? What will you do today to remind yourself that you are being created anew each moment?

Light as Degrees of Color Appearance
Presents the theory that light is degrees of color appearance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory that light is not a separate quality but merely the degrees of appearance of color, and how its proponents defend it against objections.

Core Idea
Light is just color appearing fully, not an added quality.

Some scholars who accept that light is a quality in a body went further to identify light with the degree of color appearance. This theory, presented in the text, claims that light, darkness, and shadow are just varying degrees of the same color's appearance. The following passage explains this view and addresses a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the main idea.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify how the theory defines light, darkness, and shadow as degrees of appearance.
Notice that absolute appearance is light, absolute concealment is darkness, and intermediate is shadow.
3
Examine the objection and response
Notice the objection that we perceive a difference between illuminated and dark colors, and see how the theory responds by attributing it to degree of appearance, not an extra quality.
4
Reflect on the extreme claim
Consider the claim about sunlight and why it dazzles. Think about its implications for the theory.
The extreme claim uses sunlight as an example where extreme appearance hides the color itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فرع) على بطلان كون الضوء جسماً ( من المعترفين بأنه أي الضوء ليس جسماً. بل هو ( كيفية) في الجسم ( من قال : هو مراتب ظهور اللون ( وادعى أن الظهور المطلق هو قوله : ( وادعى أن الظهور المطلق إلخ بيان لمراتب ظهور اللون والمراد بالظهور المطلق هو الفرد الكامل . الضوء والخفاء المطلق هو الظلمة والمتوسط بينهما هو الظل وتختلف مراتبه بحسب القرب والبعد من الطرفين فإذا ألف الحس مرتبة من تلك المراتب ثم شاهد ما هو أكثر ظهوراً من الأول يحسب أنَّ هناك بريقاً ولمعاناً وليس الأمر كذلك بل ليس هناك كيفية زائدة على اللون الذي ظهر ظهوراً أتم فالضوء هو اللون الظاهر على مراتب مختلفة لا كيفية موجودة زائدة عليه فإن أورد عليه أنا ندرك التفرقة بين اللون المستنير وبين اللون المظلم . قالوا : إِنَّ ذلك بسبب أنَّ أحدهما خفي والآخر ظاهر لا بسبب كيفية أخرى موجودة مع المستنير وقد بالغ بعضهم في ذلك حتى قال : إِنَّ ضوء الشمس ليس إلا الظهور التام للونه ولما اشتد ظهوره ومبلغ الغاية في ذلك بهر الأبصار حتى خفي اللون لا لخفائه في نفسه بل لعجز البصر عن إدراك ما هو جلي في الغاية هذا تقرير مذهبهم
(Branch) upon the falsity of light being a body: from those who acknowledge that light is not a body, but rather it is a quality in the body, (some said: it is the degrees of appearance of color), and claimed that absolute appearance is... His saying: (And he claimed that absolute appearance etc.) – an explanation of the degrees of appearance of color; and what is meant by absolute appearance is the perfect individual. Light, and absolute concealment is darkness, and the intermediate between them is shadow, and its degrees vary according to nearness and distance from the two extremes. When the sense becomes accustomed to a degree of those degrees, then observes what is more apparent than the first, it thinks that there is brilliance and sparkle, but the matter is not so; rather, there is no additional quality beyond the color that has appeared more completely. So light is the color that appears in different degrees, not an existent quality additional to it. If it is objected that we perceive a difference between illuminated color and dark color, they say: That is because one is hidden and the other is apparent, not because of another quality existing with the illuminated. And some of them went to extremes in that until they said: The light of the sun is nothing but the complete appearance of its color, and when its appearance intensified to the utmost degree in that, it dazzled the eyes so that the color became hidden – not because of its own hiddenness, but because of the inability of the sight to perceive what is extremely clear. This is the exposition of their doctrine.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a theory about light held by some scholars who acknowledge light is a quality but define it specifically as the degrees of appearance of color. They argue that light is not a separate quality; rather, it is the color itself appearing to varying degrees. Absolute appearance is light, absolute concealment is darkness, and shadow is intermediate. When the eye observes a brighter color, it mistakenly thinks there is an added brilliance, but in reality it is just the same color appearing more fully. They address the objection that we distinguish between illuminated and dark colors by saying this distinction is due to one being apparent and the other hidden, not due to an extra quality. Some even claim that sunlight is just the complete appearance of the sun's color, and its extreme brightness overwhelms the eye so that the color itself is not perceived. The gloss clarifies that 'absolute appearance' means the perfect instance (al-fard al-kāmil). The passage ends by stating this is their doctrine, setting the stage for refutation.

Correction: This theory denies that; light is the color itself appearing more fully, not an added quality.

B) The color itself appearing in different degrees.
C) A body that illuminates.
D) A non-existent illusion.

Key Takeaway
The theory identifies light with the degree of color appearance, denying any additional quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something in your life that you consider 'brilliant' or 'special'—a talent, a possession, or a person. Do you treat it as if it has an added quality separate from its essence? How can you learn to see it as simply a fuller manifestation of its own nature, and what would change in your attachment to it?
?
When you experience something overwhelming—like beauty, awe, or clarity—do you assume it must come from something external and extraordinary? The passage suggests extreme clarity can dazzle us into missing the simple reality. What is one 'dazzling' thing in your life that might actually be a clear truth you are not ready to face? Will you sit with it today without adding your own interpretations?
?
The theory argues that we mistake degrees of appearance for separate qualities. Where in your spiritual life do you confuse intensity of feeling with presence of truth? Are you chasing 'brilliance' of experiences rather than the simple, consistent appearance of faith in your daily actions? Commit to one action that reflects steady appearance over seeking dramatic light.

Refuting the Identity of Light and Color Appearance
Refutes the identity of light and color appearance; presents al-Razi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments against the theory that light is identical to the appearance of color, and how al-Razi clarifies the debate by distinguishing ontological from verbal disputes.

Core Idea
Light is not the color's appearance; their natures differ, and the debate may be merely verbal.

This passage presents two crisp arguments that dismantle the claim that light is nothing but the appearance of color, followed by an objection and its rebuttal. The climax comes with Imam al-Razi's incisive analysis, which shows that only one interpretation of the theory is substantive—the rest reduce to either falsity or a mere verbal quibble. The discussion demonstrates how precise conceptual distinctions can transform a metaphysical dispute into a matter of definition.

Guided Path
1
First refutation argument
Read the first argument: light is a changing phenomenon (mutajaddid), while color is permanent (mustamir). Since two things that differ in this fundamental property cannot be identical, light cannot be the color itself.
Note the key contrast: light varies in degrees (shadda), color persists.
2
Second refutation argument
Read the second argument: light is common to all colors (black, white, etc.), yet colors differ in essence (mahiyya). If light were identical to any particular color, it could not belong to essentially different colors. Therefore, light is not the color itself.
This argument uses the principle that identical things share all properties; here, light's universality clashes with color's diversity.
3
Objection and response
The opponent might say the changing thing is a new color (lun yahduth) rather than something additional. The response shows this would entail colors constantly renewing with light intensity—either successively or simultaneously—both of which the opponent rejects.
This strengthens the original refutation by blocking a plausible escape.
4
Al-Razi's three interpretations
Read al-Razi's analysis: he identifies three ways to interpret 'light is the appearance of color.' (1) If it means light is an additional quality that causes appearance, it's a verbal dispute. (2) If it means light is a relative state (the color's appearing to sense), it's false because light is non-relative. (3) If it means light is the renewed color itself, then their statement has no validity.
Focus on why interpretation (2) is decisively false: light is directly perceived as an absolute entity, not a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطله أنه أي القائل اعترف أنَّ ثمة أمراً متجدداً على اختلاف مراتبه عبر عنه بالظهور وسماه ضوءاً فلا يكون الضوء الذي هو هذا المتجدد نفس اللون لكونه أمراً مستمراً فبطل مذهبه لهذا ( ولأنه أعني الضوء مشترك بين الألوان كلها فإنَّ السواد والبياض وغيرها قد تكون مضيئة مشرقة ولا شك أنها غير مشاركة في الماهية ، بل متخالفة فيها فلا يكون الضوء نفسها . ( وفيهما ) أي في هذين الوجهين المبطلين لمذهبهم ( نظر، إذ ربما يقول ذلك القائل : الأمر المتجدد الذي اعترفت به لون يحدث فلا يكون الضوء زائداً على اللون وفيه بحث إذ يلزمه حينئذ تجدد الألوان بحسب اشتداد الضوء شيئاً فشيئاً، سواء كانت متعاقبة في الوجود أو مجتمعة في المحل، وكلاهما باطل عندهم. قال الإمام الرازي : هؤلاء الذين قالوا : الضوء ظهور اللون إن جعلوا الضوء كيفية زائدة على ذات اللون وسموه بالظهور لأنه سبب له فذلك نزاع لفظي، وإن زعموا أن ذلك الظهور تجدد حالة نسبية أعني ظهور اللون عند الحس فهذا باطل لأنَّ الضوء أمر غير نسبي فلا تفسيره بالحالة النسبية وإن جعلوه عبارة عن اللون المتجدد فلا يكون لقولهم : الضوء يصح الأمور قوله : ( هو اللون الظاهر) مقتضى ما سبق أن يقول : فالضوء هو ظهور اللون لكنه نبه على أن مرادهم بمراتب ظهور اللون اللون الظاهر على مراتب . قوله : ( ويبطله أنه اعترف إلخ ) الظاهر أنه معارضة لكن أتى بها قبل الانتهاض بالدليل. قوله : ( لأن الضوء أمر غير نسبي لأنا نرى الضوء بيقين أولاً بالذات ولو كان من النسبية لم يكن مرئياً كذلك . قوله : ( فلا يكون لقولهم إلخ) لا يخفى أن مثل هذه المسامحات شائعة إذ حمل ظهور
And it refutes it: that he, i.e., the speaker, acknowledged that there is a changing thing in its varying degrees, which he expressed by 'appearance' and called it light, so light which is this changing thing cannot be the color itself because it (color) is a permanent thing; thus his doctrine is refuted by this. (And because it, i.e., light, is common among all colors – for black, white, and others may be illuminated and bright, and there is no doubt that they do not share in the essence, but are different in it – so light cannot be they themselves. (And in them) i.e., in these two refuting aspects of their doctrine, (there is consideration. For perhaps that speaker says: The changing thing which I acknowledged is a color that occurs, so light is not additional to color. And in it there is discussion, for then it would entail the renewal of colors according to the intensification of light gradually, whether they be successive in existence or simultaneous in the locus, and both are invalid according to them. Imam al-Razi said: Those who said that light is the appearance of color – if they make light an additional quality upon the essence of color and call it 'appearance' because it is the cause of it, then that is a verbal dispute. And if they claim that that appearance is the renewal of a relative state, i.e., the appearance of color to the sense, then this is false because light is a non-relative thing, so it cannot be interpreted as a relative state. And if they make it an expression for the renewed color, then there is no validity to their saying: light... [?] matters. His saying: (It is the apparent color) – the implication of what preceded is that he should say: light is the appearance of color, but he pointed out that what they mean by degrees of appearance of color is the apparent color in degrees. His saying: (And it refutes it that he acknowledged etc.) – it appears to be an objection, but he brought it before proceeding with the proof. His saying: (Because light is a non-relative thing, for we see light with certainty primarily by itself, and if it were relative it would not be visible thus. His saying: (So there is no validity to their saying etc.) – it is not hidden that such licenses are common when they interpret appearance...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage refutes the theory that light is merely the appearance of color. The refutation offers two arguments: First, light is a changing phenomenon (mutajaddid) while color is permanent, so they cannot be identical. Second, light is common among all colors (e.g., black and white can both be illuminated), but colors differ in essence, so light cannot be identical to any of them. The passage then examines an objection: the opponent might say the changing thing is a new color, so light is not additional. The response is that this entails continual renewal of colors with light intensity, which is absurd (colors cannot be simultaneously renewing or successively changing). Then Imam al-Razi clarifies the issue: if they mean light is an additional quality causing appearance, it is a verbal dispute; if they mean light is a relative state (the color's appearing to sense), it is false because light is an absolute entity; if they mean light is the renewed color, then their statement has no validity. The glosses explain that the text's phrasing 'light is the apparent color' implies the theory reduces light to color appearance. One gloss notes that the refutation was brought prematurely but still valid; another emphasizes light's non-relative nature for it is directly perceived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the theory that light is the appearance of color is unambiguously false or nonsensical.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if interpreted as 'light is an additional quality that causes appearance,' the dispute is merely verbal, not a substantive metaphysical error.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, which interpretation of the theory 'light is the appearance of color' would make the disagreement merely verbal?
A) If they mean light is the color itself appearing.
B) If they mean light is an additional quality that causes appearance.
C) If they mean light is a relative state of the color's appearance.
D) If they mean light is a body.

Key Takeaway
The identity of light and color appearance is refuted by their differing properties, and al-Razi clarifies that only one interpretation of the theory is substantive; the rest are either false or merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that your beliefs about reality are based on clear distinctions. But al-Razi shows that even sophisticated thinkers can mistake a verbal dispute for a real one. When was the last time you held a conviction that, upon honest reflection, might have been merely about words? What would it cost you to admit that today?
?
The argument that light is changing while color is permanent seems straightforward—yet it challenges us to examine what we treat as permanent in our own lives. Is there an aspect of your character or faith that you consider unchanging, but that might actually be subject to renewal or decline? Are you willing to investigate that possibility with the same rigor as these scholars?
?
Al-Razi's clarification that some disagreements are merely verbal is freeing, but also demanding. It requires you to listen to your opponent's exact meaning before dismissing them. Think of a person you disagree with on a significant issue. Have you genuinely sought to understand their interpretation, or have you conflated their words with something worse? Commit to one conversation this week where you prioritize understanding over rebuttal.

The Decisive Refutation and the Philosophers' Intensity Proof
Author presents the decisive refutation and philosophers' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the decisive argument that light is not identical to color, based on the crystal example, and the refutation of the philosophers' proof from the weakening of weaker lights by stronger ones.

Core Idea
Light can exist without color, disproving their identity.

In this passage, the author delivers the authoritative refutation against those who claim light is merely the appearance of color. Using the example of crystal in darkness, he shows that light can be perceived where no color exists. He then presents the philosophers' argument from the weakening of weaker lights by stronger ones, and explains that this is due to the limitation of sense perception, not a real disappearance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Get a sense of the flow from the refutation to the philosophers' proof.
2
Understand the decisive refutation
Focus on the crystal example: it shows light without color. Also note the argument that if light were identical to color, lights would oppose each other, but light only opposes darkness.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Analyze the philosophers' proof from intensity
Follow the chain: firefly, lamp, moon, sun. The philosophers argue that weaker lights disappear before stronger ones, suggesting light is just the appearance of color.
Notice the progression from weaker to stronger.
4
Grasp the refutation of that proof
The author clarifies that the weakening is only perceptual: the sense cannot perceive the weaker when the stronger is present. There is no actual extinction.
This refutes the philosophers' conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمعتمد في الرد على هذا القائل أنَّ البلور في الظلمة إذا وقع عليه ضوء يرى ضوؤه ولا يرى لونه لعدمه فقد وجد الضوء بدون اللون كما قد وجد أيضاً اللون بدونه، فإن السواد وغيره من الألوان قد لا يكون مضيئاً، وأيضاً لو كان الضوء عين اللون لكان بعضه ضداً لبعضه لكنه باطل لأنَّ الضوء لا يقابله إلا الظلمة احتج القائل بأنَّ الضوء هو ظهور اللون لا كيفية زائدة عليه بل الحس كما مر إذا ترقى من الأدنى إلى الأعلى ظن هناك بريقاً ولمعاناً بأنه يزول الضوء الأضعف بالأقوى كاللامع بالليل مثل اليراعة وعين الهرة فإنه مضيئاً یری في الظلمة ولا يرى ضوء في السراج ثم السراج فإنه يرى مضيئاً شديداً ويضمحل ضوؤه في ضوء القمر ثم القمر فإنه مضيء ولا ضوء له في الشمس ثم الشمس فإنها الغاية في الإضاءة التي يزول فيها ضوء ما عداها وما هو أي ليس زوال الأضعف بالأقوى إلا لأن الحس لا يدرك الأضعف عند الأقوى ولا زوال ثمة بحسب نفس الأمر، بل الحس لما ضعف في الظلمة وكان للامع بالليل قدر من الظهور ظن أن ذلك الظهور كيفية زائدة على لونه ثم إذا تقوى بنور السراج ونظر إلى اللامع لم ير له لمعاناً لزوال ضعف البصر وكذا الكلام في السراج والقمر فقد ظهر أن أضواء هذه الأشياء ليست إلا ظهور ألوانها عند الحس كما أنَّ زوالها ليس إلا
The relied-upon refutation against this speaker is that crystal in darkness, when light falls on it, its light is seen but its color is not seen due to its absence. So light is found without color, just as color is also found without it. For black and other colors may not be luminous. Also, if light were identical to color, then some lights would be opposite to others, but that is false because light is only opposed by darkness. The speaker argued—that light is the appearance of color, not an additional quality, but the sense, as mentioned, when it ascends from the lower to the higher thinks there is brilliance and glitter—by saying: the weaker light disappears before the stronger, like the luminous at night such as the firefly and the cat's eye, for it is seen glowing in darkness but no light is seen in the lamp; then the lamp: it is seen intensely luminous and its light fades in moonlight; then the moon: it is luminous but has no light in the sun; then the sun: it is the utmost in brightness, extinguishing the light of everything else. And this, i.e., the disappearance of the weaker before the stronger, is only because the sense does not perceive the weaker when the stronger is present, and there is no actual disappearance in reality. Rather, when the sense is weak in darkness and the nocturnal luminous has a degree of appearance, it thinks that appearance is an additional quality on its color. Then when it is strengthened by the light of the lamp and looks at the luminous, it sees no glitter due to the removal of the weakness of sight. The same applies to the lamp and the moon. Thus it appears that the lights of these things are nothing but the appearance of their colors to the sense, just as their disappearance is nothing but...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage provides the authoritative refutation (al-muʿtamad) of the philosophers' view. The key argument: crystal in darkness shows its light but not its color, proving light exists without color. Conversely, colors like black can exist without being luminous. Additionally, if light were identical to color, lights would be mutually opposite, but light is only opposed by darkness. Then the philosopher's own proof (iḥtijāj) is presented: they argue that weaker lights vanish before stronger ones (e.g., firefly vs. lamp, lamp vs. moon, moon vs. sun), and this is due to the sense's inability to perceive the weaker, not because of an actual extinction. The sense mistakenly thinks the weak light is an added quality, but once the sense strengthens, it realizes it was just the color's appearance. The passage concludes that all lights are merely the appearance of colors to the sense, and their disappearance is similarly perceptual, not real.

Key Terms
المعتمد
البلور
الظلمة
الضوء بدون اللون
الاحتجاج
الضعيف والأقوى
الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disappearance of light in brighter environments proves that light is not a real quality but merely the appearance of color.
Correction: The author argues that the disappearance is only due to the limitation of sense perception; the weaker light still exists but is not perceived in the presence of a stronger light.

Checkpoint
What is the decisive argument (المعتمد) against the philosophers' claim that light is the appearance of color?
A) Fireflies show light without color
B) Crystal in darkness shows light without color
C) The moon has no light in the sun
D) Colors oppose one another

Key Takeaway
Light can exist independently of color; the apparent disappearance of weaker lights is a perceptual limitation, not an ontological extinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you trusted your senses (sight, feeling) to judge something as false or nonexistent, only to later realize you were limited by your own strength? How does this lesson challenge you to seek deeper reality beyond appearances?
?
The philosophers argued that because a weaker light disappears in stronger light, light is not real. What 'lights' in your faith or character seem to vanish when you are in a brighter environment? Are they truly gone, or is you perception weak?
?
This passage shows that the same object (like a firefly) can appear luminous in darkness but not in lamplight. What aspect of your relationship with Allah seems to 'fade' when worldly distractions intensify? Will you strengthen your inner sight to perceive what is always there?

The Glittering Thing: Ray vs. Glitter and the Objection about Air
Author defines the glittering thing and distinguishes intrinsic ray from extrinsic glitter, then answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the intrinsic ray (shuʿāʿ) and the extrinsic glitter (barīq), and how the objection about air being invisible is answered through the condition of color.

Core Idea
Ray is intrinsic, glitter is acquired; air is invisible because it lacks sufficient color.

This passage introduces a subtle optical phenomenon: a glittering sheen on illuminated bodies that is distinct from light itself. The author classifies this sheen into intrinsic ray and extrinsic glitter, then defends the distinction against an objection based on the invisibility of air. Understanding this clarifies how Islamic kalam engaged with empirical observations and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the glittering thing
Start by reading the author's definition of 'the glittering thing' (al-mutaraqriq) as a sheen on illuminated bodies. Notice the description that it appears to flow from them and nearly conceals their color.
This sets the phenomenon to be analyzed.
2
Identify the two types: ray and glitter
The passage divides the glittering thing into two categories: what belongs to the body by itself (ray) and what comes from another (glitter). Pay attention to the sun and mirror examples.
The analogy with light and brightness clarifies the intrinsic/extrinsic relationship.
3
Understand the objection about air
Read the objection: if air were affected by this glittering thing or light, it should be perceivable like a wall is, but it is not; therefore, air is not affected. This challenges the existence of the phenomenon.
The objection relies on a general principle about perception.
4
Follow the answer involving color
The response denies the entailment: color may be a necessary condition for perception. Air lacks sufficient color (it is either colorless or very weakly colored), so it cannot be seen even if affected. This preserves the possibility that air is affected.
This shows the limits of the objection.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the author uses logical analysis and empirical observation (visibility conditions) to defend a theoretical distinction. This is a common pattern in kalam: defining terms, classifying, addressing objections.

Source Text

المقصد الرابع : أن ثمة شيئاً غير الضوء يترقرق أي يتلألأ ويلمع على بعض الأجسام المستنيرة كأنه شيء يفيض منها ويكاد يستر لونها وهو له إما لذاته ويسمى حينئذ شعاعاً كما للشمس من التلألؤ واللمعان الذاتي وإما عن غيره ويسمى حينئذ بريقاً كما للمرآة التي حاذت الشمس ونسبة البريق إلى الشعاع نسبة النور إلى الضوء في أن الشعاع والضوء ذاتيان للجسم والبريق والنور مستفادان من غيره. احتج المانع بأنه لو تكيف الهواء به لأحس به كما يحس بالجدار المتكيف به لكن الهواء لا يحس به أصلاً فلا يكون متكيفاً بالضوء وجوابه منع الملازمة لجواز أن يكون اللون شرطاً في الإحساس به فلا يكون التكيف بالضوء وحده كافياً في رؤية المتكيف بالضوء الضعيف والهواء إما غير ملون بالكلية وإما له لون ضعيف جداً بحيث يكون له أضعف مما للماء والأحجار المشفة فلا يكون ذلك اللون كافياً في رؤية الهواء مع كفايته في قبوله للضوء إن جعل قبوله له مشروطاً باللون
The fourth aim: that there is a thing other than light that glitters, i.e., it shines and gleams upon some illuminated bodies, as if it is a thing flowing from them and nearly conceals their color. It belongs to the body either by itself and is then called a ray (shuʿāʿ), like the sun's intrinsic shining and gleaming, or from another and is then called a glitter (barīq), like a mirror that faces the sun. The relation of glitter to ray is like the relation of light (nūr) to brightness (ḍawʾ), in that ray and brightness are intrinsic to the body, while glitter and light are acquired from another. The objector argued: if the air were affected (mutakayyif) by it, it would be perceived as the wall affected by it is perceived, but the air is not perceived at all, so it is not affected by brightness. The answer is: the entailment is denied, because color may be a condition for perceiving it, so being affected by brightness alone is not sufficient for seeing what is affected by weak brightness. Air is either completely uncolored or has a very weak color, weaker than that of water and transparent stones, so that color is not sufficient for seeing the air, while it is sufficient for the air's acceptance of brightness—if acceptance is made conditional upon color.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines a phenomenon called 'the glittering thing' (al-mutaraqriq) that is distinct from light. The author first explains that this thing appears on illuminated bodies as a sheen, and it is either intrinsic to the body, in which case it is called a ray (shuʿāʿ), or acquired from another source, in which case it is called a glitter (barīq). The analogy is given: the relation of glitter to ray is like light (nūr) to brightness (ḍawʾ); brightness and ray are intrinsic, while light and glitter are derived. The passage then addresses an objection: if air were affected by this glittering thing, it should be perceptible like a wall that is affected, but air is not perceptible. The response is that the entailment is denied because color may be necessary for perception; air lacks sufficient color to be seen even if it is affected. This shows that being affected by brightness alone is not enough for visual perception; the medium must also have a certain degree of color.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The glittering thing is the same as light; air affected by light should be visible.
Correction: The passage separates the glittering thing from light, and shows that invisibility does not disprove affectation because perception may require color, which air lacks.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objector claim that air is not affected by brightness?
A) Because air is transparent and cannot be affected.
B) Because if it were affected, it would be perceived like a wall, but it is not perceived.
C) Because air has no color at all.
D) Because brightness only affects opaque bodies.

Key Takeaway
The glittering thing is distinct from light, with intrinsic ray and extrinsic glitter; air's invisibility does not disprove it being affected because perception may require color, which air lacks.

?

?
The author says air may have a weak color insufficient for sight but enough to receive light. What part of your deen feels faintly colored—not impressive to others—but still able to absorb divine influence? How can you focus on that reception without craving visibility or praise?

The Definition of Sound and Its Confusion with Causes
Author introduces audibles and defines sound, refuting misconceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence of sound is intuitive and distinct from its physical causes.

Core Idea
Sound's essence is self-evident, not to be confused with its causes.

This passage from the discussion of audibles clarifies that sound, despite being intuitively known, has been mistakenly identified with its causes—the wave of air, or the strike or split. The author defends the intuitive nature of sound and warns against conflating it with its physical causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview. Notice the structure: introduction, two sections, then the first aim on sound.
The text ends abruptly with 'ربما'; the author's argument continues, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the proposed definitions
The passage mentions two incorrect definitions: sound as the wave of air, and sound as the strike or split. Understand why these are considered causes, not the essence.
The author calls the wave the 'proximate cause' and the strike/split 'remote causes'.
3
Understand the author's correction
The author asserts that sound's quiddity is intuitive and needs no definition. He supports this by noting that the wave is perceived by touch, not hearing, showing that sound is something else.
This highlights the importance of distinguishing between a thing and its causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : المسموعات من المحسوسات المسموعات وهي الأصوات والحروف التي هي كيفيات عارضة للأصوات ومباحثه قسمان القسم الأول : في الصوت قدمه على الحرف لكونه معروضاً له متقدماً عليه بالطبع وفيه مقاصد المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج إن الصوت وإن كان بديهي التصور كسائر المحسوسات إلا أنه قد اشتبهت عند بعضهم ماهيته بسببه فقيل : الصوت هو التموج أي تموج الهواء وهو سببه القريب وقيل : الصوت هو القرع أو القلع مع أن هذين سببان له بعيدان والحق كما أشرنا إليه أن ماهيته بديهية مستغنية عن التعريف ومغايرة لما توهموه فإن التموج محسوس باللمس ألا أن یری الصوت الشديد ربما
The third type: audible things. Among perceptibles are audible things, namely sounds and letters (ḥurūf) which are qualities accidental to sounds. Its investigations are two sections. The first section: on sound. He prefaced it over the letter because it is a substrate for it, prior to it by nature. Within it are aims. The first aim: sound is the wave (al-tamawwuj). Indeed, sound, although its conception is intuitive like other perceptibles, its quiddity has been confused by some due to its cause. So it was said: sound is the wave, i.e., the waving of air, which is its proximate cause. And it was said: sound is the strike (al-qarʿ) or the split (al-qalʿ), although these are two remote causes. The truth, as we have indicated, is that its quiddity is intuitive, needing no definition, and different from what they imagined, for the wave is perceived by touch, but you see that intense sound sometimes...

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion of the third type of perceptibles: audibles. The author first states that audibles include sounds and letters, where letters are qualities that occur to sounds. The investigation is divided into two sections, the first being about sound, which is given precedence because it serves as the substrate for the letter. The first aim within this section is to define sound. Despite sound being intuitively known, some have confused its essence with its cause. Two incorrect views are mentioned: that sound is the wave (tamawwuj) of air (which is actually its proximate cause), and that sound is the strike or split (which are remote causes). The author affirms that the truth is that the essence of sound is self-evident, needing no definition, and is distinct from these causes. He supports this by noting that the wave is perceptible by touch, not by hearing, showing that sound is something else.

Correction: The wave of air is the proximate cause of sound, not sound itself. Sound's essence is intuitively known and is perceived by hearing, while the wave is perceived by touch.

Two Empirical Proofs That Hearing Requires Air to Reach the Ear
Sanusi provides two empirical proofs for the necessity of air reaching the ear for hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two empirical demonstrations that hearing sound depends on the physical arrival of the air carrying it at the earhole.

Core Idea
Sound does not travel magically; it needs air to reach the ear.

Sanusi offers two concrete experiments—one with a tube, one with wind—to show that hearing is not an abstract relation but requires the air medium to physically arrive at the ear. These proofs ground the theoretical claim in everyday experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the careful wording of the two proofs.
2
Understand the tube experiment
Visualize the tube: speaker at one end, listener at the other. Why does only that person hear? Because the tube confines the air.
3
Understand the wind experiment
Think of the mu'adhdhin's voice: downwind hears, upwind does not. Why? The wind moves the air carrying the sound.
4
Connect to the general principle
Both proofs show that hearing requires the air to arrive at the ear. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إن الاحساس بالصوت يتوقف على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى حاسة مة السامع لوجوه الأول : أنَّ من وضع فمه في طرف أنبوبة طويلة ووضع طرفها الآخر في صماخ إنسان وتكلم فيه بصوت عال سمعه ذلك الإنسان دون غيره من الحاضرين وإن كانوا أقرب إلى المتكلم من ذلك الإنسان وما هو إلا لحصرها أي ليس سماعه للصوت دون غيره إلا لحصر الأنبوبة الهواء الحامل للصوت ومنعها إياه من الانتشار والوصول إلى صماخ الغير فلا يصل إلا إلى صماخ ذلك الإنسان فلا يسمعه إلا هو . الثاني : أنه أعني الصوت يميل مع الريح كما هو المجرب في صوت المؤذن على المنارة فمن كان منه في جهة تهب الريح إليها يسمع صوته وإن كان بعيداً ومن كان في غير تلك الجهة لا يسمعه وإن تساويا في مسافة البعد وما ذلك إلا لأن الريح تميل الهواء الحامل له وتحركه إلى الجانب الذي هبت إليه فدل على أنَّ
And we only said that the sensation of sound depends on the arrival of the air carrying it to the sense of hearing, due to proofs: First, that if a person places his mouth at one end of a long tube and places its other end in the earhole of another person and speaks in it with a loud voice, that person hears it without others present, even if they are closer to the speaker than that person; and it is only due to the tube confining the air carrying the sound and preventing it from spreading and reaching the earhole of others, so it only reaches the earhole of that person, so only he hears it. Second, that sound inclines with the wind, as is experienced with the voice of the mu'adhdhin on a minaret: so whoever is in a direction towards which the wind blows hears his voice, even if far away, and whoever is not in that direction does not hear it, even if they are equal in distance; and that is only because the wind inclines the air carrying the sound and moves it to the side towards which it blew. Thus it indicates that...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents two empirical proofs to support the claim that hearing sound requires the actual arrival of the air carrying it at the earhole. The first proof uses a tube: when someone speaks into one end of a long tube held to another person's ear, only that person hears, because the tube confines the air and prevents it from dispersing to others. If sound could be heard without the air reaching the ear, others nearby would also hear it. The second proof invokes the common experience of a mu'adhdhin's voice on a minaret: the sound is heard downwind but not upwind, even at equal distances, because the wind moves the air carrying the sound. Both proofs demonstrate that the sensation of sound is tied to the physical arrival of the air medium at the ear, not to some abstract relation. The conclusion (indicated by 'فدل على أنَّ') is that hearing depends on the arrival of the air.

صماخ
ريح
مؤذن
منارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think sound travels independently of the air medium.
Correction: These proofs show that hearing requires the actual arrival of the air carrying the sound at the earhole.

Checkpoint
What does the tube experiment in this passage demonstrate?
A) Sound travels faster in tubes.
B) Sound can be heard without air reaching the earhole.
C) Sound is heard only when the air carrying it reaches the earhole.
D) Tubes amplify sound.

Key Takeaway
Hearing sound depends on the actual arrival of the air carrying the sound to the ear, as shown by the tube and wind experiments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'air' (means of connection) have you neglected in your relationship with Allah, causing His guidance not to reach your heart? How will you actively bring that medium back today?
?

?
If you were to test your own spiritual hearing like the tube experiment, what barriers (like distractions or sins) are blocking the air of revelation from reaching your inner ear? Name one barrier you will remove this week.

Objection and Response: The Wind Proof Refined
Objection and refutation concerning the wind proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a specific objection to the wind proof is refuted by showing that the disturbance of hearing actually confirms the dependence of sensation on air arrival.

Core Idea
Disturbance in hearing proves the very dependence it was meant to disprove.

The author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif raises an empirical counterexample: when wind blows, we hear the mu'adhdhin's voice moving away, yet we are in a windless spot. He claims this shows sound reaches the ear without the air carrier. The author responds by noting that our hearing is disturbed, which indicates that the air must have reached our ear but with interference. This turns the objection into further evidence for the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay close attention to the objector's empirical claim: that we hear a sound even though the air carrying it does not reach our ear because we are in a windless location.
The objector uses a common experience: the mu'adhdhin's voice seems to shift direction with the wind.
2
Identify the response
Locate the phrase 'وفيه نظر' (There is a consideration). Read the response and notice the key point: the disturbance (تشوش) of hearing indicates that the air did reach the ear, but with interference.
The response uses the very fact of disturbance as evidence for dependence.
3
Reflect on the logic
Think about why the disturbance proves dependence. If hearing did not depend on air arrival, the sound would remain clear regardless of wind. The fact that it becomes disturbed shows that the air carrier is indeed necessary.
This turns the objection into a supportive argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترض عليه صاحب الصحائب بأنا ندرك أن صوت المؤذن عند هبوب الرياح يميل عن جهتنا إلى خلافها، وذلك ضروري يعرفه كل أحد بالتجربة ومن المعلوم ضرورة أنّ الهواء الحامل لذلك الصوت ما وصل إلى صماخنا إذ نحن وقتئذ في موضع لا ريح فيه حتى يقال : إنه صرفه عن جهتنا بل كان خارجاً عن ذلك الموضع صرفه الريح عن جهتنا فقد سمعنا صوتا مع عدم وصول الهواء الحاصل لذلك الصوت إلى صماخنا، وفيه نظر لأن تشوش سماع الصوت حينئذ يدل على وصول الهواء الحامل له إلى صماخنا إذ لو لم يكن الإحساس متوقفاً على ذلك الوصول لما تشوش ضرورة والتالي باطل بالتجربة فكذا المقدم
The author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif objected: We perceive that the voice of the mu'adhdhin when the winds blow inclines from our direction to opposite it, and this is known necessarily by everyone through experience; and it is necessarily known that the air carrying that sound did not reach our earhole, because we are at that time in a place where there is no wind, so it cannot be said that it directed it away from our direction; rather it was outside that place, the wind directed it away from our direction. So we heard a sound without the air carrying that sound reaching our earhole. There is a consideration in this: because the disturbance of hearing the sound at that time indicates the arrival of the air carrying it to our earhole; for if sensation were not dependent on that arrival, it would not have necessarily been disturbed, and the consequent is false by experience, so the antecedent is also false.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif against the second proof (wind proof). The objector argues that when wind blows, we hear the mu'adhdhin's voice moving away from us; since we are in a wind-free location, the air carrying the sound could not have reached our earhole. Yet we still hear, so hearing does not require air arrival. The response (وفيه نظر) refutes this by pointing out that our hearing of the sound is disturbed (tashawwush) – we hear it poorly or indistinctly. This disturbance actually proves that hearing depends on the air's arrival, because if it were not dependent, the sound would remain clear irrespective of wind. The disturbance is empirical evidence for the conclusion, so the objection fails and actually supports the original claim.

Key Terms
صاحب الصحائف
ريح
تشوش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that hearing does not depend on air reaching the ear.
Correction: The disturbance of hearing actually proves the opposite: if hearing were independent, it would not be disturbed; disturbance indicates interference with the air carrier, confirming its role.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection from wind?
A) He concedes that sound can be heard without air reaching the ear.
B) He argues that the disturbance in hearing proves that sensation depends on air arrival.
C) He claims that the wind does not affect sound propagation.
D) He denies the experience of the mu'adhdhin.

Key Takeaway
The disturbance of hearing in the wind experiment confirms that sensation depends on the arrival of the air carrier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the wind disturbs your hearing of the adhan, do you ever pause to consider that it is Allah's design that makes the sound reach you? How could this awareness change your experience of hearing the call to prayer?
?
The objector used an everyday experience to challenge a truth. In your own life, what common experiences have you allowed to weaken your conviction in the signs of Allah? Will you commit to re-examining them with the insight that apparent exceptions often prove the rule?
?
The disturbance in hearing was proof of dependence. What 'disturbances' in your spiritual life—difficulties, doubts, trials—might actually be evidence of Allah's intricate design? Ask yourself: Are you willing to let these disturbances drive you closer to Him rather than away?
