First Proof: Short Vowels as Parts of Long Vowels
The author gives the first proof for part-of-ness, then presents an objection with two interpretative scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that short vowels are parts of long vowels based on the property of increase and decrease, and the objection that challenges this reasoning.

Core Idea
The minimal extreme of a variable thing reveals its component parts.

The author begins by presenting a key argument in phonology: short vowels are actually parts of long vowels. The proof relies on the fact that long vowels can be lengthened or shortened, so they must have two extremes. The decreasing extreme, identified by induction, is the short vowel. However, an objector raises a subtle challenge: what exactly happens at that extreme? Does the long vowel cease to be itself, or does it retain some lengthening? This sets the stage for a dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how the proof uses the concepts of increase (زيادة) and decrease (نقصان) to argue for an extreme.
2
Identify the objection's two horns
The objector presents two possible interpretations of what happens at the decreasing extreme. Find them in the text: either the long vowel exits its reality or it does not.
Each horn leads to a different problem for the proof.
3
Understand the dilemma
The objection shows that if the long vowel retains its reality, some lengthening must remain, so the endpoint is not a pure short vowel. If it loses its reality, the endpoint is not analogous to the increasing end.
This undermines the straightforward identification of short vowel as part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركات أبعاض المصوتات. أما أولاً : فلأن هذه المصوتات قابلة للزيادة والنقصان، وكل ما كان كذلك فله طرفان، ولا طرف في النقصان للمصوتات إلا هذه الحركات بشهادة الاستقراء. قولح: (إلا) هذه الحركات قيل : إن أريد أن تلك الحروف تخرج عن حقيقتها عند وصولها إلى طرف النقصان صح الحكم بكون الطرف الناقص هو الحركة المحضة بلا إشباعها. لكن اتجه أن الطرف الناقص حينئذ لا يكون كالزائد، وعلى طريقته. وإن أريد أنها لا تخرج عن حقيقتها فلا بد من اعتبار الإشباع مع تلك الحركات ولو قليلاً، فلا يكون الطرف الناقص محض تلك الحركات بلا إشباع، فلا يثبت بعضيتها لهم. وأجيب تارة باختيار الأول ومنع وجوب كون الطرف الناقص على حد الزائد، وتارة باختيار الثاني ولا حاجة إلى اعتبار الإشباع، لأن تلك الحركات المجردة عن الإشباع من أفراد المصوتة أيضاً. إذ ينقسم المصوتة إلى مقصورة هي الحركات وممدودة هي الحروف المخصوصة كما سيأتي الآن، ولو سلم وجوب اعتبار الإشباع تم الاستدلال أيضاً. لأن الحركات مأخوذة من الإشباع القليل لما كانت تمام الماهية الناقصة لتلك الحروف، كما أن بعض ماهيتها تلك الحركات قطعاً. ويرد على الجواب الأول: إن الحروف المذكورة إذا خرجت عن حقيقتها عند الوصول إلى الطرف الناقص وتحققت الحركات لم يثبت كون الحركات أبعاض الحروف، إلا بأن يبين أن وصولها إلى ذلك الطرف بانتفاء جزء منها مع بقاء أجزاء أخرى، وفيه المصادرة، وبه يندفع الوجه الأول من الجواب الثاني أيضاً. إذ ما له التزام خروج الحروف التي ادعى بعضية الحركات منها عن حقيقتها المخصوصة؟ وأي فائدة لإطلاق المصوتة بالمعنى العام على تلك الحركات. وأما الوجه الثاني منه ففيه أن كون الحروف المذكورة حركات مأخوذة مع الإشباع قليلاً أو كثيراً أول المسألة، ولو ثبت لم يحتج إلى اعتبار جانب النقصان كما لا يخفى، فتأمل.
The short vowels are parts of the long vowels. As for the first (proof): because these long vowels are susceptible to increase and decrease, and everything that is so has two ends, and there is no end in the decrease for the long vowels except these short vowels, by the testimony of induction. He said: '(Except) these short vowels' it was said: If it is intended that those letters exit from their reality when they reach the end of decrease, the ruling that the decreasing end is the pure short vowel without its lengthening is sound. But it appears that the decreasing end then is not like the increasing, and on its path. And if it is intended that they do not exit from their reality, then it is necessary to consider lengthening with those short vowels, even if little, so the decreasing end is not the mere of those short vowels without lengthening, so their being parts is not proven for them. And it was answered sometimes by choosing the first and denying the obligation that the decreasing end be on the same level as the increasing, and sometimes by choosing the second and there is no need to consider lengthening, because those short vowels stripped of lengthening are also individuals of the long vowel. For the long vowel is divided into short, which are the short vowels, and long, which are the specific letters, as will come now. And even if the necessity of considering lengthening is granted, the inference is also complete. Because the short vowels taken from slight lengthening are the complete deficient quiddity of those letters, just as part of their quiddity is those short vowels definitely. And it is objected to the first answer: Those mentioned letters, if they exit from their reality when reaching the decreasing end and the short vowels occur, it does not prove that the short vowels are parts of the letters, unless it is shown that their reaching that end is by the negation of a part of them while other parts remain, and therein is petitio principii, and by this the first aspect of the second answer is also repelled. For what (obliges) the exit of the letters for which the part-of-ness of short vowels is claimed from their specific reality? And what benefit is there in applying 'long vowel' in the general sense to those short vowels? And as for the second aspect of it, in it is that the mentioned letters being short vowels taken with lengthening little or much is the beginning of the question, and if it were established, there would be no need to consider the side of decrease, as is not hidden, so ponder.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that short vowels (حركات) are parts of long vowels (مصوتات), followed by an objection-and-answer dialectic. The proof argues that long vowels can vary in length (increase/decrease), so they must have two extremes; the minimal extreme (end of decrease) is the pure short vowel, based on inductive observation. An objector counters: if by 'end of decrease' we mean the point where long vowels lose their reality and become mere short vowels, then the endpoint is indeed the short vowel, but then the decreasing endpoint is not analogous to the increasing endpoint (which is a full long vowel). If instead we mean that the long vowel retains its reality, then some lengthening must remain, so the endpoint is not a pure short vowel, and part-of-ness is not proven. The author records two answers: (1) choose the first interpretation and deny that endpoints must be symmetrical; (2) choose the second interpretation but argue that short vowels without any lengthening are still individuals of the category 'long vowel' because the term 'vowel' (مصوتة) is divided into short (حركات) and long (حروف مخصوصة). Thus even without lengthening, short vowels are a subset. A rebuttal of the first answer follows: if long vowels exit their reality to become short vowels, then part-of-ness is not established unless it is shown that the loss is of a part while the rest remains—this is circular (مصادرة). The same rebuttal also undermines the first aspect of the second answer. As for the second answer, the author notes that considering short vowels as taken with slight lengthening is already the point at issue, so it begs the question. The passage ends with an invitation to ponder.

Key Terms
حركات
مصوتات
زيادة ونقصان
طرفان
إشباع
بعضية
استقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the decreasing endpoint of a long vowel is simply the short vowel without any further qualification.
Correction: The objection shows that the nature of the endpoint depends on whether the long vowel retains its reality; if it does, some lengthening remains, so the endpoint is not a pure short vowel.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must long vowels have two ends?
A) Because they can be pronounced at different pitches.
B) Because they are composed of short vowels.
C) Because they are susceptible to increase and decrease.
D) Because they have duration in time.

Key Takeaway
The first proof identifies short vowels as the decreasing extreme of long vowels, but the objection reveals that the nature of that extreme is ambiguous.

Answers to the Objection and Rebuttal
The author records two answers to the objection and then rebuts both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two answers given to the objection and the counter-rebuttal that undermines them.

Core Idea
Defending a proof often requires redefining the terms of the debate.

Having faced a strong objection, the defender offers two distinct answers. The first accepts one interpretation and denies symmetry; the second redefines what counts as a 'vowel' to include short vowels even without lengthening. The author then critically examines both, exposing logical flaws such as circular reasoning and question-begging.

Guided Path
1
Read the two answers
Read the passage containing the two answers. The first answer chooses the first horn of the dilemma and denies that the endpoints must be analogous. The second answer chooses the second horn but argues that short vowels without lengthening are still vowels.
Note the phrase 'إذ ينقسم المصوتة إلى مقصورة وممدودة' which redefines the category.
2
Follow the rebuttal
The author first rejects the first answer on grounds of circular reasoning (مصادرة). Then he criticizes the second answer's aspects: the first aspect is also circular, the second aspect begs the question.
The rebuttal uses logical terms like 'مصادرة' and 'أول المسألة'.
3
Reflect on the dialectic
See how philosophical debate proceeds by refining definitions and exposing assumptions. The passage ends with 'فتأمل' inviting careful thought.

Source Text

The short vowels are parts of the long vowels. Second Proof and Classification of Vowels
The author gives the second proof by extension, then classifies vowels into short and long.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that short vowels are parts of long vowels through extension, and the classification of vowels by mouth opening and heaviness.

Core Idea
Extending a short vowel directly produces a long vowel, revealing them as parts of the same sound.

This lesson continues the argument about vowels by presenting a second proof based on extension. The author shows that sensory experience confirms that lengthening a short vowel yields the corresponding long vowel without needing a new consonant. He then classifies long vowels by openness and heaviness, concluding that vowels split into short and long categories. You will also see an objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument and classification.
Notice the logical structure: proof, classification, objection, and commentary.
2
Analyze the proof by extension
Understand the conditional reasoning: if short vowels were not parts, extending a different sound would require a new consonant; but sense shows we get long vowels directly, so short vowels are parts.
The key is the 'implication' (ملازمة) and its refutation via empirical observation.
3
Examine the classification and objection
Note the three long vowels ordered by mouth opening (alif, ya, waw) and three by heaviness (damma, kasra, fatha). Then consider the objection that the proof only works for appropriate vowel extensions.
The author notes that heaviness/lightness is relative to the letter itself, not temperaments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثانياً: فلأن الحركات لو لم تكن أبعاض المصوتات لما حصلت المصوتات بتمديدها، فإن الحركة إذا كانت مخالفة لها ومددتها لم يمكنك أن تذكر المصوت إلا باستئناف صامت آخر يجعل المصوت تبعاً له، لكن الحس شاهد بحصول المصوتات بمجرد تمديد الحركات. ثم إن أوسع المصوتات باعتبار انفتاح الفم هو الألف ثم الياء ثم الواو، وأثقلها الضمة المحتاجة إلى مزيد تحريك الشفتين، ثم الكسرة ثم الفتحة، فقد جعل الحركات داخلة في المصوتات، فلذلك انقسم المصوتة إلى مقصورة هي الحركات وممدودة هي الحروف المخصوصة. قوله : (فإن الحركة إذا كانت مخالفة) هذا بيان الملازمة. وقوله : لكن الحس بيان لبطلان التالي. واعترض على هذا الوجه بأن حصول المصوتة بتمديد الحركة المناسبة لها دون المخالفة لا يدل على كون الحركة الممتدة بعضها منها. قوله : (وأثقلها الضمة) الخ هذا الذي ذكره من الثقل والخفة إنما هو بالقياس إلى الحرف نفسه. وأما بالقياس إلى الأمزجة فقد يختلف ذلك بحسب اختلافها، كذا في شرح الملخص.
And as for the second (proof): Because if the short vowels were not parts of the long vowels, the long vowels would not be obtained by extending them. For if the short vowel is different from it (the long vowel) and you extend it, you cannot produce the long vowel except by restarting with another consonant that makes the long vowel dependent on it, but sense testifies that long vowels are obtained merely by extending short vowels. Then the widest of the long vowels in terms of mouth opening is alif, then ya, then waw, and the heaviest is damma which needs more movement of the lips, then kasra, then fatha. Thus he made the short vowels included in the long vowels, therefore the long vowel is divided into short, which are the short vowels, and long, which are the specific letters. His saying: 'For if the short vowel is different' is an explanation of the implication. And his saying: 'but sense' is an explanation of the falsity of the consequent. And it was objected to this aspect that the occurrence of the long vowel by extending the appropriate short vowel (not the different one) does not indicate that the extended short vowel is part of it. His saying: 'and the heaviest is damma' etc.: what he mentioned of heaviness and lightness is only relative to the letter itself. As for relative to temperaments, it may vary according to their difference, as in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof that short vowels (حركات) are parts of long vowels (مصوتات), followed by a classification of vowels. The proof is based on extension: if you take a short vowel and extend it (e.g., prolong the vowel sound), you directly obtain the corresponding long vowel—no additional consonant is needed. If short vowels were not parts, extending a different sound would require starting over with a consonant to produce a long vowel, but sensory experience shows that long vowels arise simply from lengthening the short vowel. The author then classifies long vowels (المصوتات) by mouth opening: alif is widest, then ya, then waw; by heaviness: damma is heaviest due to lip rounding, then kasra, then fatha. This leads to the division of the category 'vowel' (مصوتة) into short (حركات) and long (حروف مخصوصة). After this, the author comments on the proof: the conditional statement 'if the short vowel is different' establishes the implication (ملازمة), and 'sense testifies' negates the consequent. An objection follows: the proof only works if the extended short vowel is appropriate (i.e., the same point of articulation), but the text notes that the extension of the appropriate short vowel is what produces the long vowel, implying that the short vowel is indeed part of it. Finally, the author remarks that the stated heaviness/lightness is relative to the consonant itself; relative to individual temperaments, it may differ—a note from Sharh al-Mulakhkhas.

Key Terms

حروف مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that extending any short vowel yields any long vowel.
Correction: The proof requires the short vowel to be appropriate (i.e., same point of articulation) for the long vowel; extension of a different short vowel does not produce the desired long vowel.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof use to show that short vowels are parts of long vowels?
A) The fact that short vowels cannot be extended.
B) The sensory observation that extending a short vowel produces a long vowel directly.
C) The alphabetical order of letters.
D) The medical properties of vowels.

Key Takeaway
Short vowels are integral parts of long vowels, as shown by extension, and vowels are classified by mouth shape and weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you extend a short vowel in your recitation, do you sense that it becomes the long vowel naturally, or do you force it? What does this teach you about the harmony in Allah's creation of sound?
?
If the heaviness of damma is relative to the letter itself, not absolute for all people, how can you humbly adjust your own perception of 'difficulty' in worship? Does your soul sometimes prefer light actions (fatha-like) over heavy ones (damma-like)?
?
The proof relies on immediate sensory experience. When was the last time you let your senses (like hearing or sight) draw you closer to faith? How can you train yourself to see Allah's signs in the ordinary, like the sound of a vowel?

Two Proofs for the Priority of Consonant over Vowel
Sanusi enumerates two proofs for consonant priority and their objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs that the consonant is prior to the short vowel and the objections raised against them.

Core Idea
The consonant's instantaneity and the impossibility of a vowel alone prove its priority.

Sanusi presents two arguments for the temporal priority of the consonant over the short vowel. The first relies on the instantaneous nature of the consonant versus the temporal nature of the vowel; the second appeals to our inner experience that a vowel cannot be pronounced without a consonant. However, each proof faces a challenge, and Sanusi addresses them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two reasons are clearly numbered.
2
Identify the first proof and its objection
The first proof uses instantaneity vs temporality. Find the objection that the consonant might occur at the last instant of the vowel's time.
3
Identify the second proof and its objection
The second proof uses the independence argument. Find the objection that disproving vowel priority does not prove consonant priority.
4
Understand Sanusi's responses
Sanusi responds to the second objection by suggesting that even if the vowel were prior, the consonant could follow as a separate individual. Note that the first objection is left open.
This leads to the extension about quiescent consonants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والحرف الصامت سابق على الحركة لوجهين الأول : أن الصامت البسيط حقيقة وحساً آني، والحركة زمانية. والآن متقدم على الزمان، فما يوجد في الآن الذي هو أول زمان وجود الشيء كان سابقاً على ما يحدث فيه. وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون حدوث الحرف الآني في الآن الذي هو آخر زمان الحركة، ولا بد لنفيه من دليل. الثاني : أن الحركة لو كانت سابقة على الحرف لكان المتكلم بالحركة مستغنياً عن التكلم بالحرف، لأن السابق غني عن المسبوق المحتاج إليه، والتالي باطل لأنا نجد من أنفسنا وجداناً ضرورياً أنه لا يمكن التكلم بالحركة دون التكلم بالحرف. واعترض عليه بأنه ليس يلزم من إبطال تقدم الحركة على الحرف الصامت تقدمه عليها، لجواز أن لا يسبق أحدهما الآخر بل يوجدان معاً. على أنا نقول : جاز أن يكون السابق مستبقاً للمسبوق بحيث تخلفه عنه، فلا يثبت حينئذ بطلان تقدم الحركة على الحرف. وبهذا يعلم أيضاً بطلان أن الابتداء بالصامت الساكن جائز، وإلا توقف الصامت المتقدم على ما قيل من الصوت المتأخر المحتاج إلى ذلك المتقدم وهو محال. يمتنع قوله : ( حقيقة وحساً ) آني احتراز عن البسيط حساً لا حقيقة كالراء. قوله : ( والحركة زمانية ) قد مر أن معنى حركة الحرف كونها بحيث يمكن أن يوجد عقيبها مصوت مخصوص من المصوتات، فزمانية الحركة ممنوعة فلا بد لها من دليل. قوله : ( وقد يقال : جاز إلخ ) قد يجاب بدعوى الضرورة الوجدانية في أن الحرف ليس بعد الحركة. قوله : ( واعترض إلخ ) قد يجاب : بأنه لما علم بطلان تقدم الحركة على الحرف بالضرورة الوجدانية والمفروض أن الصامت آني والحركة زمانية، والآني يستحيل وجوده مع الزماني معية زمانية، استحال وجودهما بتلك المعية. قوله : ( وبهذا يعلم أيضاً بطلان إلخ ) وجه البطلان أنه لما ثبت تقدم الحرف على الحركة بل جوز معيتهما، جاز أن يكون من قبيل الشرط المتعاكس من غير لزوم تقدم الشرط على المشروط. وأيضاً لما جاز تقدم الحركة على الحرف لم يصح قوله على المصوت المتأخر المحتاج إلى ذلك التقدم، فإن تأخر المصوت مبني للدور. إذ لو تقدم لم يلزم توقفه على الصامت بمعنى احتياجه إليه المستدعي لتقدمه. وبالجملة لا يلزم من عدم إمكان الابتداء بالساكن أن يكون المصوت متقدماً على الصامت المتقدم عليه. بل اللازم منه أن الصامت لا يحصل إلا مع المصوت ولا استحالة فيه. وبما ذكرنا يظهر أن المراد بالمصوت في قوله : وإلا توقف الصامت
He said: And the consonant is prior to the short vowel for two reasons. First: The simple consonant in reality and in sensation is instantaneous (ani), and the short vowel is temporal. The instant is prior to time, so what exists in the instant which is the first time of the existence of a thing was prior to what occurs in it. And it may be said: It is possible that the occurrence of the instantaneous consonant is in the instant which is the last time of the short vowel, and it needs a proof to negate it. Second: If the short vowel were prior to the consonant, the speaker of the short vowel would be independent of speaking the consonant, because the prior is independent of the posterior that needs it, but the consequent is false because we find from ourselves by necessary inner experience that it is not possible to speak the short vowel without speaking the consonant. And it was objected that from the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant it does not necessarily follow that the consonant is prior to it, because it is possible that neither precedes the other but they exist together. Moreover, we say: It is possible that the prior is followed by the posterior such that it lags behind it, so then the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is not established. And by this it is also known that beginning with a quiescent consonant is not permissible, otherwise the prior consonant would depend on the subsequent sound that needs that prior, which is impossible. His saying: 'in reality and in sensation' instantaneous is a precaution against what is simple in sensation but not in reality, like ra'. His saying: 'and the short vowel is temporal' it has passed that the meaning of a short vowel of a letter is that it is such that a specific long vowel from among the long vowels can exist after it, so the temporality of the short vowel is disputed and requires a proof. His saying: 'and it may be said: it is possible etc.' it may be answered by claiming necessary inner experience that the consonant is not after the short vowel. His saying: 'and it was objected etc.' it may be answered: That since the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is known by necessary inner experience, and it is assumed that the consonant is instantaneous and the short vowel is temporal, and the instantaneous cannot exist with the temporal in temporal simultaneousness, their existence in that simultaneousness is impossible. His saying: 'and by this it is also known the falsity etc.' The aspect of falsity is that since the priority of the consonant over the short vowel is established, even if their simultaneity is allowed, it may be of the type of mutual condition without the necessity of the priority of the condition over the conditioned. Also, since the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is allowed, his statement about the subsequent sound needing that priority is not valid, for the posteriority of the sound is based on circularity. For if it were prior, its dependence on the consonant in the sense of needing it that demands its priority would not be necessary. In sum, from the impossibility of beginning with a quiescent consonant it does not follow that the sound is prior to the consonant that precedes it. Rather, what follows from it is that the consonant does not occur except with the sound, and there is no impossibility in that. And by what we have mentioned, it becomes clear that what is meant by 'sound' in his saying: 'otherwise the consonant would depend'
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage proves that the consonant (صامت) is temporally prior to the short vowel (حركة) using two arguments, then addresses objections and extends the discussion to the impossibility of starting a word with a quiescent (sakin) consonant. The first argument: the consonant is instantaneous (آنى), while the vowel is temporal (زمانية). The instant is prior to time, so what exists in the first instant of a thing's existence precedes what occurs within that time. A possible objection: the instantaneous consonant might occur at the last instant of the vowel's time, requiring a proof to rule this out. The second argument: if the vowel were prior, the speaker could utter the vowel without needing the consonant, but inner experience shows this is impossible—one cannot pronounce a vowel without a preceding consonant. An objection: the falsity of vowel priority does not prove consonant priority; they might be simultaneous or the vowel might even precede if the consonant follows as a later individual. The author then notes that if consonant priority or simultaneity is allowed, then beginning with a quiescent consonant (i.e., starting speech with a consonant without a vowel) is impossible, because that would make a prior thing dependent on a later thing, which is absurd. The text then provides commentary on specific phrases: 'instantaneous' excludes letters like 'ra' that are simple in sensation but not in reality; the temporality of the vowel is disputed and requires proof; the objection to the first reason can be answered by appealing to necessary inner experience that the consonant is not after the vowel; the objection to the second reason can be answered by noting that simultaneous existence of an instantaneous and a temporal thing is impossible, so they cannot be simultaneous. The final part clarifies that the impossibility of starting with a quiescent consonant does not imply that the vowel is prior to the consonant; rather, both occur together, with the consonant requiring the vowel for pronunciation. The passage end clarifies the intended meaning of 'sound' (مصوت) in the original text.

Key Terms

حركة
آني
زمانية
سابق
مسبوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof alone proves that the consonant is absolutely prior to the vowel.
Correction: The second proof only shows that the vowel cannot be prior, but the objection shows that simultaneity or vowel priority with a lagging consonant is possible. Thus, the proof only establishes that the consonant is not posterior, not that it is necessarily prior.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof for the priority of the consonant over the short vowel?
A) The consonant is longer in duration.
B) The consonant is instantaneous and the vowel is temporal, so the instant precedes time.
C) The vowel is created after the consonant in the vocal tract.
D) The consonant has a higher pitch.

Key Takeaway
The consonant's priority over the vowel is argued through its instantaneity and our inability to pronounce a vowel alone, though objections leave room for simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that inner experience proves you cannot speak a vowel alone. When was the last time you tested a deeply held belief by direct personal experience rather than accepting it from others? Will you do that today for one belief you've never questioned?
?
The first proof relies on the 'instant' being prior to time. Do you live as if the eternal instant of God's knowledge is prior to your temporal actions? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt that every moment emerges from an eternal decree?
?
Objections are raised against both proofs, yet Sanusi presents them without final resolution. Are you willing to sit with uncertainty about foundational issues in your faith, or do you demand neat answers? What would it cost you to admit that some truths remain inconclusive?

Impossibility of Beginning with a Quiescent Consonant
Sanusi deduces the impossibility of starting with a quiescent consonant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible to begin speech with a quiescent (sakin) consonant.

Core Idea
Starting with a quiescent consonant would make a prior thing depend on a later thing, which is absurd.

Sanusi extends the discussion of consonant priority to the practical issue of whether one can start a word with a consonant that has no vowel. He argues that this is impossible, because it would imply that the prior consonant depends on a subsequent sound for its existence, contradicting the priority established earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
The passage is short. Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical implication: if quiescent beginning were allowed, the prior consonant would depend on the later sound.
2
Connect to the previous proofs
Recall that the consonant is prior to the vowel (or at least not posterior). This extension uses that to argue that a consonant cannot exist without a subsequent vowel.
3
Understand the absurdity
If the consonant is prior and the vowel is posterior, then the prior should not depend on the posterior. But beginning with a quiescent consonant would require the consonant to depend on the following sound for its pronunciation, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والحرف الصامت سابق على الحركة لوجهين الأول : أن الصامت البسيط حقيقة وحساً آني، والحركة زمانية. والآن متقدم على الزمان، فما يوجد في الآن الذي هو أول زمان وجود الشيء كان سابقاً على ما يحدث فيه. وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون حدوث الحرف الآني في الآن الذي هو آخر زمان الحركة، ولا بد لنفيه من دليل. الثاني : أن الحركة لو كانت سابقة على الحرف لكان المتكلم بالحركة مستغنياً عن التكلم بالحرف، لأن السابق غني عن المسبوق المحتاج إليه، والتالي باطل لأنا نجد من أنفسنا وجداناً ضرورياً أنه لا يمكن التكلم بالحركة دون التكلم بالحرف. واعترض عليه بأنه ليس يلزم من إبطال تقدم الحركة على الحرف الصامت تقدمه عليها، لجواز أن لا يسبق أحدهما الآخر بل يوجدان معاً. على أنا نقول : جاز أن يكون السابق مستبقاً للمسبوق بحيث تخلفه عنه، فلا يثبت حينئذ بطلان تقدم الحركة على الحرف. وبهذا يعلم أيضاً بطلان أن الابتداء بالصامت الساكن جائز، وإلا توقف الصامت المتقدم على ما قيل من الصوت المتأخر المحتاج إلى ذلك المتقدم وهو محال. يمتنع قوله : ( حقيقة وحساً ) آني احتراز عن البسيط حساً لا حقيقة كالراء. قوله : ( والحركة زمانية ) قد مر أن معنى حركة الحرف كونها بحيث يمكن أن يوجد عقيبها مصوت مخصوص من المصوتات، فزمانية الحركة ممنوعة فلا بد لها من دليل. قوله : ( وقد يقال : جاز إلخ ) قد يجاب بدعوى الضرورة الوجدانية في أن الحرف ليس بعد الحركة. قوله : ( واعترض إلخ ) قد يجاب : بأنه لما علم بطلان تقدم الحركة على الحرف بالضرورة الوجدانية والمفروض أن الصامت آني والحركة زمانية، والآني يستحيل وجوده مع الزماني معية زمانية، استحال وجودهما بتلك المعية. قوله : ( وبهذا يعلم أيضاً بطلان إلخ ) وجه البطلان أنه لما ثبت تقدم الحرف على الحركة بل جوز معيتهما، جاز أن يكون من قبيل الشرط المتعاكس من غير لزوم تقدم الشرط على المشروط. وأيضاً لما جاز تقدم الحركة على الحرف لم يصح قوله على المصوت المتأخر المحتاج إلى ذلك التقدم، فإن تأخر المصوت مبني للدور. إذ لو تقدم لم يلزم توقفه على الصامت بمعنى احتياجه إليه المستدعي لتقدمه. وبالجملة لا يلزم من عدم إمكان الابتداء بالساكن أن يكون المصوت متقدماً على الصامت المتقدم عليه. بل اللازم منه أن الصامت لا يحصل إلا مع المصوت ولا استحالة فيه. وبما ذكرنا يظهر أن المراد بالمصوت في قوله : وإلا توقف الصامت
He said: And the consonant is prior to the short vowel for two reasons. First: The simple consonant in reality and in sensation is instantaneous (ani), and the short vowel is temporal. The instant is prior to time, so what exists in the instant which is the first time of the existence of a thing was prior to what occurs in it. And it may be said: It is possible that the occurrence of the instantaneous consonant is in the instant which is the last time of the short vowel, and it needs a proof to negate it. Second: If the short vowel were prior to the consonant, the speaker of the short vowel would be independent of speaking the consonant, because the prior is independent of the posterior that needs it, but the consequent is false because we find from ourselves by necessary inner experience that it is not possible to speak the short vowel without speaking the consonant. And it was objected that from the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant it does not necessarily follow that the consonant is prior to it, because it is possible that neither precedes the other but they exist together. Moreover, we say: It is possible that the prior is followed by the posterior such that it lags behind it, so then the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is not established. And by this it is also known that beginning with a quiescent consonant is not permissible, otherwise the prior consonant would depend on the subsequent sound that needs that prior, which is impossible. His saying: 'in reality and in sensation' instantaneous is a precaution against what is simple in sensation but not in reality, like ra'. His saying: 'and the short vowel is temporal' it has passed that the meaning of a short vowel of a letter is that it is such that a specific long vowel from among the long vowels can exist after it, so the temporality of the short vowel is disputed and requires a proof. His saying: 'and it may be said: it is possible etc.' it may be answered by claiming necessary inner experience that the consonant is not after the short vowel. His saying: 'and it was objected etc.' it may be answered: That since the falsity of the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is known by necessary inner experience, and it is assumed that the consonant is instantaneous and the short vowel is temporal, and the instantaneous cannot exist with the temporal in temporal simultaneousness, their existence in that simultaneousness is impossible. His saying: 'and by this it is also known the falsity etc.' The aspect of falsity is that since the priority of the consonant over the short vowel is established, even if their simultaneity is allowed, it may be of the type of mutual condition without the necessity of the priority of the condition over the conditioned. Also, since the priority of the short vowel over the consonant is allowed, his statement about the subsequent sound needing that priority is not valid, for the posteriority of the sound is based on circularity. For if it were prior, its dependence on the consonant in the sense of needing it that demands its priority would not be necessary. Rather, what follows from it is that the consonant does not occur except with the sound, and there is no impossibility in that. Commentary

Why is it impossible to begin with a quiescent consonant?
A) Because quiescent consonants do not exist in Arabic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Nine Tastes: Classification and Mnemonic
Classification of tastes into nine types with a Persian mnemonic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how tastes are classified into nine types based on the combination of agent and receiver, and the Persian mnemonic that summarizes them.

Core Idea
Tastes are systematically derived from agent and receiver categories.

This passage introduces a systematic classification of tastes based on the qualities of the agent (hot/cold/moderate) and the receiver (subtle/dense/moderate). By multiplying these, nine fundamental tastes emerge. A Persian mnemonic helps memorize them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification principle
The author states that tastes are derived by multiplying three agent types (hot, cold, moderate) with three receiver types (subtle, dense, moderate), resulting in nine tastes.
This is a systematic a priori approach.
2
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text and its literal translation carefully to grasp the terminology.
3
Examine the Persian mnemonic
The Persian couplets list the nine tastes with their corresponding agent and receiver. Notice the poetic structure and the 'arsh wa nashr' (enallage) style.
4
Identify the nine tastes
From the mnemonic, try to list the nine tastes: pungent, bitter, sharp, salt-inducing, sour, constrictive, astringent, greasy, sweet, and insipid? (The mnemonic is cryptic; the commentary explains that nine are intended.)
The exact correspondence may require interpretation.
5
Reflect on the system
Consider how this classification aims to cover all possible simple tastes by combining basic qualities.

النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : المذوقات وأصولها
إما حار أو بارد أو معتدل والقابل إما لطيف أو كثيف أو معتدل ) . وإذا ضرب أقسام الفاعل في أقسام القابل حصل أقسام تسعة فتنقسم الطعوم بحسبها أيضاً. واعترض عليه بأنَّ انحصار الفاعل في الحرارة والبرودة والكيفية المتوسطة بينهما ممنوع، وأيضاً المراتب المتوسطة بين غايتي الحرارة والبرودة وكذا بين غايتي اللطافة والكثافة غير محصورة فجاز أن تكون كل واحدة من تلك المراتب فاعلة أو قابلة لطعم بسيط على حدة، فلا ينحصر عدد الطعوم البسيطة في عدة محصورة فضلا عن التسعة والعشر، وأيضاً الخيار والقرع والحنطة النية يحس من كل منها بطعم لا تركيب فيه وليس من التسعة المذكورة وأيضاً الاختلاف بالشدة والضعف إن اقتضى الاختلاف النوعي فأنواع الطعوم غير منحصرة، وإن لم يقتض كان القبض والعفوصة نوعاً واحداً. إذ لا اختلاف بينهما إلا بالشدة والضعف فإنَّ القابض كما سيأتي يقبض ظاهر اللسان وحدة والعفص يقبض ظاهره وباطنه معاً. وأيضاً حدوث الطعوم التسعة على تلك الوجوه المخصوصة لم يقم عليها برهان ولا أمارة تفيد غلبة الظن، ولهذا قيل : مباحث الطعوم دعاوى خالية عن الدلائل، إلا أنَّ المصنف ذكر في كيفية الحدوث مناسبات،
قوله : ( حصل أقسام تسعة فينقسم طعوم بحسبها ولقد جمعها بعض الفضلاء في ثلاث أبيات فارسية مع الإشارة إلى قابل كل منها وفاعله على اللف والنشر المرتب حيث قال : تیز تلخ است وتيك شور انكيز در لطيف وكثيف وأوسط حار آورد ترشي وعفوصت وقبض کر برودت بدان سه کرددیار دسم وحلو وتفه شود آري معتدل را بدان سه باشد کار
قوله : (غير محصورة) قال الله : رحمه : عدم الانحصار أن اعتبر غاية الحرارة غير متناهية بأن يكون كل حرارة تعرض في الشدة بتصور أخرى فوقها وكذا البرودة حقيقي وإلا فهو مبالغة في الكثرة . وسيجيء تحقيقه.
قوله : ( وأيضاً الخيار والقرع والحنطة النية قد يجاب عنه : بأنَّ طعم هذه الأمور راجع إلى أحد الأنواع التسعة لكن انضم فيها إلى الكيفية الذوقية كيفية لمسية لا يميز الحس بينهما فيتخيل أن طعمها مغاير للأنواع التسعة، وليس كذلك في الحقيقة
The fourth type - the first aim: Tastes and their principles. Either hot or cold or moderate, and the receiver either subtle or dense or moderate. And if the divisions of the agent are multiplied by the divisions of the receiver, nine divisions result, and tastes are divided accordingly as well. And it was objected to him that the confinement of the agent to heat, cold, and the intermediate quality between them is denied; also the intermediate ranks between the extremes of heat and cold, and likewise between the extremes of subtlety and density, are not limited, so it is possible that each one of those ranks is an agent or a receiver for a simple taste individually; thus the number of simple tastes is not confined to a limited number, let alone nine or ten. Also cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat: from each of them a taste is sensed that has no composition in it, and it is not among the nine mentioned. Also difference by intensity and weakness: if it necessitates specific difference, then the kinds of tastes are unlimited; and if it does not necessitate, then astringency (qabḍ) and constriction ('ufūṣa) would be one type, for there is no difference between them except by intensity and weakness, for the astringent, as will come, contracts the surface of the tongue alone, and the constrictive contracts its surface and interior together. Also the occurrence of the nine tastes in those specific ways has no proof established for it, nor any indication that gives prevalence of opinion; hence it was said: the investigations of tastes are claims devoid of evidence. Except that the author mentioned, concerning the manner of occurrence, appropriate connections. His statement: (Nine divisions result, so tastes are divided accordingly) and indeed some virtuous persons compiled them in three Persian couplets indicating the receiver and agent of each, in organized order, saying: Pungent is bitter and sharp is salt-inducing in the subtle and dense and moderate—heat brought sourness, constriction, and astringency—cold made those three appear—greasy, sweet, and insipid become—yes, moderate, by those three, is occupied. His statement: (not limited) — God have mercy on him — the non-limitation is if the extreme of heat is considered infinite, such that every heat that occurs in intensity is conceived as having another above it, and similarly cold is real; otherwise it is hyperbole in abundance. His statement: (Also cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat) — it may be answered about it: that the taste of these things returns to one of the nine types, but a tactile quality joins the taste quality in them, and the sense does not distinguish between them, so it is imagined that their taste is different from the nine types, but in reality it is not so.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of tastes, a series of objections, and a specific answer. It begins by stating the principles: the agent (hot, cold, moderate) and the receiver (subtle, dense, moderate) combine to yield nine types of tastes. However, multiple objections are then raised: the agent is not limited to three; the gradations are infinite; cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat appear to have tastes not from the nine; differences in intensity could make tastes infinite or collapse them; and there is no proof for the specific occurrence of the nine. The author then provides a Persian mnemonic summarizing the nine types. A marginal note explains that 'unlimited' might be hyperbolic about the extremes. The key answer given is regarding cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat: their taste actually belongs to one of the nine, but a tactile quality is mixed in, so the sense perceives a composite, leading to the illusion of a new taste.

Objections and the Answer about Cucumber, Gourd, and Raw Wheat
Objections to the nine-taste classification and the answer regarding cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections raised against the nine-taste classification and the resolution for the apparent counterexamples of cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat.

Core Idea
Apparent counterexamples to the nine tastes are actually composites of taste and touch.

After presenting the nine-taste classification, the text addresses several objections. One key objection is that cucumber, gourd, and raw wheat seem to have tastes outside the nine. The answer provided explains that their taste is actually from one of the nine, but it is mixed with a tactile quality that creates the illusion of a new taste.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection section
The objector raises multiple points: the agent is not limited to three, infinite gradations, the three food examples, intensity differences, and lack of proof.
2
Focus on the cucumber/gourd/wheat objection
This is a concrete counterexample: these items have a taste not found in the nine. Read the objection carefully.
3
Study the author's answer
The answer asserts that their taste is from one of the nine, but a tactile quality (kayfiyya lamsiyya) mixes in, so the sense perceives a composite, mistaking it for a new taste.
This preserves the classification.
4
Consider other objections
The other objections (e.g., infinite gradations, lack of proof) are noted but not fully answered here; the author acknowledges that the classification is based on suitable connections, not strict proof.
5
Reflect on the defense strategy
The answer uses the idea of sensory illusion due to composite qualities, a common approach in Islamic philosophy to defend categorizations.

How Heat Produces Bitterness, Pungency, and Saltiness
The author explains how heat generates different tastes depending on the density of the receptive body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heat generates different tastes based on the density of the receptive body, as explained in the commentary.

Core Idea
The density of a substance determines whether heat makes it bitter, pungent, or salty.

This passage from a classical theological commentary explains a fascinating natural phenomenon: heat interacts differently with materials of different densities, resulting in distinct tastes. The author uses this to illustrate how secondary causes produce effects according to the disposition of the receiver. Read on to see how bitterness, pungency, and saltiness arise from a single cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start with the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the full flavor of the argument.
2
Identify the three tastes
The passage names three tastes produced by heat in different receptacles: bitterness, pungency, and saltiness. Notice the conditions that lead to each.
3
Understand the role of density
The key factor is the density of the receiver: dense (kathīf), subtle (laṭīf), moderate (muʿtadil). Each affects how heat acts and what quality results.
4
Trace the causal reasoning
Heat causes separation. In dense bodies, separation is great due to resistance, leading to bitterness. In subtle, small but penetrating separation yields pungency. Moderate gives intermediate saltiness.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
This example shows how a single cause (heat) produces diverse effects based on the recipient. This is a key idea in kalam and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أنَّ النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : المذوقات وأصولها ربما أوقعت لبعض النفوس ظناً بتلك الوجوه فقال : ( فالحار) أي الحرارة كما هو المشهور في الكتب، أو الأمر الحار كما يتبادر من العبارة. فإنَّ الفاعل هو الصورة النوعية بحسب كيفياتها التي هي آلاتها في أفاعيلها ) يفعل كيفية غير ملائمة ) للأجسام التي ندركها ( إذ من شأنه التفريق ) لما عرفت من أن الحرارة تحدث تفريقاً، ولا شك أنَّ التفريق حالة غير ملائمة للأجسام، فلذلك كانت الكيفية الحادثة من تأثير الحرارة غير ملائمة على حسب التفريق الحاصل من تأثيرها، كما أشار إليه بقوله : ( ففي الكثيف ) أي فيفعل الحار في القابل الكثيف كيفية غير ملائمة (في الغاية وهي المرارة فإنها أبغض الطعوم وأبعدها عن الملاءمة، ولو فرض ملاءمتها البعض الأجسام كان ذلك لبعده عن الاعتدال ( لشدة المقاومة وكون التفريق عظيماً) القابل إذا كان كثيفاً قاوم الحرارة مقاومة شديدة ومنعها عن النفوذ فيه فيجتمع حينئذ أجزاء الحرارة وتتفرق تفريقاً عظيماً لأنّ الحرارة المجتمعة أشد تأثيراً فيكون أثرها أقوى، فلا جرم تكون الكيفية الحادثة حينئذ في غاية البعد عن الملازمة ( و ) يفعل الحار ( في ) القابل ( اللطيف) كيفية غير ملائمة أيضاً إلا أنها تكون في عدم الملاءمة ( دونه ) أي دون ما ذكر أولاً . ( وهي ( أي تلك الكيفية الحادثة اللطيف ( الحرافة إذ تتفرق تفريقاً صغيراً لكنه يكون غائصاً يعني أنَّ القابل إذا كان لطيفاً لم يقاوم الفاعل الجار ولم يمنعه من النفوذ فيه فيغوص في أجزائه فيضعف التأثير لعدم اجتماع الحرارة ويكون التفريق صغيراً. فلا بد أن تكون الكيفية الحادثة فيه حينئذ غير ملاءمة، وأن تكون دون المرارة في عدم الملاءمة (و) يفعل الحار في القابل المعتدل ملوحة وهي بينهما ) أي بين المرارة والحرافة في عدم الملاءمة لأن مقاومة المعتدل للحرارة أقل من مقاومة الكثيف وأكثر من مقاومة اللطيف فيكون التفريق. قوله : (أي الحرارة إما على حذف المضاف أو إطلاق الحار على الحرارة لأنها حارة نفسها كما مر . نظائره في بحث الوجود، أو يكون من قبيل إطلاق اسم الفاعل على بحرارة هي المصدر.
Meaning that: the fourth type - the first aim: Tastes and their principles perhaps placed in some souls an opinion about those ways, so he said: 'Thus the hot (al-ḥār)' i.e., heat as is well-known in the books, or the hot thing as appears from the expression. For the agent is the specific form according to its qualities which are its instruments in its acts; it produces an unpleasing quality for the bodies we perceive, since its property is separation, because you know that heat produces separation, and there is no doubt that separation is an unpleasing state for bodies. Therefore the quality produced by the effect of heat is unpleasing according to the separation resulting from its effect, as he indicated by his statement: 'Thus in the dense (al-kathīf)', i.e., the hot acts upon the dense receiver producing an unpleasing quality (to the utmost degree), which is bitterness (al-marāra), for it is the most hated of tastes and the farthest from pleasantness; and if its pleasantness for some bodies is supposed, that would be due to its distance from moderation, because of the intensity of resistance and the fact that the separation is great. When the receiver is dense, it resists heat strongly and prevents it from penetrating it, so then the parts of heat gather and separate enormously, because gathered heat is more effective, so its effect is stronger; thus necessarily the quality produced then is at the utmost distance from pleasantness. And the hot acts upon the subtle (al-laṭīf) receiver an unpleasing quality also, but it is in unpleasantness (below that) i.e., below what was first mentioned, and it is (that quality produced in the subtle) pungency (al-ḥarāfa), because it separates a small separation, but it is deep-penetrating. That is, when the receiver is subtle, it does not resist the effective agent and does not prevent it from penetrating it, so it sinks into its parts, and the effect weakens due to lack of accumulation of heat, and the separation is small. So necessarily the quality produced then is unpleasing, and is below bitterness in unpleasantness. And the hot acts upon the moderate (al-muʿtadil) receiver, producing saltiness (mulūḥa), which is between them, i.e., between bitterness and pungency in unpleasantness, because the resistance of the moderate to heat is less than the resistance of the dense and more than the resistance of the subtle, so the separation is... His statement: (i.e., heat either as a deletion of the genitive construction or as a denominating the hot to heat because it is itself hot, as similar examples passed in the discussion of existence, or it is of the type of denominating the active participle to heat which is the source.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage explains in detail how heat produces different tastes in different receptacles. It begins by clarifying that 'hot' (al-ḥār) means heat itself or the hot thing. The agent (heat) causes separation (tafrīq), which is unpleasing. In a dense (kathīf) receiver, resistance is strong, so heat gathers and causes great separation, leading to bitterness (marāra), the most unpleasant taste. In a subtle (laṭīf) receiver, heat penetrates easily and cannot accumulate, causing a small separation that is deep, leading to pungency (ḥarāfa), which is less unpleasant. In a moderate (muʿtadil) receiver, the resistance is intermediate, so the effect is moderate, yielding saltiness (mulūḥa), which is between bitterness and pungency in unpleasantness. A marginal note explains the terminology: 'al-ḥār' might be a deletion of the genitive or a metonymy for heat itself.

مرارة
حرافة
ملوحة
كثيف
لطيف
معتدل
تفريق
ملاءمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The taste produced by heat depends only on the type of heat.
Correction: The passage explains that the density of the receiver is essential: bitterness arises in dense bodies, pungency in subtle, and saltiness in moderate bodies.

Checkpoint
What taste does heat produce in a dense (kathīf) receptacle?
A) Saltiness
B) Pungency
C) Bitterness
D) Sweetness

Key Takeaway
The same cause, heat, produces different tastes—bitterness, pungency, saltiness—depending on whether the receiving body is dense, subtle, or moderate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that the same heat produces vastly different tastes depending on the receiver's density. Reflect on a trial or hardship you faced recently. Did you respond with dense resistance (making it bitter), subtle avoidance (pungent but shallow), or moderate acceptance (salty but bearable)? Which response helped you grow, and what would you change next time? Commit to one specific way you will adjust your receptivity to future difficulties.
?
Bitterness is described as the most hated taste because it arises from intense resistance and separation. Think of a relationship or situation where your own stubborn resistance caused lasting bitterness. How could you have lowered your defenses to receive the 'heat' differently, turning a potential bitterness into something more manageable? Write a concrete step you will take this week to practice softness in a difficult interaction.
?
Saltiness is the product of a moderate receiver—neither too dense nor too subtle. In what area of your life are you either too resistant (dense) or too passive (subtle) regarding an uncomfortable truth? What would it look like to adopt a moderate stance, allowing the 'heat' of reality to produce a balanced response? Name one change you will make today to move toward that moderation.

Objection to the Claim of Stronger Effect from Gathered Heat
Objection about gathered vs. dispersed heat and receiver density.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the claim that accumulated heat has a stronger effect, considering the density of the receiver.

Core Idea
Density of the receiver can reduce the effect of even intense heat.

In this passage, a commentator raises an objection to the idea that gathered heat always produces a stronger effect. The objection points out that while gathered heat is more intense, the density of the receiving body can impede some of that effect. This introduces an important nuance in the discussion of heat's action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two types of heat (gathered vs. dispersed) and two types of receiver (dense vs. subtle).
The objection is structured as a comparative analysis.
2
Understand the reasoning
Gathered heat is more intense but dense receiver hinders; dispersed heat is less intense but subtle receiver does not hinder as much. The net effect is questionable.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The objection makes the claim of stronger effect doubtful (محل نظر).
This is a classic kalam objection format.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider why this matters for arguments about heat's effect and the interaction between agent and recipient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيكون أثرها أقوى قيل عليه الحرارة المجتمعة وإن كانت أشد تأثيراً إلا أن كثافة القابل يمنع بعض التأثير والحرارة الغير المجتمعة الأجزاء وإن كان تأثيرها أقل من تأثير المجتمعة . لكن لطافة القابل لا يمنع التأثير مثل منعه فكون الأثر في الأول أقوى محل نظر . قوله : ( لكنه يكون غائصاً الأظهر أن يقال : لكونه غائصاً لأنه دليل كون التفريق صغيراً كما يفهم من سياق كلام الشارح أيضاً وليس هذا محل الاستدراك كما لا يخفى على الذائق.
His statement: (Thus its effect is stronger) — it was said against it: The gathered heat, even if it is more effective, yet the density of the receiver prevents some of the effect, and the heat that is not gathered in parts, even if its effect is less than the effect of the gathered heat, however the subtlety of the receiver does not prevent the effect as much as the prevention by density; so the effect in the first case being stronger is subject to consideration. His statement: (But it is deep-penetrating) — the more apparent is to say: because it is deep as a proof that the separation is small, as understood from the context of the commentator's words also, and this is not a place for correction, as is not hidden from the one who tastes.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal glosses. The first addresses the claim that accumulated heat produces a stronger effect. An objector argues that while gathered heat is indeed more intense, the density of the receiver reduces some of that effect; conversely, while dispersed heat is less intense, the subtlety of the receiver does not impede it as much. Therefore, it is questionable whether the effect is truly stronger in the dense case. The second gloss clarifies the phrase 'deep-penetrating' (ghā'iṣ): it is better to say that the depth itself is evidence of the smallness of the separation, as understood from the commentator's context, and this is not a place for correction.

Key Terms
أثر
قوي
كثافة
لطافة
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accumulated heat always yields a stronger effect regardless of the receiver.
Correction: The objection shows that density of the receiver can counteract the intensity, making the effect not necessarily stronger.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why might the effect of gathered heat not be as strong as claimed?
A) The heat dissipates quickly.
B) The density of the receiver prevents some effect.
C) The heat becomes less intense over time.
D) The subtlety of the receiver blocks the effect.

Key Takeaway
The strength of heat's effect depends not only on its intensity but also on the density or subtlety of the receiver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Cause of Saltiness
Defines saltiness as intermediate and explains its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how saltiness is defined as an intermediate quality and how it is generated from a balanced mixture of bitter and pungent elements.

Core Idea
Saltiness is not a simple taste but a balance between bitterness and pungency.

This passage examines the nature of saltiness, showing that it is not a fundamental taste but an intermediate one. It explains why salty things can sometimes taste bitter and sometimes pungent, and it provides both a rational verification and a practical cause for this phenomenon. As you read, notice how the author uses both empirical example and theoretical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of saltiness
Read the Arabic and English to see how saltiness is defined as intermediate between bitterness and pungency.
Pay attention to the words 'تميل' and 'مرة'.
2
Identify the verification method
The author gives a practical experiment: taking bitter ashes, mixing with water, and cooking to produce saltiness. Consider why this demonstrates intermediate nature.
This is a 'تحقيق'.
3
Understand the causal theory
The passage states that saltiness arises from a balanced mixture (مخالطة باعتدال) of tasteless water and dry, burnt earthy parts. Note how imbalance leads to bitterness.
4
Consider practical implications
The passage mentions making salt from ashes, alkali, etc. Relate this to the causal theory.
This connects theory to everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولذلك ) أي ولأن الملوحة كيفية متوسطة بين كيفيتي المرارة والحرافة ( تميل) الملوحة ( إلى المرارة مرة وإلى الحرافة (أخرى أي يكون طعم المالح تارة قريباً من المرارة بحيث يتوهم أنه مر وتارة قريباً من الحرافة بحيث يتخيل أنه حريف وتحقيقه) أي تحقيق كون الملوحة متوسطة بينهما ( أنه إذا أخذ لطيف الرماد المر وخلط بالماء وطبخ حصلت الملوحة ) . وهذا ما قيل من أنّ سبب حدوث الملوحة مخالطة رطوبة مائية قليلة الطعم أو عديمته بأجزاء أرضية محترقة يابسة المزاج مرة الطعم مخالطة باعتدال فإنَّ الأجزاء الأرضية إذا كثرت أمرت ومن هذا السبب تتولد الأملاح وتصير المياه ملحاً وقد يصنع الملح من الرماد والقلي والنورة وغير ذلك بأن يطبخ في الماء ويصفى ويغلى ذلك الماء حتى ينعقد ملحا أو يترك حتى ينعقد بنفسه
(Therefore) and because saltiness is a quality intermediate between the two qualities of bitterness and pungency, (saltiness inclines) to bitterness sometimes and to pungency (another) i.e., the taste of salty is sometimes close to bitterness so that it is imagined to be bitter, and sometimes close to pungency so that it is imagined to be pungent. And its verification i.e., the verification that saltiness is intermediate between them (is that if the fine part of bitter ashes is taken and mixed with water and cooked, saltiness results). And this is what is said: that the cause of the occurrence of saltiness is the mixing of a watery moisture of little or no taste with earthy burnt parts of dry temperament and bitter taste, in a balanced mixture; for if the earthy parts increase, they become bitter. From this cause salts are generated and waters become salt. And salt can be made from ashes, alkali, lime, and other things by cooking them in water, filtering, and boiling that water until it solidifies into salt, or leaving it until it solidifies by itself.

Commentary
This passage defines saltiness and explains its cause. It begins by stating that saltiness is a quality (kayfiyya) intermediate between bitterness (al-marāra) and pungency (al-ḥarāfa). The author supports this by noting that the salty taste sometimes resembles bitterness and sometimes pungency, so it lies between them. The verification of this intermediate nature is given: taking the fine part of bitter ashes, mixing with water, and cooking produces saltiness. Then the cause of saltiness is explained: a balanced mixture (mukhālaṭa bi-‘tiddāl) of a watery moisture of little or no taste with burnt earthy parts that are dry and bitter. If the earthy parts dominate, bitterness results. From this process salts are generated and waters become salty. The passage also describes practical salt-making from ashes, alkali, and lime by cooking and evaporation.

Objection: Sourness Caused by Heat? The Real Agent is Cold
Objection and answer on the cause of sourness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that weak heat causes sourness is resolved by affirming that non-intense cold is the true agent.

Core Idea
Heat only enables cold to act; it does not directly produce sourness.

This passage presents a sharp objection and its answer. Someone argues that sourness in juice or milk is clearly caused by weak heat, not cold. The response reinterprets the observation: heat merely breaks the intensity of cold, allowing a mild cold to produce sourness. Understanding this requires distinguishing the ultimate cause from the immediate trigger.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that sourness in juice and milk is produced by weak heat, not by cold. Consider why this seems plausible from everyday experience.
The objection is based on direct observation: heat is applied, sourness results.
2
Study the answer's logic
The answer denies that heat is the agent. Instead, it explains that intense cold is broken by weak heat, resulting in non-intense cold which then causes sourness.
Notice the shift: heat is only a catalyst, not the efficient cause.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
The answer preserves the principle that cold produces sourness while explaining away the apparent counterexample. Heat merely modifies the intensity of cold.
This shows how apparent counterexamples can be explained without rejecting the original doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفي القابل اللطيف حموضة اعترض عليه بعض الأفاضل بأن العصير وكذا اللبن ربما يتحمض بالحرارة الضعيفة دون البرودة. وأجيب : بأنَّ فاعل الحموضة برودة غير شديدة فإن شديد البرودة يكسر بالحرارة الضعيفة شدتها فيفعل البرودة الغير الشديدة حموضة

And it was answered: that the agent of sourness is a non-intense cold; for intense cold is broken by weak heat of intensity, so the non-intense cold produces sourness.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer from the commentary. The objector argues that sourness in juice and milk can be produced by weak heat, not by cold. The answer clarifies that the actual agent of sourness is a mild cold (burūda ghayr shadīda). When intense cold is present, weak heat can break its intensity, resulting in a non-intense cold that then causes sourness. Thus, the ultimate cause remains cold, not heat.

Cold's Tastes: Astringency, Sourness, and Constriction
Classification of cold's tastes in three substrates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cold produces different tastes (astringency, sourness, constriction) depending on the density or subtlety of the substrate.

Core Idea
Cold's tastes vary by substrate: dense gives astringency, subtle gives sourness, moderate gives constriction.

This passage classifies the effects of cold on different substrates, explaining how the same quality (cold) yields distinct tastes due to variations in condensation and resistance. The author details three cases: dense, subtle, and moderate substrates, each producing a characteristic taste with a specific degree of aversion. Understanding this classification helps explain natural phenomena like fruit ripening.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the three substrates and the tastes they produce.
2
Identify the three substrates and tastes
The passage discusses dense (kathīf), subtle (laṭīf), and moderate (muʿtadil) substrates. List the taste each produces: astringency, sourness, constriction.
Astringency approaches bitterness in aversion.
3
Understand the mechanism
Cold causes condensation. In dense substrates, resistance doubles condensation, causing strong astringency. In subtle, condensation is weaker, yielding sourness. Moderate gives intermediate constriction.
The fruit example shows how astringent fruit becomes sour as it ripens (becomes more watery).
4
Analyze the aversion hierarchy
The passage ranks the tastes by aversion: astringency is most aversive, then constriction, then sourness. Notice why the author says astringent fruit grips both inside and outside of the tongue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبارد يفعل كالحار كيفية غير ملائمة إذ من شأنه التكثيف الذي لا يلائم الأجسام أيضاً . لكن عدم ملاءمته أقل من عدم ملاءمة التفريق ولذلك كانت الكيفيات الحادثة بواسطة التفريق أشد في المنافرة من الكيفيات الحادثة بتوسط التكثيف . ثم إنَّ هذه الكيفيات أيضاً مختلفة في عدم الملاءمة على حسب مراتب التكثيف في القوة والضعف، وإليه الإشارة بقوله : ( ففي الكثيف) أي فيفعل البارد في القابل الكثيف عفوصة لأنه يتضاعف التكثيف يعني أن الكثيف يمنع البرودة عن النفوذ ويقاومها فيجتمع حينئذ أجزاء البرودة، ويؤثر فيه تأثيراً عظيماً، ويكثفه تكثيفاً بليغاً متضاعفاً، فيحدث فيه العفوصة التي تقرب من المرارة في المنافرة، (و) يفعل البارد (في) القابل اللطيف حموضة لأنَّ اللطيف لا يقاوم البرودة فينفذ في أعماقه ويكثفه تكثيفاً أقل بكثير مما في القابل الكثيف، فيحدث فيه كيفية يكون عدم ملاءمتها أقل من عدم ملاءمة العفوصة بكثير أيضاً ، وهي الحموضة وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار بقوله: ) لأنه ( أي الفاعل البارد ( يكثف ( القابل اللطيف ( يبرده ويغوص ) فيه ( بلطافته ) أي لطافته فيضعف فيه تأثيره ) فيكون عدم ملاءمته ) أي عدم ملاءمة الطعم الحادث في ذلك القابل اللطيف (بين بين . ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ الصواب تبديلهما بأقل كما أشرنا إليه (ولذلك ) أي : ولأن الحموضة تحدث من فعل البارد في اللطيف ( كان الثمر (العفص لشدة برده وكثافته ) كلما ازداد مائية ) ولطافة واعتدل قليلا بإسخان الشمس المنضج ) ازداد حموضة و يفعل البارد (في) القابل ( المعتدل قبضاً وهو في عدم الملاءمة ( دون العفوصة) وفوق الحموضة لأنّ تكثيف البرودة في المعتدل أقل من تكثيفها في الكثيف، وأكثر من تكثيفها في اللطيف على قياس ما مر فيحدث فيه كيفية عدم ملاءمتها بين بين وهو القبض وكونه في عدم الملاءمة فوق الحموضة ظاهر، وأما كونه في ذلك دون العفوصة فإليه أشار بقوله : ( إذ العفص يقبض باطن اللسان وظاهره )
And the cold acts like the hot in producing a non-pleasant quality, since its property is condensation which is also not compatible with bodies. But its incompatibility is less than the incompatibility of separation. Therefore the qualities produced by means of separation are more intense in aversion than qualities produced by means of condensation. Then these qualities also differ in incompatibility according to the degrees of condensation in strength and weakness, and to this he pointed by his saying: 'So in the dense' i.e., the cold acts in the dense receptive subject producing astringency because condensation is doubled, meaning that the dense prevents the cold from penetrating and resists it, so then the parts of cold gather, affecting it greatly, and condenses it with a strong doubled condensation, thus producing astringency which approaches bitterness in aversion. And the cold acts in the subtle receptive subject producing sourness because the subtle does not resist the cold, so it penetrates into its depths and condenses it with a condensation much less than in the dense receptive subject, thus producing a quality whose incompatibility is much less than the incompatibility of astringency, which is sourness. And to what we mentioned he pointed by his saying: 'Because' i.e., the cold agent 'condenses' the subtle receptive subject 'cools it and penetrates' into it 'by its subtlety' i.e., its subtlety, so its effect weakens in it, so its incompatibility i.e., the incompatibility of the taste occurring in that subtle receptive subject 'is intermediate'. And it is not hidden from you that the correct thing is to replace them with 'less' as we indicated. 'Therefore' i.e., because sourness occurs from the action of cold in the subtle, 'the astringent fruit' due to its intense cold and density, 'every time it increases in wateriness' and subtlety and becomes moderate a little by the heating of the ripening sun, 'it increases in sourness'. And the cold acts in the moderate receptive subject producing constriction, and it is in incompatibility below astringency and above sourness because the condensation of cold in the moderate is less than its condensation in the dense and greater than its condensation in the subtle, in analogy to what passed, so a quality arises whose incompatibility is intermediate, which is constriction. And its being above sourness in incompatibility is apparent, and as for its being below astringency in that, he pointed to it by his saying: 'Since the astringent (fruit) grips the inside and outside of the tongue' together, so nature strongly recoils from it, 'and the constrictor grips only its outside, so the recoil is not at that extreme.'
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage classifies the effects of cold in producing tastes. It first states that cold, like heat, produces a non-pleasant quality (kayfiyya ghayr mulāʾima) because its property is condensation (takhthīf), which is also incompatible with bodies. However, cold's incompatibility is less than that caused by separation (tafrīq). Therefore, tastes produced by separation are more aversive than those from condensation. Then it explains how the degree of aversion varies according to the intensity of condensation. The author then details three cases using the commentator's phrasing: (1) In a dense substrate (al-kathīf), cold produces astringency (ʿufūṣa) because the substrate resists cold, causing a doubling of condensation, resulting in astringency close to bitterness. (2) In a subtle substrate (al-laṭīf), cold produces sourness (ḥumūḍa) because the subtle does not resist, so condensation is less, resulting in a less aversive taste. (3) In a moderate substrate (al-muʿtadil), cold produces constriction (qabḍ), which is intermediate in aversion. The fruit example: astringent fruit (al-ʿafṣ) becomes more sour as it becomes more watery and moderate from sun ripening. The passage concludes by clarifying that astringent fruit grips both inside and outside of the tongue, causing strong recoil, while constrictor fruit grips only the outside, so the recoil is less extreme.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sourness is caused by heat or by a different quality.
Correction: The passage explains that sourness is caused by cold acting on a subtle substrate, producing weaker condensation.

Checkpoint
What taste does cold produce in a moderate substrate (al-muʿtadil)?
A) Sourness
B) Astringency
C) Constriction
D) Bitterness

Key Takeaway
Cold produces astringency in dense substrates, sourness in subtle substrates, and constriction in moderate substrates, with aversion levels corresponding to the intensity of condensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as a dense fruit resists cold and becomes astringent, what areas of your heart have become dense and resistant to divine instruction, causing bitterness and aversion? Will you ask Allah to soften your heart so that you receive guidance with sweetness, like the fruit that becomes sour when ripened?
?
The astringent taste grips both inside and outside of the tongue, creating a strong recoil. Is there a sin or habit in your life that has a double grip on you — affecting both your inner thoughts and outer actions? How can you apply the lesson's principle that different substrates respond differently, and ask Allah to change your 'substrate' to one that accepts correction more gently?
?
Cold's condensation is less aversive than heat's separation. Where in your life have you experienced a 'cold' trial (a difficulty that condensed your faith or patience) that was actually easier to bear than a 'hot' one? How does this reflect Allah's mercy in choosing the trial that suits your heart's density? Will you thank Him for the trials that are bearable, and ask for strength for those that are not?

Moderate Temperature and Pleasant Taste
States that moderate temperature produces a pleasant taste by avoiding extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why moderate temperature produces a pleasant taste and why it is not simply due to weak heat.

Core Idea
Pleasant taste arises from balance, not from weak extremes.

In this passage, the author explains the effect of the moderate temperature (al-muʿtadil) that lies between hot and cold. He clarifies that its pleasant result comes from avoiding both excessive separation and excessive condensation, acting in a balanced manner. This corrects a common misconception that such a taste is merely from weak heat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's claim and reasoning sink in.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the negative statements (does not separate strongly, does not condense strongly).
2
Identify the two extremes avoided
The moderate avoids strong separation (tafrīq shadeed) and strong condensation (takhthīf qawī). Contrast with intense heat and cold.
These are the actions of extreme temperatures.
3
Understand the balanced action
Instead of extremes, the moderate does a 'between between' action (fiʿl bayn bayn). This balanced action produces the pleasant taste.
4
Note the correction of misconception
Some think the pleasant taste comes from weak heat, but the author says it is from the moderate itself. Recognize the distinction.
Weak heat would still be a form of heat, not balance.
5
Reflect on the principle of balance
Consider why balance (iʿtidāl) is often associated with pleasantness in many contexts. This passage illustrates a broader philosophical idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتدل الذي هو بين الحار والبارد يفعل فعلاً ملائماً وذلك لأنه لا يفرق تفريقاً شديداً، ولا يكثف أيضاً تكثيفاً قوياً، بل يفعل فعلا بين بين فيحدث منه طعم ملائم (وهو) أي ما يحدث من فعله (في) ويظن أنها من فعل الحرارة الضعيفة وليس كذلك
And the moderate, which is between hot and cold, produces a pleasant action, because it does not separate with a strong separation, nor does it condense with a strong condensation, but it does an action in-between, so a pleasant taste arises from it. (It is) i.e., what arises from its action (in) and it is thought that it is from the action of weak heat, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of the moderate temperature (al-muʿtadil) between hot and cold. It states that the moderate produces a pleasant (mulāʾim) action and taste. The reason is that it does not cause strong separation (tafrīq) like intense heat, nor strong condensation (takhthīf) like intense cold. Instead, it acts in a balanced way (fiʿl bayn bayn). The resulting taste is therefore agreeable. The passage also notes a common misconception: some think this pleasant taste comes from weak heat, but the author corrects this—it is from the moderate, not from weak heat.

?
This lesson shows that pleasant outcomes come from balanced actions, not from extremes or from mere weakness. Examine your worship: are you following the balanced sunnah of the Prophet (sallAllahu alayhi wa sallam) or falling into excess or neglect? Commit to one small adjustment this week that brings you closer to the prophetic balance.
